Show thinking, not just final accuracy numbers. [[Home]] <<include "Footer">> Dysgraphia is a neurological disorder characterized by writing difficulties. It generally manifests as a problem with handwriting, spelling, and organizing thoughts on paper. It's not necessarily a reflection of a person's intelligence, but it can significantly impact learning and performance in academic or professional settings. Symptoms may include inconsistent letter formation, poor spatial planning on paper, difficulty spelling, or slow writing speed. It's often found in people with other learning disabilities such as dyslexia, and its exact cause isn't fully understood. Treatments mainly involve occupational therapy and teaching strategies to cope with the condition. <<include "Header">> ''SMU Data Science Capstone Projects'' Applied ML capstone projects completed through Southern Methodist University's data science programme. [[Home]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Overview This map is organized around how data grows in complexity — and how our tools grow with it. ''Start with a number'' [[Single Number]] → [[Short but skinny]] → Short and fat → [[Deep Learning|Really tall and skinny]] ''How we learn from data'' [[Statistics]] — foundations of uncertainty and estimation [[Statistical Machine Learning]] — connecting stats to algorithms [[Statsitical Learning Theory]] — the theory of generalization ''Core mathematical tools'' [[Core Linear Algebra]] | [[Branches of Mathmatics]] | [[Mathmatical Concepts]] ''The main paradigms'' [[learning Paradigms]] — supervised, unsupervised, self-supervised [[Reinforcement Learning]] — learning from interaction, not labeled data [[Lower Dimensional Representation]] — finding structure in high-dim data ''Navigating the map'' [[ML People]] | [[PodCasts]] | [[Institutes]] | [[Conferences]] --- //"The goal is to turn data into information, and information into insight."// — Carly Fiorina [[Home]] ← back <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> '' Stationary Time Series'' A time series $\{X_t\}$ is (weakly) stationary if its statistical properties do not change over time: $$ E[X_t] = \mu \quad \forall t $$ $$ \text{Cov}(X_t, X_{t+k}) = \gamma(k) \quad \text{depends only on lag } k $$ ''Why Stationarity Matters'' Most classical time series models (ARMA, AR, MA) assume stationarity. If the mean or variance trends over time, the model's parameter estimates are meaningless. ''Tests for Stationarity'' * ''Augmented Dickey-Fuller (ADF)'': tests for a unit root (non-stationarity). Reject $H_0$ → stationary. * ''KPSS'': tests stationarity directly. Reject $H_0$ → non-stationary. * ''Visual'': rolling mean and variance should be flat ''Making a Series Stationary'' * ''Differencing'': $X'_t = X_t - X_{t-1}$ removes trends * ''Seasonal differencing'': $X'_t = X_t - X_{t-s}$ * ''Log transform'': stabilises variance when series grows multiplicatively ''Strictly vs Weakly Stationary'' Strict stationarity requires the full joint distribution to be time-invariant. Weak (covariance) stationarity only requires constant mean and autocovariance — the standard assumption for linear models. <<button "Quiz: What does the ADF test's null hypothesis state?">> <<replace "#sts-q">> ''✓ The null hypothesis is that the series has a unit root (is non-stationary / has a stochastic trend). Rejecting $H_0$ supports stationarity.'' [[Non-Stationary Time Series]] | [[ARIMA]] | [[Auto-Correlation]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="sts-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Non-Stationary Time Series'' A series whose statistical properties change over time: drifting mean, changing variance, or seasonality. ''Types of Non-Stationarity'' * ''Trend-stationary'': has a deterministic trend $\mu(t) = \alpha + \beta t$. Fix by detrending. * ''Difference-stationary (unit root)'': $X_t = X_{t-1} + \varepsilon_t$ — a random walk. Fix by differencing. * ''Heteroscedastic'': variance changes over time. Fix by log transform or GARCH models. * ''Seasonal'': repeating patterns at fixed periods. Fix by seasonal differencing. ''Random Walk'' $$ X_t = X_{t-1} + \varepsilon_t, \quad \varepsilon_t \sim N(0, \sigma^2) $$ Variance grows without bound: $\text{Var}(X_t) = t\sigma^2$. Not stationary. Financial prices are often modelled as random walks. ''ARIMA Integration'' The ''I'' in ARIMA stands for integration order $d$ — how many times you must difference the series to make it stationary. ARIMA(p,1,q) differences once. ''Spurious Regression'' Regressing two independent non-stationary series on each other produces high $R^2$ and significant t-statistics by chance — a statistical artefact. <<button "Quiz: What does 'differencing' a time series do?">> <<replace "#nsts-q">> ''✓ It replaces each value with $X_t - X_{t-1}$, converting a random walk into stationary white noise and removing linear trends from the series.'' [[ Stationary Time Series]] | [[ARIMA]] | [[Auto-Correlation]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="nsts-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''The Gaussian (Normal) Distribution'' The most important distribution in all of statistics and ML. Its central role is not arbitrary — it arises from deep mathematical necessities and is the natural limit of many processes. --- ''Definition'' Univariate: $$ \mathcal{N}(x; \mu, \sigma^2) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi\sigma^2}} \exp\!\left(-\frac{(x-\mu)^2}{2\sigma^2}\right) $$ Parameters: mean $\mu \in \mathbb{R}$, variance $\sigma^2 > 0$. $\mathbb{E}[X] = \mu$, $\text{Var}(X) = \sigma^2$, mode = median = mean. ''Standard normal'': $Z \sim \mathcal{N}(0,1)$. Any $X \sim \mathcal{N}(\mu,\sigma^2)$ satisfies $Z = (X-\mu)/\sigma$. --- ''Why is the Gaussian Everywhere? — The Central Limit Theorem'' ''CLT'': Let $X_1, X_2, \ldots$ be i.i.d. with mean $\mu$ and finite variance $\sigma^2$. Then: $$ \sqrt{n}\left(\bar{X}_n - \mu\right) \xrightarrow{d} \mathcal{N}(0, \sigma^2) $$ Any quantity that is a sum (or average) of many independent contributions converges to Gaussian, regardless of the original distribution. This is why: * Measurement errors are Gaussian (sum of many small instrument fluctuations) * Heights, test scores are approximately Gaussian (sum of many genetic/environmental factors) * Gradient noise in SGD is approximately Gaussian --- ''Multivariate Gaussian'' $$ \mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}; \boldsymbol{\mu}, \Sigma) = \frac{1}{(2\pi)^{d/2}|\Sigma|^{1/2}} \exp\!\left(-\frac{1}{2}(\mathbf{x}-\boldsymbol{\mu})^\top \Sigma^{-1} (\mathbf{x}-\boldsymbol{\mu})\right) $$ $\Sigma \in \mathbb{R}^{d \times d}$: positive definite covariance matrix. The exponent $(\mathbf{x}-\boldsymbol{\mu})^\top \Sigma^{-1} (\mathbf{x}-\boldsymbol{\mu})$ is the ''Mahalanobis distance'' — it measures deviation from the mean in units of standard deviations, accounting for correlations. Contour lines of constant density are ''ellipsoids'' whose axes are the eigenvectors of $\Sigma$, with radii proportional to $\sqrt{\lambda_i}$. --- ''Useful Properties'' ''Closure under linear transformations'': if $\mathbf{x} \sim \mathcal{N}(\boldsymbol{\mu}, \Sigma)$ and $\mathbf{y} = A\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{b}$, then $\mathbf{y} \sim \mathcal{N}(A\boldsymbol{\mu}+\mathbf{b}, A\Sigma A^\top)$. ''Independence ↔ Uncorrelation'' (only for Gaussians): if $\mathbf{x}$ is jointly Gaussian and $\text{Cov}(X_i, X_j) = 0$, then $X_i \perp X_j$. ''Conditional distribution'': if $\begin{pmatrix}\mathbf{x}_1 \\ \mathbf{x}_2\end{pmatrix} \sim \mathcal{N}(\boldsymbol{\mu}, \Sigma)$ with block structure, then: $$ \mathbf{x}_1 | \mathbf{x}_2 \sim \mathcal{N}(\boldsymbol{\mu}_1 + \Sigma_{12}\Sigma_{22}^{-1}(\mathbf{x}_2 - \boldsymbol{\mu}_2),\; \Sigma_{11} - \Sigma_{12}\Sigma_{22}^{-1}\Sigma_{21}) $$ This is the foundation of Gaussian processes, Kalman filters, and Bayesian linear regression. ''Product of Gaussians is Gaussian'': $\mathcal{N}(\mu_1,\sigma_1^2) \cdot \mathcal{N}(\mu_2,\sigma_2^2) \propto \mathcal{N}(\mu_*, \sigma_*^2)$ with known formulas. The Gaussian is its own conjugate prior. --- ''The 68-95-99.7 Rule'' For $X \sim \mathcal{N}(\mu, \sigma^2)$: $$ P(|X-\mu| \leq \sigma) \approx 0.683 $$ $$ P(|X-\mu| \leq 2\sigma) \approx 0.954 $$ $$ P(|X-\mu| \leq 3\sigma) \approx 0.997 $$ In $d$ dimensions, the probability mass concentrates in a thin shell at radius $\approx \sqrt{d}$ — the ''concentration of measure'' phenomenon. Distances become less informative in high dimensions. --- ''Maximum Entropy Property'' Among all distributions with fixed mean $\mu$ and variance $\sigma^2$, the Gaussian has the ''maximum entropy''. It is the least informative distribution consistent with those two constraints — the "flattest" or "most spread out" option. This is another reason it appears as a natural default. --- ''Applications in ML'' * [[Gaussian Mixture Models]]: mixture of $K$ Gaussians for density estimation and clustering * [[Gaussian Processes]]: prior over functions; inference via conditional Gaussian formulas * [[Bayesian Methods]]: Gaussian prior + Gaussian likelihood = Gaussian posterior (conjugate) * [[Variational Autoencoder]]: $p(\mathbf{z}) = \mathcal{N}(0,I)$ as latent prior; ELBO = recon + KL to Gaussian * Noise models: weight initialisation, gradient noise, label noise * [[Maximum Likelihood Estimation]]: MLE under Gaussian noise = least squares <<button "Quiz: Why is uncorrelatedness equivalent to independence for Gaussians but not in general?">> <<replace "#gauss-q">> ''✓ For jointly Gaussian variables, the joint distribution is fully determined by means and covariances. Zero covariance makes the joint density factorise as $p(x_1,x_2) = p(x_1)p(x_2)$ — independence. For non-Gaussian variables, dependence can exist without linear correlation (e.g. $Y = X^2$: uncorrelated but dependent).'' <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="gauss-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Poisson Distribution'' Models the number of events occurring in a fixed interval of time or space when events happen at a constant rate and independently. $$ P(X = k) = \frac{\lambda^k e^{-\lambda}}{k!}, \quad k = 0, 1, 2, \ldots $$ ''Parameters'' Single parameter $\lambda > 0$: the expected number of events (both mean and variance equal $\lambda$). $$ E[X] = \lambda, \qquad \text{Var}(X) = \lambda $$ ''Derivation'' Poisson is the limit of Binomial$(n, p)$ as $n \to \infty$, $p \to 0$, with $np = \lambda$ fixed. Useful when $n$ is large and $p$ is small. ''Applications'' * Radioactive decay counts per second * Number of customer arrivals per hour * Rare disease occurrences per population * Error counts in a codebase per 1000 lines * Word frequencies in text (Zipf/Poisson mixture) ''Poisson Regression'' Generalised linear model for count data with a log link: $$ \log(\lambda_i) = \mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta} $$ Used for modelling counts as a function of covariates. <<button "Quiz: Why are mean and variance equal in the Poisson distribution?">> <<replace "#pois-q">> ''✓ Both equal $\lambda$ by construction. This "equidispersion" property is a strong assumption — real count data often shows overdispersion ($\text{Var} > \mu$), motivating Negative Binomial models.'' [[Bernoulli Distribution]] | [[Gaussian Distribution]] | [[Distribtuions]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="pois-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Bernoulli Distribution'' The simplest discrete distribution — a single binary trial. $$ P(X=1) = p, \quad P(X=0) = 1-p $$ $$ E[X] = p, \quad \text{Var}(X) = p(1-p) $$ ''Relation to Other Distributions'' * ''Binomial'': sum of $n$ independent Bernoulli trials → $\text{Bin}(n,p)$ * ''Geometric'': number of Bernoulli trials until first success * ''Categorical'': generalises Bernoulli to $k > 2$ classes * ''Logistic regression'': models $P(Y=1|\mathbf{x}) = \sigma(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x})$ — a Bernoulli likelihood ''Maximum Likelihood Estimation'' Given $n$ i.i.d. samples $x_1,\ldots,x_n \in \{0,1\}$: $$ \hat{p}_{\text{MLE}} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_i x_i = \bar{x} $$ The sample proportion maximises the Bernoulli log-likelihood. ''Cross-Entropy Connection'' Binary cross-entropy loss is the negative log-likelihood of a Bernoulli model: $$ \mathcal{L} = -\sum_i [y_i \log \hat{p}_i + (1-y_i)\log(1-\hat{p}_i)] $$ <<button "Quiz: What distribution is the sum of $n$ independent Bernoulli$(p)$ trials?">> <<replace "#bern-q">> ''✓ Binomial$(n, p)$: counts the number of successes in $n$ independent identical Bernoulli trials.'' [[Poisson Distribution]] | [[Gaussian Distribution]] | [[Cross-entropy]] | [[Logistic Regression]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="bern-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> While we like to think about how data stands on its own, it really doesn't. In Pure CS we can refer to this as casting. In very low level lanaguges we don't have types, rather we refer back to specific spots in memory, and read bits of a specific lenth. THat is a pointer, doesn't inherently mean anything, without the accompanying type information. It similar in Data Science. Here data only means something with an acccompaning set of assumptions. Let's start with a single number that defines a dataset: 10 Often, we assume that it means its the average because a the mean of data set is so typically chosen value. But of course it need not be that way. In that case we are really saying there is a function f() that takes data and returns a value. This value is assume to be the "best" approximation of the underlying data set. We dont' talk about the average that way, but its valid. We could choose another function, say the slope of a line that fits our data exactly. BLANK Image Alternatively, we could fit a polynomial where 10 is the value of a single sin wave that propogates endlesly. 10 could also be a categorical value that defines class. This gets even more interesting as we expand to a sequecne such as: x = [10, 11,10, 12,11, 10, 10, 9, 10, 8] <br /> <br /> <br /> <br /> <br /> [[Independent Draws From Same Distibution]] Whats next? A. [[treat as stationary time series| Stationary Time Series]] B. [[treat as non-stationary time series|Non-Stationary Time Series]]Note how we are treating this like a column vector... X = [[10,20], [11,10], [12,11], [10, 10], [9, 10], [8,10]] Y =[[4], [4], [4], [4], [4], [4]] The fundimental questions we are asking is is there a colleation between each column of data and the Y. How quikcly and efficently can we identify the existance of a relationship. <div> <button onclick="history.go()">Home</button> </div><<include "Header">> ## Covariance Matrix The covariance matrix captures the pairwise relationships between all variables in a dataset. It tells you how features vary together — which pairs move in the same direction, which in opposite directions, and which are independent. ''Definition'' For a random vector $\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{R}^d$ with mean $\boldsymbol{\mu}$: $$\boldsymbol{\Sigma} = \mathbb{E}[(\mathbf{x} - \boldsymbol{\mu})(\mathbf{x} - \boldsymbol{\mu})^\top]$$ The $(i,j)$ entry is $\text{Cov}(X_i, X_j) = \mathbb{E}[(X_i - \mu_i)(X_j - \mu_j)]$. The diagonal entries are variances: $\Sigma_{ii} = \text{Var}(X_i)$. ''Properties'' - Always symmetric: $\boldsymbol{\Sigma} = \boldsymbol{\Sigma}^\top$ - Always positive semi-definite: $\mathbf{v}^\top \boldsymbol{\Sigma} \mathbf{v} \geq 0$ for all $\mathbf{v}$ - Diagonal entries ≥ 0 (variances are non-negative) ''From sample data'' Given data matrix $\mathbf{X} \in \mathbb{R}^{n \times d}$ (centred): $$\hat{\boldsymbol{\Sigma}} = \frac{1}{n-1}\mathbf{X}^\top\mathbf{X}$$ ''Applications in ML'' [[PCA]] — principal components are eigenvectors of the covariance matrix [[Gaussian Distribution]] — a multivariate Gaussian is parameterised by $\boldsymbol{\mu}$ and $\boldsymbol{\Sigma}$ [[Gaussian Mixture Models]] — each component has its own covariance matrix [[Correlation Matrix]] — normalised covariance: $\text{Corr}(X_i, X_j) = \Sigma_{ij}/\sqrt{\Sigma_{ii}\Sigma_{jj}}$ --- [[Core Linear Algebra]] ← back | [[PCA]] | [[Gaussian Distribution]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Expected Value The expected value (expectation) of a random variable is its probability-weighted average — the value you would get "on average" over many repetitions. It is the single most important summary of a distribution. ''Definition'' For a discrete random variable $X$: $$\mathbb{E}[X] = \sum_x x \cdot P(X = x)$$ For a continuous random variable: $$\mathbb{E}[X] = \int_{-\infty}^\infty x \, p(x) \, dx$$ ''Properties'' - ''Linearity'': $\mathbb{E}[aX + bY] = a\mathbb{E}[X] + b\mathbb{E}[Y]$ — holds even if $X$ and $Y$ are dependent - ''Law of total expectation'': $\mathbb{E}[X] = \mathbb{E}[\mathbb{E}[X \mid Y]]$ - Expectation of a non-linear function: $\mathbb{E}[f(X)] \neq f(\mathbb{E}[X])$ in general (Jensen's inequality) ''In ML'' Loss functions are expectations over the data distribution: $$\mathcal{L}(\theta) = \mathbb{E}_{(x,y) \sim p_{\text{data}}}[\ell(f_\theta(x), y)]$$ SGD approximates this expectation with mini-batches. The quality of the approximation improves with batch size. ''Connections'' [[Distribtuions]] — expectation is derived from the distribution [[Stocastic Gradient Descent]] — approximates the expected gradient with a mini-batch [[Bayesian Methods]] — posterior expectations replace point estimates [[bellman equation]] — the Bellman equation is defined using expectations over transitions --- [[Statistics]] ← back | [[Distribtuions]] | [[Stocastic Gradient Descent]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## P-Value The p-value is the probability of observing data at least as extreme as what was observed, assuming the null hypothesis is true. It is the most widely used — and most widely misinterpreted — concept in statistics. ''Definition'' $$p = P(\text{data as extreme or more} \mid H_0 \text{ true})$$ A small p-value means: "if $H_0$ were true, seeing this data would be very unlikely." It does not mean the hypothesis is false, or that the result is practically important. ''The standard threshold'' $p < 0.05$ is conventional but arbitrary. Fisher chose it as a rough guide; it was never meant to be a universal standard. Many fields now require $p < 0.005$ or focus on effect sizes. ''What p-values do NOT mean'' - $p = 0.03$ does not mean there is a 3% chance $H_0$ is true - $p > 0.05$ does not mean $H_0$ is true — only that you lack evidence against it - A significant result is not necessarily a large or important effect ''Better practices'' - Report effect sizes and confidence intervals alongside p-values - [[hypothesis test]] — the broader framework p-values belong to - [[null hypothesis]] — what you are testing against - Pre-register your analysis to avoid p-hacking ''Connections in ML'' [[T-test]] — a specific hypothesis test with its own p-value [[A/B Testing]] — p-values are standard in A/B test analysis [[Spurious Correlations]] — multiple comparisons inflate false positive rates [[Regression to the Mean]] — a common source of spuriously significant results --- [[Statistics]] ← back | [[hypothesis test]] | [[null hypothesis]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Autocorrelation'' Autocorrelation (ACF) measures the correlation of a time series with a lagged version of itself: $$ \rho(k) = \frac{\text{Cov}(X_t, X_{t-k})}{\text{Var}(X_t)} = \frac{\gamma(k)}{\gamma(0)} \in [-1, 1] $$ where $\gamma(k)$ is the autocovariance at lag $k$. ''ACF vs PACF'' * ''ACF'': correlation at lag $k$ including indirect effects through intermediate lags * ''PACF'': correlation at lag $k$ ''after removing'' the effects of lags $1, \ldots, k-1$ ''Using ACF/PACF to Identify ARIMA Orders'' | Pattern | Suggests | | ACF tails off, PACF cuts off at $p$ | AR($p$) process | | ACF cuts off at $q$, PACF tails off | MA($q$) process | | Both tail off | ARMA process | | No significant lags | White noise | ''Ljung-Box Test'' Tests whether the first $h$ autocorrelations are jointly zero (white noise hypothesis). Used to check model residuals. ''Autocorrelation in ML'' * Violates i.i.d. assumption in regression * Temporal data leakage if train/test splits are random * Used in signal processing, anomaly detection, financial modelling <<button "Quiz: What does the PACF measure that the ACF does not?">> <<replace "#acf-q">> ''✓ The partial correlation at lag $k$ after removing contributions from shorter lags. This isolates the direct relationship between $X_t$ and $X_{t-k}$.'' [[ARIMA]] | [[ Stationary Time Series]] | [[Distributions]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="acf-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Correlation Matrix'' A symmetric matrix where entry $(i,j)$ is the Pearson correlation coefficient between features $i$ and $j$: $$ R_{ij} = \frac{\text{Cov}(X_i, X_j)}{\sigma_i \sigma_j} = \frac{\Sigma_{ij}}{\sqrt{\Sigma_{ii}\Sigma_{jj}}} \in [-1, 1] $$ ''Relation to Covariance Matrix'' $$ R = D^{-1/2} \Sigma D^{-1/2} $$ where $D = \text{diag}(\sigma_1^2, \ldots, \sigma_p^2)$. Correlation normalises by standard deviations — a scale-free version of covariance. ''Interpretation'' * $R_{ij} = 1$: perfect positive linear relationship * $R_{ij} = 0$: no linear relationship (may still have non-linear) * $R_{ij} = -1$: perfect negative linear relationship * $R_{ii} = 1$: diagonal is always 1 ''Uses in ML'' * Feature selection: drop one of any highly correlated pair ($|R| > 0.9$) — reduces multicollinearity * PCA: operates on the correlation matrix when features have different scales * Portfolio optimisation: covariance/correlation drives risk estimation * Spurious correlation detection ''Multicollinearity'' When predictors are highly correlated, OLS estimates become numerically unstable (large variance). VIF (Variance Inflation Factor) quantifies this. <<button "Quiz: How does the correlation matrix differ from the covariance matrix?">> <<replace "#cm-q">> ''✓ Correlation normalises each covariance by the product of standard deviations, putting all values in $[-1,1]$. It is scale-invariant — unaffected by the units or magnitude of each feature.'' [[Covariance Matrix]] | [[PCA]] | [[Feature Engineering]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="cm-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> <img src="assets/linear_regression.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="Linear regression diagram"/> ! Linear Regression !! The Foundational Idea Before neural networks, before support vectors, before kernels — there was linear regression. It is the simplest supervised learning model: assume the output is a linear function of the inputs, find the weights that fit the data best, make predictions. It is also the model you understand first and abandon last. Interpretable, fast, theoretically well-understood, and surprisingly effective on many real-world problems when features are engineered thoughtfully. !! The Model Predict output $\hat{y}$ as a weighted sum of input features: $\hat{y} = w_1 x_1 + w_2 x_2 + \cdots + w_p x_p + b = \mathbf{w}^\top \mathbf{x} + b$ Each weight $w_j$ represents how much $\hat{y}$ changes per unit increase in $x_j$, holding all other features constant. The bias $b$ sets the intercept. In 1D, this is a line. In $p$ dimensions, it's a hyperplane. !! The Loss: Mean Squared Error We want the line that best fits the training data. "Best" means minimizing the average squared residual: $\text{MSE} = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2$ Squaring residuals has two effects: it penalizes large errors more than small ones, and it makes all errors positive (so they don't cancel). The MSE loss surface is a convex bowl — there is exactly one global minimum. !! The Closed-Form Solution Unlike most ML models, linear regression has an exact analytical solution. Setting the gradient of MSE to zero and solving: $\mathbf{w}^* = (\mathbf{X}^\top \mathbf{X})^{-1} \mathbf{X}^\top \mathbf{y}$ This is the ''Normal Equation''. It requires inverting a $p \times p$ matrix — feasible when $p$ is small (hundreds of features), impractical when $p$ is large (millions). For large-scale problems, gradient descent is preferred. !! Geometric Interpretation Linear regression finds the projection of $\mathbf{y}$ onto the column space of $\mathbf{X}$. The fitted values $\hat{\mathbf{y}} = \mathbf{X}\mathbf{w}^*$ are the closest point in that column space to $\mathbf{y}$, measured in Euclidean distance. The residuals $\mathbf{e} = \mathbf{y} - \hat{\mathbf{y}}$ are orthogonal to every column of $\mathbf{X}$ — a geometric fact that directly yields the normal equation. !! Assumptions and Diagnostics The standard linear model assumes: (1) linearity — $E[y|\mathbf{x}] = \mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}+b$; (2) independence of errors; (3) constant variance (homoscedasticity); (4) normality of errors (for confidence intervals). Violations show up in residual plots. Curved residuals → linearity violated (add polynomial features). Fan-shaped residuals → heteroscedasticity (log-transform $y$). Outliers → consider robust regression or Huber loss. !! Regularized Variants Ordinary least squares overfits when features are collinear or when $p \approx n$. Regularization fixes both: ''Ridge (L2)'': $\min \|\mathbf{y}-\mathbf{X}\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \lambda\|\mathbf{w}\|^2$ — shrinks all weights, closed form: $(\mathbf{X}^\top\mathbf{X} + \lambda\mathbf{I})^{-1}\mathbf{X}^\top\mathbf{y}$ ''Lasso (L1)'': $\min \|\mathbf{y}-\mathbf{X}\mathbf{w}\|^2 + \lambda\|\mathbf{w}\|_1$ — drives many weights to zero (feature selection), no closed form !! Technical Details | Property | Value | |---------|-------| | Parameters | $p+1$ (weights + bias) | | Closed-form solution | $(\mathbf{X}^\top\mathbf{X})^{-1}\mathbf{X}^\top\mathbf{y}$ | | Complexity (normal eq.) | $O(np^2 + p^3)$ | | Complexity (gradient descent) | $O(np)$ per step | | Output | Continuous $\hat{y} \in \mathbb{R}$ | $R^2 = 1 - \text{SS}_\text{res}/\text{SS}_\text{tot}$ measures fraction of variance explained (1 = perfect, 0 = predicts mean only). !! Quiz <<button "Q1: Why is MSE the standard loss for linear regression?">><<replace "#linreg-q1">>MSE is convex in the weights, ensuring a unique global minimum. It penalizes large errors more than small ones. It also yields a clean closed-form solution (the normal equation) by setting the gradient to zero.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="linreg-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: What is the normal equation and when should you use it?">><<replace "#linreg-q2">>w* = (X^T X)^{-1} X^T y — the exact closed-form solution. Use it when p (features) is small (hundreds), since it requires inverting a p×p matrix. For large p or large n, gradient descent is more practical.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="linreg-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: What does R² measure and what does R²=0 mean?">><<replace "#linreg-q3">>R² measures the fraction of variance in y explained by the model. R²=1 means perfect predictions. R²=0 means the model is no better than predicting the mean of y — it captures none of the variance in the data.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="linreg-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: Why does Ridge regression always have a solution even when X^T X is singular?">><<replace "#linreg-q4">>Adding λI to X^T X ensures the matrix is positive definite and therefore invertible, even when X^T X is rank-deficient (e.g., when features are collinear or p > n). The λ shifts all eigenvalues up by λ.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="linreg-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: What does it mean geometrically that residuals are orthogonal to X?">><<replace "#linreg-q5">>The fitted values ŷ = Xw* are the orthogonal projection of y onto the column space of X. Residuals e = y − ŷ point in the direction perpendicular to that space. This orthogonality is exactly the condition X^T e = 0, which gives the normal equation.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="linreg-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Change of Basis'' Expressing the same vector in a different coordinate system by transforming between bases. ''Setup'' Given two bases $\mathcal{B} = \{\mathbf{b}_1,\ldots,\mathbf{b}_n\}$ and $\mathcal{C} = \{\mathbf{c}_1,\ldots,\mathbf{c}_n\}$, the change-of-basis matrix $P$ maps coordinates from $\mathcal{B}$ to $\mathcal{C}$: $$ [\mathbf{v}]_{\mathcal{C}} = P^{-1}[\mathbf{v}]_{\mathcal{B}} $$ The columns of $P$ are the $\mathcal{B}$ basis vectors expressed in the $\mathcal{C}$ coordinates. ''Diagonalisation'' A matrix $A$ is diagonalisable if there exists a basis of eigenvectors. In that basis: $$ A = P D P^{-1} $$ where $D$ is diagonal with eigenvalues. Computing $A^k = P D^k P^{-1}$ is trivial. ''PCA as Change of Basis'' PCA finds an orthonormal basis (principal components) aligned with maximum variance directions. Projecting data onto this basis gives uncorrelated features in decreasing order of variance. ''Why It Matters'' Many computations (matrix powers, exponentials, convolutions) become trivial in the right basis. Choosing coordinates wisely is the core of much of applied mathematics and signal processing. <<button "Quiz: Why does PCA change the basis of the data?">> <<replace "#cob-q">> ''✓ To align coordinates with the directions of maximum variance. In the new basis (eigenvectors of the covariance matrix), features are uncorrelated and ordered by how much variance they capture.'' [[PCA]] | [[Eiganvectors and Eiganvalue]] | [[Core Linear Algebra]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="cob-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Curse of Dimensionality As the number of dimensions grows, the geometry of space changes in counterintuitive ways that make many algorithms break down. More features are not always better. ''What goes wrong'' ''Distances become meaningless:'' In high dimensions, the ratio of the maximum to minimum distance between points approaches 1. All points look equally far apart — nearest-neighbour methods fail. ''Data becomes exponentially sparse:'' To maintain the same density, the number of data points must grow exponentially with dimension. $100$ points cover 1D well; to cover 100D equally well you need $100^{100}$ points. ''The unit hypercube'' Consider a unit hypercube in $d$ dimensions. A hypersphere inscribed in it has volume: $$V_d = \frac{\pi^{d/2}}{\Gamma(d/2+1)}$$ As $d \to \infty$, almost all the volume of the hypercube concentrates in the corners — far from the centre. Most of your data lives in a thin shell near the surface. ''Implications for ML'' - [[K-means Clustering]] — all points equidistant; cluster structure disappears - [[Kernel Methods]] — RBF kernel concentrations collapse - [[Regularization]] — essential when features $\gg$ samples - [[PCA]] / [[Lower Dimensional Representation]] — solutions: reduce to the dimensions that matter ''The saving grace: the manifold hypothesis'' Real high-dimensional data (images, text, audio) typically lies near a low-dimensional manifold embedded in a high-dimensional space. [[Embeddings]] and deep networks learn to find and represent this manifold. --- [[Mathmatical Concepts]] ← back | [[PCA]] | [[Lower Dimensional Representation]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Parametric Approaches'' A model is parametric if it is described by a fixed, finite set of parameters $\theta$ regardless of how much data is available. ''Examples'' * Linear regression: $\theta = (\mathbf{w}, b)$ — always $p+1$ parameters * Logistic regression, GMM, HMM, neural networks (fixed architecture) ''Non-Parametric Approaches'' The model complexity grows with data: * k-NN: stores all training data; "parameters" = the entire dataset * Kernel regression: uses all $n$ training points at prediction time * Gaussian Processes: covariance function over all data * Decision trees (fully grown): depth grows with data ''Trade-offs'' | | Parametric | Non-Parametric | | Complexity | Fixed | Grows with $n$ | | Speed (prediction) | Fast | Often $O(n)$ | | Assumptions | Stronger (functional form) | Weaker | | Asymptotic | Biased if misspecified | Consistent as $n\to\infty$ | ''Semi-parametric'': fix some structure, leave other aspects flexible (e.g. Cox proportional hazards model: parametric hazard ratio, non-parametric baseline hazard). <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Linear Combination'' A linear combination of vectors $\{\mathbf{v}_1,\ldots,\mathbf{v}_k\}$ is any expression of the form: $$ \mathbf{u} = \alpha_1 \mathbf{v}_1 + \alpha_2 \mathbf{v}_2 + \cdots + \alpha_k \mathbf{v}_k $$ The set of all such combinations is the ''span'' of the vectors. ''Linear Independence'' Vectors are linearly independent if no vector is a linear combination of the others — equivalently, $\sum_i \alpha_i \mathbf{v}_i = \mathbf{0} \implies \alpha_i = 0$ for all $i$. ''Everywhere in ML'' Every operation in a neural network layer is a linear combination followed by a nonlinearity. Attention is a weighted linear combination of value vectors. PCA components are linear combinations of original features. Word2Vec analogy arithmetic adds and subtracts word vectors (linear combinations). <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ! Principal Component Analysis (PCA) <img src="assets/pca.svg" alt="PCA diagram — data cloud with PC axes (left) and 1D projection (right)" style="width:100%;max-width:620px;display:block;margin:16px auto 24px auto;"> Real data is almost always redundant. If you measure height, weight, arm span, and shoe size on a thousand people, you don't really have four independent pieces of information — you have one strong underlying signal (body size) showing up four times, with noise. PCA is the tool that finds those underlying signals and discards the redundancy. --- !! The Core Insight Imagine plotting height against weight. The scatter forms an elongated ellipse tilted diagonally — tall people tend to weigh more, so the cloud stretches northeast. That diagonal is the direction of maximum variation across your data. It's also the most informative direction: it separates people most clearly, captures the most structure. ''PC1 is the long axis of that ellipse.'' It's the direction along which your data varies the most. PC2 is the short axis — perpendicular to PC1, capturing what's left over after PC1 is accounted for. Each subsequent component is orthogonal to all previous ones and captures the next largest slice of variation. This is the whole idea. PCA rotates your coordinate system to align with the natural axes of your data's variation, then lets you keep only the most important ones. --- !! Variance as Information Why should we care about directions of maximum variance? Because variance measures how much data points differ from each other along a direction. A direction where everything is the same value tells you nothing — every point looks identical from that angle. A direction with high variance is one that ''distinguishes'' points, which is exactly what makes it useful for analysis, visualisation, and compression. The flip side: low-variance directions are often noise. Measurement jitter, irrelevant fluctuations, rounding errors — these tend to show up as small, diffuse variance spread across many dimensions. Dropping them doesn't hurt much. --- !! What the Math Is Actually Doing The covariance matrix $\Sigma$ encodes how every pair of variables varies together. If height and weight tend to move in the same direction, their covariance is positive and large. PCA finds the ''eigenvectors'' of $\Sigma$ — the special directions that the covariance matrix simply stretches, without rotating. Those stretching directions are the principal components. The corresponding ''eigenvalues'' $\lambda_1 \geq \lambda_2 \geq \ldots \geq \lambda_p$ tell you the variance along each axis. A large $\lambda_1$ and small $\lambda_2$ means a very elongated ellipse — one direction dominates, and you can compress heavily without losing much. In practice, you rarely form $\Sigma$ explicitly. Instead you run SVD directly on the centred data matrix $\tilde{X}$. The right singular vectors //are// the eigenvectors of $\Sigma$, and the computation is more numerically stable. --- !! Choosing How Many Components You've run PCA. Now how many components do you keep? The ''Explained Variance Ratio'' answers this: $$\text{EVR}_k = \frac{\sum_{j=1}^{k} \lambda_j}{\sum_{j=1}^{p} \lambda_j}$$ This is the fraction of total variance your $k$ components capture. A scree plot — eigenvalues ranked largest to smallest — typically shows a sharp drop after the first few components, then a long flat tail. The "elbow" in that curve is a common place to cut. There's no universal right answer. For visualisation, $k = 2$ or $k = 3$ is forced on you. For preprocessing before a classifier, you might keep enough components for EVR = 0.95 and let the downstream model decide what matters. --- !! When PCA Breaks Down PCA is a linear method. It finds the best //flat// subspace to project onto. If your data lives on a curve — a Swiss roll, a sphere, a nonlinear manifold — PCA will smear it into a flat projection that misrepresents the structure. [[Lower Dimensional Representation|t-SNE or UMAP]] handle those cases. PCA is also sensitive to scale. A feature measured in millimetres will dominate one measured in kilometres. Always standardise features to zero mean and unit variance before running PCA when your variables have different units. Outliers are another hazard. A single extreme point can pull the leading eigenvector toward it, since variance is quadratic in distance. Robust PCA variants address this. --- !! Technical Details ''Three equivalent formulations:'' (1) Maximum variance — find unit vectors maximising $\mathbf{w}^\top \Sigma \mathbf{w}$, solved by the top eigenvectors of $\Sigma$. (2) Minimum reconstruction error — the same eigenvectors minimise $\frac{1}{n}\sum_i \|\mathbf{x}_i - \hat{\mathbf{x}}_i\|^2$, and the error equals $\sum_{j=k+1}^p \lambda_j$. (3) Best low-rank approximation — by the Eckart-Young theorem, the truncated SVD $\tilde{X} \approx U_k \Sigma_k V_k^\top$ is the optimal rank-$k$ approximation in Frobenius norm. ''Algorithm:'' (1) Centre: $\tilde{X} = X - \bar{\mathbf{x}}\mathbf{1}^\top$. (2) SVD: $\tilde{X} = U \Sigma_s V^\top$. (3) Principal components are the columns of $V$; scores are $Z = \tilde{X} V_k$. For $p \gg n$, use randomised SVD (Halko et al. 2011) for $O(npk)$ complexity. ''Probabilistic PCA:'' A generative model $\mathbf{x} = W\mathbf{z} + \boldsymbol{\mu} + \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}$ with $\mathbf{z} \sim \mathcal{N}(0,I)$, $\boldsymbol{\varepsilon} \sim \mathcal{N}(0,\sigma^2 I)$ recovers standard PCA as $\sigma^2 \to 0$ and handles missing data naturally via EM. ''Linear autoencoders:'' A linear encoder-decoder pair trained with MSE loss converges to the PCA subspace. Deep nonlinear autoencoders generalise this to curved manifolds. --- !! Quiz ''1. What is the "maximum variance" formulation of PCA, and what is its solution?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#pca-q1">>'✓ Find the unit vector $\mathbf{w}_1$ maximising $\mathbf{w}^\top \Sigma \mathbf{w}$ subject to $\|\mathbf{w}\|=1$. It equals the sum of the discarded eigenvalues: $\sum_{j=k+1}^p \lambda_j$. Minimising reconstruction error is mathematically identical to maximising retained variance.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="pca-q7"></span> ''8. MLE recovers standard PCA as $\sigma^2\to 0$. What is the connection between a linear autoencoder and PCA?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#pca-q10">>'✓ A linear autoencoder (encoder $\in\mathbb{R}^{p\times k}$, decoder $\in\mathbb{R}^{k\times p}$, MSE loss) trained to minimise reconstruction error converges to the PCA subspace. The weight matrices span the same column space as the top $k$ eigenvectors. Nonlinear autoencoders generalise this to curved manifolds.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="pca-q10"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Fast Fourier Transform (FFT)'' An efficient algorithm to compute the Discrete Fourier Transform (DFT) in $O(n \log n)$ instead of $O(n^2)$. ''Discrete Fourier Transform'' $$ X[k] = \sum_{n=0}^{N-1} x[n] \cdot e^{-i2\pi kn/N}, \quad k = 0,\ldots,N-1 $$ Decomposes a discrete signal into its frequency components. $|X[k]|$ is the amplitude at frequency $k/N$. ''Cooley-Tukey Algorithm'' Exploits the structure of DFT by recursively splitting into even and odd indices (divide and conquer): $$ X[k] = \underbrace{\sum_{n=0}^{N/2-1} x[2n] e^{-i2\pi kn/(N/2)}}_{E[k]} + e^{-i2\pi k/N}\underbrace{\sum_{n=0}^{N/2-1} x[2n+1] e^{-i2\pi kn/(N/2)}}_{O[k]} $$ ''Applications in ML'' * Audio: spectrograms for speech/music models (mel-filterbank, MFCCs) * Time series: frequency-domain features; anomaly detection * Convolution theorem: $f * g = \text{IFFT}(\text{FFT}(f) \cdot \text{FFT}(g))$ — faster than direct convolution for large filters * FNet (Lee-Thorp 2021): replaces attention with Fourier transforms in transformers <<button "Quiz: What is the computational advantage of FFT over naive DFT?">> <<replace "#fft-q">> ''✓ FFT computes the DFT in $O(n \log n)$ vs $O(n^2)$ for the naive algorithm. For $n = 10^6$ that is a $\sim 50{,}000\times$ speedup.'' [[Auto-Correlation]] | [[ Stationary Time Series]] | [[Branches of Mathmatics]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="fft-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''ARIMA — AutoRegressive Integrated Moving Average'' The classical workhorse model for univariate time series forecasting. Combines three components: $$ \underbrace{\text{AR}(p)}_{\text{autoregressive}} + \underbrace{I(d)}_{\text{differencing}} + \underbrace{\text{MA}(q)}_{\text{moving average}} $$ ''Model Equation'' After differencing $d$ times, the series $X'_t$ follows: $$ X'_t = c + \sum_{i=1}^p \phi_i X'_{t-i} + \varepsilon_t + \sum_{j=1}^q \theta_j \varepsilon_{t-j} $$ * $p$: AR order — how many past values predict the present * $d$: integration order — how many differences to achieve stationarity * $q$: MA order — how many past errors enter the model * $\varepsilon_t$: white noise residuals ''Choosing $(p,d,q)$'' * $d$: ADF test until stationary * $p$: partial autocorrelation function (PACF) cuts off at lag $p$ * $q$: autocorrelation function (ACF) cuts off at lag $q$ * Or: use AIC/BIC to select automatically (auto-ARIMA) ''Seasonal ARIMA (SARIMA)'' Adds seasonal AR, I, MA terms: SARIMA$(p,d,q)(P,D,Q)_s$ ''Modern Alternatives'' Prophet (Facebook), neural AR models (N-BEATS), Temporal Fusion Transformers outperform ARIMA on many benchmarks. <<button "Quiz: What does the 'I' in ARIMA stand for, and what does it fix?">> <<replace "#arima-q">> ''✓ Integrated — the series is differenced $d$ times to make it stationary. Without differencing, AR and MA components cannot be reliably estimated.'' [[ Stationary Time Series]] | [[Non-Stationary Time Series]] | [[Auto-Correlation]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="arima-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Parametric Stationary Time Series Models'' Closed-form, parameter-estimated models for stationary sequences. ''AR(p) — AutoRegressive'' $$ X_t = c + \sum_{i=1}^p \phi_i X_{t-i} + \varepsilon_t $$ Stationarity requires roots of $1 - \phi_1 z - \cdots - \phi_p z^p = 0$ to lie outside the unit circle. ''MA(q) — Moving Average'' $$ X_t = \mu + \varepsilon_t + \sum_{j=1}^q \theta_j \varepsilon_{t-j} $$ Always stationary; invertibility requires roots of $1 + \theta_1 z + \cdots$ outside unit circle. ''ARMA(p,q)'' Combines both: AR terms capture autocorrelation at long lags efficiently; MA terms handle short-lag dynamics. ''Parameter Estimation'' * Yule-Walker equations (moments method for AR) * Maximum likelihood via Kalman filter * Conditional least squares (fast but biased) ''Model Selection'' ACF → MA order; PACF → AR order; AIC/BIC → compare models. Residuals should be white noise (Ljung-Box test). ''Contrast with Non-parametric'': kernel smoothers, spectral methods make no parametric assumptions but need more data. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> <img src="assets/lstm.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="LSTM architecture diagram"/> ! Long Short-Term Memory (LSTM) !! The Problem with Plain RNNs A recurrent neural network processes sequences by maintaining a hidden state $h_t$ that carries information forward: $h_t = \tanh(W_h h_{t-1} + W_x x_t)$. In principle, this hidden state can remember anything. In practice, it remembers almost nothing from more than a few steps ago. The reason is ''vanishing gradients''. To learn from an event 50 steps ago, the gradient must flow backward through 50 matrix multiplications. At each step it's multiplied by a weight matrix whose eigenvalues are typically less than 1 — so the gradient shrinks exponentially, effectively vanishing before it can update early parameters. The RNN can't learn long-range dependencies. !! The LSTM Solution: A Protected Memory Lane The LSTM (Hochreiter & Schmidhuber, 1997) adds a second pathway: the ''cell state'' $C_t$, a lane of memory that runs parallel to the hidden state but passes through the network with only linear interactions — no repeated multiplication by learned matrices. Information placed into the cell state travels nearly unchanged across many time steps. Three ''gates'' control what information flows where. Each gate is a sigmoid layer outputting values in [0, 1] — think of them as soft on/off switches. !! The Three Gates ''Forget gate'': Should we erase anything from memory? $f_t = \sigma(W_f [h_{t-1}, x_t] + b_f)$ Output near 0 → forget; near 1 → keep. If the network is processing a new sentence, the forget gate learns to reset subject/verb memory from the previous sentence. ''Input gate'': What new information should enter memory? $i_t = \sigma(W_i [h_{t-1}, x_t] + b_i)$, $\tilde{C}_t = \tanh(W_C [h_{t-1}, x_t] + b_C)$ The input gate decides ''which'' locations to update; the candidate $\tilde{C}_t$ provides the new content to write there. ''Output gate'': What do we read out to produce $h_t$? $o_t = \sigma(W_o [h_{t-1}, x_t] + b_o)$, $h_t = o_t \cdot \tanh(C_t)$ The output gate selects which parts of the cell state are relevant to expose as the hidden state (and thus to downstream computation). !! Cell State Update $C_t = f_t \odot C_{t-1} + i_t \odot \tilde{C}_t$ This is the key equation. Element-wise multiply the old cell state by the forget gate (selectively erase), then add the gated candidate (selectively write). Because this is addition — not multiplication through a sigmoid — gradients flow backward through the cell state with near-zero attenuation. Long-range learning becomes possible. !! Variants and Successors ''Peephole connections'' allow gates to see the cell state directly, not just $h_{t-1}$. ''GRU'' (Gated Recurrent Unit) merges the cell and hidden states into one, combining forget and input gates into a single ''update gate'' — fewer parameters, often comparable performance. For many tasks, ''Transformers'' now outperform LSTMs by using attention to connect any two positions directly, bypassing sequential processing entirely. But LSTMs remain valuable for streaming data, time-series, and resource-constrained settings. !! Technical Details | Quantity | Dimension | Description | |----------|-----------|-------------| | $x_t$ | input_size | Current input | | $h_t$ | hidden_size | Hidden/output state | | $C_t$ | hidden_size | Cell state | | Gates $f,i,o$ | hidden_size | Sigmoid activations | | $\tilde{C}_t$ | hidden_size | Candidate cell values | Parameter count: $4 \times$ hidden_size $\times$ (input_size + hidden_size + 1) !! Quiz <<button "Q1: Why do plain RNNs fail at long-range dependencies?">><<replace "#lstm-q1">>Backpropagating through many time steps requires multiplying gradients by the same weight matrix repeatedly. Since eigenvalues are typically < 1, gradients shrink exponentially and cannot reach distant time steps.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lstm-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: What is the cell state and why does it help?">><<replace "#lstm-q2">>The cell state is a protected memory lane that runs through the network with only additive interactions (no repeated matrix multiplications). Gradients can flow backward through it without vanishing.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lstm-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: What does the forget gate do?">><<replace "#lstm-q3">>It decides which elements of the previous cell state to erase. A value near 0 means "forget this"; near 1 means "keep this." For example, it resets irrelevant context when new information arrives.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lstm-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: How does the cell state update combine forgetting and writing?">><<replace "#lstm-q4">>C_t = f_t ⊙ C_{t-1} + i_t ⊙ C̃_t. The forget gate selectively erases old memory; the input gate selectively writes new candidate values. Addition (not multiplication) keeps gradients stable.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lstm-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: How does a GRU differ from an LSTM?">><<replace "#lstm-q5">>A GRU merges the cell and hidden states into one, and combines the forget and input gates into a single update gate. Fewer parameters, less memory, often similar performance — faster to train on smaller datasets.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lstm-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> <img src="assets/cnn.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="CNN diagram"/> ! Convolutional Neural Networks !! The Problem with Fully-Connected Layers A fully-connected network treats every pixel as independent. Feed it a 256×256 image and the first layer has 65,536 inputs connected to every neuron — millions of parameters just to process one image. Worse, it has no sense of space: shift an object one pixel to the right and the network sees a completely different input. Images have structure. Edges, textures, and shapes appear at different locations, and recognizing them shouldn't require separate learned features for each possible position. CNNs exploit this structure. !! Convolution: Pattern Detection by Sliding a Filter A ''convolutional filter'' (kernel) is a small matrix of learned weights — typically 3×3 or 5×5. It slides across the input, computing a dot product at each position. The result is a ''feature map'': high values where the pattern matches, low values where it doesn't. A 3×3 filter looking for vertical edges will fire strongly wherever dark pixels are left and bright pixels are right. Crucially, the same filter is used everywhere — the network learns the pattern once and detects it anywhere in the image. This is called ''parameter sharing'', and it's why CNNs need far fewer parameters than fully-connected nets. Multiple filters run in parallel on the same input, each detecting a different feature. A layer with 64 filters produces a 64-channel feature map — 64 different "views" of the input. !! Building Up Representations Early layers detect simple, local patterns: edges, corners, color gradients. Later layers combine these into higher-level features: textures, object parts, whole objects. This hierarchical composition mirrors how the visual cortex works. ''Pooling'' layers reduce spatial dimensions between convolutional layers. Max pooling takes the maximum activation in each small window — discarding exact position while keeping the presence of a feature. This gives the network ''translation invariance'': a cat in the upper-left and a cat in the lower-right both produce similar deep representations. !! Stride and Receptive Field ''Stride'' controls how far the filter moves at each step. Stride 1 = move one pixel; stride 2 = move two pixels (halving the output size). Larger strides reduce computation but also resolution. Each neuron in a deep layer only "sees" a portion of the original image — its ''receptive field''. After several layers, deep neurons have large receptive fields covering the entire image, allowing them to integrate global context. !! From Features to Predictions After several convolutional and pooling layers, the feature maps are flattened into a vector and passed through fully-connected layers. The final layer has one output per class, producing logits that a softmax converts to probabilities. This architecture — many conv/pool layers followed by FC layers — was pioneered by LeNet (1989) and made practical by AlexNet (2012). Modern networks like ResNet and EfficientNet add skip connections and other innovations but retain the core convolutional structure. !! Technical Details | Layer type | Operation | Key parameters | |-----------|-----------|---------------| | Conv | Sliding dot product | filters, kernel_size, stride, padding | | ReLU | max(0, x) | — | | MaxPool | max in window | pool_size, stride | | BatchNorm | normalize activations | — | | FC | Matrix multiply | units | Parameter count of one conv layer: (k × k × C_in + 1) × C_out where k = kernel size, C = channels !! Quiz <<button "Q1: Why do CNNs use far fewer parameters than fully-connected networks for images?">><<replace "#cnn-q1">>Parameter sharing: the same filter weights are used at every spatial position. A 3×3×3 filter detecting edges uses only 27 weights regardless of image size, vs. millions of unique weights in a fully-connected layer.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="cnn-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: What does a convolutional filter detect?">><<replace "#cnn-q2">>A specific local pattern — such as a vertical edge, a particular color gradient, or a texture. The filter outputs high values wherever that pattern appears in the input and low values elsewhere.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="cnn-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: What is the purpose of pooling layers?">><<replace "#cnn-q3">>To reduce spatial resolution, decreasing computation and building translation invariance. Max pooling keeps the strongest activation in each region — "was this feature present?" — discarding exact position.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="cnn-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: Why do early layers detect edges but deep layers detect objects?">><<replace "#cnn-q4">>Each layer combines patterns from the layer below. Edges compose into textures; textures into shapes; shapes into object parts; parts into objects. Hierarchical composition builds complexity layer by layer.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="cnn-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: What is a receptive field?">><<replace "#cnn-q5">>The region of the original input that influences a given neuron's activation. Neurons in early layers have small receptive fields (3×3 pixels); neurons in deep layers can have receptive fields spanning the entire image.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="cnn-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Recurrent Neural Networks RNNs process sequences by maintaining a hidden state that is updated at each timestep. The same parameters are applied at every step — making them naturally suited for variable-length sequences. ''The update equation'' $$\mathbf{h}_t = \sigma(\mathbf{W}_h \mathbf{h}_{t-1} + \mathbf{W}_x \mathbf{x}_t + \mathbf{b})$$ The hidden state $\mathbf{h}_t$ summarises everything the network has seen up to time $t$. ''The core problem: vanishing gradients'' Backpropagating through many timesteps multiplies gradients repeatedly. If the gradient is $< 1$, it vanishes; if $> 1$, it explodes. Long-range dependencies are effectively invisible. [[vanishing gradient]] | [[exploding gradient]] ''Solutions'' [[LSTM]] — Long Short-Term Memory; gated architecture that controls what to remember and forget Gated Recurrent Unit (GRU) — simpler gated alternative to LSTM [[Attention]] — let the model directly attend to any past position; precursor to Transformers [[Transformers]] — replaced RNNs for most sequence tasks by attending globally ''When RNNs are still used'' - Low-resource settings where transformer pre-training is not available - Streaming inference where tokens arrive one at a time - [[ARIMA]] / time series when combined with statistical models ''Connections'' [[LSTM]] — the practical workhorse; fixes the vanishing gradient problem [[Backpropogation and Chain Rule]] — backprop through time (BPTT) [[Sequential NN]] — contrast with feedforward architecture --- [[Deep Learning]] ← back | [[LSTM]] | [[Transformers]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ! Attention and Transformers <img src="assets/attention.svg" alt="Attention — score heatmap (left) and QKV mechanism (right)" style="width:100%;max-width:620px;display:block;margin:16px auto 24px auto;"> Before attention, sequence models processed tokens one at a time — each token only indirectly influenced tokens far away, through a chain of recurrent hidden states that degraded with distance. Attention broke this constraint. Every token can directly attend to every other token in a single operation. This simple change unlocked transformers, which now underlie virtually every state-of-the-art model in language, vision, and beyond. --- !! The Core Idea: Soft Lookup Think of attention as a ''soft database lookup''. You have a query — something you're looking for — and a set of key-value pairs. Hard lookup returns the value whose key exactly matches your query. Soft lookup returns a weighted average of //all// values, where the weights reflect how well each key matches your query. In a sequence, each position acts as both a query (what am I looking for?) and a key-value pair (what do I contain, and what do I say?). Attention lets every position gather information from every other position, weighted by relevance. --- !! Scaled Dot-Product Attention The standard formulation: $$\text{Attention}(Q, K, V) = \text{softmax}\!\left(\frac{QK^\top}{\sqrt{d_k}}\right) V$$ $Q$, $K$, $V$ are matrices of queries, keys, and values — linear projections of the input. The dot product $QK^\top$ measures similarity between every query-key pair. Dividing by $\sqrt{d_k}$ prevents the dot products from growing large and saturating the softmax. Applying softmax converts scores to weights that sum to 1. Multiplying by $V$ takes the weighted average of values. The result: each position's output is a weighted combination of all other positions' values, where the weights reflect learned relevance. --- !! Multi-Head Attention A single attention head can only look for one kind of relationship. ''Multi-head attention'' runs $h$ attention heads in parallel, each with its own $Q$, $K$, $V$ projections, then concatenates and projects the results: $$\text{MultiHead}(Q,K,V) = \text{Concat}(\text{head}_1, \ldots, \text{head}_h) W^O$$ Different heads learn to attend to different relationships simultaneously — one might track syntactic dependencies, another coreference, another positional proximity. This is one of the key reasons transformers are so expressive. --- !! The Transformer Architecture The transformer stacks layers, each containing two sub-components: ''Multi-head self-attention'' — each token attends to all tokens in the sequence (including itself). This is where information flows between positions. ''Feed-forward network'' — a two-layer MLP applied independently to each position. This is where per-position computation happens. Each sub-component is wrapped in a residual connection and layer normalisation: $\text{output} = \text{LayerNorm}(x + \text{sublayer}(x))$. The residual connections are critical for training deep transformers — they keep gradients flowing. --- !! Positional Encoding Self-attention is ''permutation-equivariant'' — it treats the input as a set, not a sequence. Swapping two tokens gives swapped outputs, with no sense of order. To encode position, transformers add a ''positional encoding'' to each token embedding before the first layer. Original transformers used sinusoidal encodings. Modern models use learned encodings, or rotary position embeddings (RoPE) which encode relative position directly into the attention computation. --- !! Why Attention Replaced RNNs RNNs process sequences step by step — inherently sequential, hard to parallelise. The path length between positions grows linearly with sequence distance, making long-range dependencies hard to learn. Attention has constant path length between any two positions — a direct connection. And the entire computation is a matrix multiplication, which parallelises perfectly on GPUs. This combination of expressiveness and parallelism is why transformers took over. --- !! Technical Details ''Complexity:'' self-attention is $O(n^2 d)$ in sequence length $n$ and dimension $d$ — the quadratic cost is the main limitation for long sequences. Efficient attention variants (Flashattention, linear attention, sparse attention) reduce this. ''Masking:'' in decoders (autoregressive generation), future positions are masked so each token can only attend to past positions — the causal mask. ''Cross-attention:'' in encoder-decoder architectures, the decoder attends to encoder outputs. $Q$ comes from the decoder, $K$ and $V$ from the encoder. ''Key dimensions:'' a typical transformer layer has $d_{\text{model}} = 512$–$4096$, $h = 8$–$32$ heads, $d_k = d_{\text{model}}/h$. --- !! Quiz ''1. What problem does attention solve that RNNs struggle with?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#att-q1">>'✓ Long-range dependencies. In RNNs, information from position $i$ must pass through every intermediate hidden state to reach position $j$ — the signal degrades over distance. Attention provides a direct connection between any two positions in a single step, with path length 1 regardless of sequence length. This makes attention far better at capturing relationships between distant tokens.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="att-q1"></span> ''2. What do Q, K, and V stand for and what is each used for?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#att-q2">>'✓ Query (Q): what this position is looking for. Key (K): what each position offers as a match to queries. Value (V): what each position actually contributes to the output. The dot product Q·Kᵀ measures query-key similarity; softmax converts to weights; multiplying by V produces the weighted sum. All three are learned linear projections of the same input.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="att-q2"></span> ''3. Why divide by $\sqrt{d_k}$ in the attention formula?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#att-q3">>'✓ For random vectors of dimension $d_k$, their dot product has variance proportional to $d_k$. Without scaling, large $d_k$ pushes dot products into regions where softmax is nearly one-hot — the gradient near-zero. Dividing by $\sqrt{d_k}$ keeps dot products at unit variance, softmax in its sensitive regime, and gradients healthy.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="att-q3"></span> ''4. A single head can only represent one attention pattern at a time; multiple heads allow the model to jointly attend to information from different representation subspaces.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="att-q4"></span> ''5. Why is self-attention $O(n^2)$ and why does this matter?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#att-q5">>'✓ Every token attends to every other token — $n$ queries each compared to $n$ keys gives $n^2$ dot products, each of dimension $d_k$. Total: $O(n^2 d_k)$. For short sequences (n < 2048) this is fine. For very long sequences (documents, genomes, high-res images), the quadratic cost in memory and compute becomes prohibitive. Efficient attention variants (sparse, linear, FlashAttention) address this with various approximations.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="att-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> <img src="assets/random_forest.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="Random Forest diagram"/> ! Random Forest !! The Wisdom of Crowds Applied to Models A single decision tree is fast and interpretable but fragile. Change a few training examples and the tree changes dramatically — high variance. The classic solution: build many trees and let them vote. But there's a catch. If all trees see the same data, they'll all make similar errors. Their votes are correlated, so averaging doesn't help much. A good ensemble needs ''diverse'' members — forecasters who make different mistakes. Random forests solve the correlation problem with two sources of randomness. !! Bagging: Different Data for Each Tree ''Bootstrap aggregating (bagging)'' trains each tree on a different random sample of the training data, drawn ''with replacement''. A bootstrap sample of $n$ examples from $n$ points has, on average, about 63% unique examples ($1 - 1/e \approx 0.632$). The remaining ~37% are duplicates; the original points left out are called ''out-of-bag'' (OOB) examples. Because each tree sees a different slice of reality, they generalize differently and make different errors. Averaging their predictions cancels out individual mistakes — as long as the errors are uncorrelated. !! Feature Randomness: Decorrelating the Trees Bagging alone doesn't decorrelate strongly if one feature is very powerful. Every tree will split on that feature at the root, and their predictions become similar again. Random forests add a second trick: at each split, only a random subset of $m$ features is considered (typically $m = \sqrt{p}$ for classification, $m = p/3$ for regression). The dominant feature can only be used in trees that happen to sample it. Other features get their chance, producing genuinely diverse trees. !! Voting and Averaging For ''classification'': each tree casts a vote for a class; the majority wins. For ''regression'': each tree outputs a number; the forest averages them. The forest prediction is more stable than any individual tree's and typically much more accurate. The variance of the average of $B$ trees is: $\frac{\rho\sigma^2}{B} + (1-\rho)\frac{\sigma^2}{B} \approx \rho\sigma^2$ for large $B$ where $\rho$ is the correlation between trees. When $\rho$ is small (decorrelated trees), averaging drives variance toward zero. When $\rho = 1$ (identical trees), averaging doesn't help at all. !! Out-of-Bag Evaluation Because each tree is trained on ~63% of the data, the remaining 37% can evaluate it for free — no separate validation set needed. Each example is out-of-bag for roughly 37% of trees, and averaging those trees' predictions on it gives an ''OOB error estimate''. In practice OOB error is nearly as reliable as cross-validation. !! Feature Importance A natural byproduct: how much does each feature contribute? For each feature, sum the improvement in split quality (Gini decrease or variance reduction) across all nodes in all trees that used it, weighted by the number of examples reaching each node. Features with high total improvement are important. Random forests also support ''permutation importance'': shuffle one feature's values and measure how much OOB accuracy drops. Drop = importance. !! Technical Details | Hyperparameter | Typical value | Effect | |----------------|---------------|--------| | n_estimators | 100–500 | More trees → lower variance, slower | | max_features | √p (classif.), p/3 (regr.) | Lower → more decorrelated | | max_depth | None (full trees) | Deeper → lower bias | | min_samples_leaf | 1 | Higher → regularization | Random forests are relatively robust to hyperparameters. The main knob is n_estimators (more is almost always better until it's too slow). !! Quiz <<button "Q1: Why does a single decision tree have high variance?">><<replace "#rf-q1">>Trees are sensitive to the training data — small changes can produce completely different split structure. They overfit to whatever data they see, making predictions unstable.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="rf-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: What is bootstrap sampling and why does it help?">><<replace "#rf-q2">>Each tree is trained on a random sample with replacement from the training data (~63% unique). Different trees see different versions of the data, so they make different errors — averaging cancels individual mistakes.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="rf-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: Why are random features selected at each split?">><<replace "#rf-q3">>To decorrelate the trees. Without it, all trees would split on the same dominant features and make correlated errors. Random feature selection forces trees to use different features, producing genuinely diverse ensembles.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="rf-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: What are out-of-bag examples and how are they useful?">><<replace "#rf-q4">>The ~37% of training examples not seen by each tree during training. These can evaluate each tree's predictions for free, giving an OOB error estimate that serves as a nearly free cross-validation.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="rf-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: How does feature importance work in random forests?">><<replace "#rf-q5">>Sum the reduction in impurity (Gini or variance) at every node that uses each feature, across all trees, weighted by sample count. Features causing larger average improvements are considered more important.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="rf-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> <img src="assets/regularization.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="Regularization diagram"/> ! Regularization !! The Core Problem: Overfitting Imagine fitting a curve through 10 data points. With enough flexibility — a degree-9 polynomial — you can pass through every single point perfectly. Training loss: zero. But show the model a new point and it fails spectacularly. This is overfitting: the model memorized the training data instead of learning the underlying pattern. Regularization is the family of techniques that deliberately constrain a model to prevent this. We accept a little more training error in exchange for much better generalization. !! The Bias–Complexity Trade-off A model's parameters are its degrees of freedom. More parameters → more expressive → more ability to overfit. Regularization imposes a ''cost'' on using that expressiveness. The model must earn every unit of complexity by reducing loss enough to justify it. Think of it like a budget: you can spend model capacity, but each dollar must buy genuine predictive power, not just noise-fitting. !! L2 Regularization (Ridge) The most common form adds the sum of squared weights to the loss: $J(\theta) = L(\theta) + \frac{\lambda}{2} \|\theta\|^2$ The $\lambda$ (lambda) term controls strength. During gradient descent, every weight is pulled slightly toward zero each step — called ''weight decay''. Large weights require the model to justify them with proportionally large reductions in prediction error. Geometrically, L2 constrains parameters to lie within a sphere centered at the origin. The optimum is where that sphere first touches the loss surface's contour lines. !! L1 Regularization (Lasso) Replace squared weights with absolute values: $J(\theta) = L(\theta) + \lambda \|\theta\|_1$ L1 has a remarkable property: it drives many weights to ''exactly zero'', producing sparse solutions. This makes Lasso act as automatic feature selection — irrelevant features get zeroed out entirely. The geometry is a diamond (L1 ball) whose sharp corners naturally land on axes. When to use which: L2 when you believe all features are somewhat relevant; L1 when you suspect many features are noise and want the model to ignore them. !! Dropout: Regularization by Noise For neural networks, dropout is an elegant alternative. During training, randomly silence a fraction $p$ of neurons each forward pass. The network never knows which neurons will be available, so it cannot rely on any single pathway — every sub-network must learn redundant, robust representations. At test time, all neurons are active, but their outputs are scaled by $(1-p)$ to maintain the same expected activation magnitude. Dropout can be interpreted as training an exponential ensemble of networks simultaneously. !! Other Regularization Strategies ''Data augmentation'' — generating synthetic training examples — regularizes by making the model see more variation. ''Early stopping'' halts training before the model starts fitting noise (a form of implicit regularization). ''Batch normalization'' acts as a regularizer through its noise-inducing mini-batch statistics. ''Max-norm constraints'' cap weight magnitudes directly. !! Technical Details | Method | Penalty term | Effect | |--------|-------------|--------| | Ridge (L2) | $\lambda\|\theta\|^2/2$ | Shrinks weights, keeps all features | | Lasso (L1) | $\lambda\|\theta\|_1$ | Sparse weights, feature selection | | Elastic Net | $\alpha L_1 + (1-\alpha)L_2$ | Combines both | | Dropout | Random zeroing (rate $p$) | Neural network ensemble | | Weight decay | Equivalent to L2 | Common optimizer option | The effective learning update under L2: $\theta \leftarrow \theta(1 - \eta\lambda) - \eta\nabla L(\theta)$ !! Quiz <<button "Q1: What does regularization fundamentally do?">><<replace "#r-q1">>It constrains model complexity to reduce overfitting — trading a small increase in training error for better generalization to new data.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="r-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: Why does L1 produce sparse weights but L2 doesn't?">><<replace "#r-q2">>The L1 ball's corners land on coordinate axes, driving some weights to exactly zero. L2's smooth sphere never intersects axes exactly — all weights shrink but remain non-zero.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="r-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: What does the λ hyperparameter control?">><<replace "#r-q3">>Regularization strength. High λ → heavy penalty on large weights → simpler model (high bias). Low λ → weak penalty → model can overfit (high variance).<</replace>><</button>> <span id="r-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: Why does dropout work as regularization?">><<replace "#r-q4">>By randomly disabling neurons during training, the network cannot rely on any single feature or pathway. It learns redundant representations, equivalent to averaging many subnetworks.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="r-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: What is weight decay and how does it relate to L2?">><<replace "#r-q5">>Weight decay multiplies each weight by (1−ηλ) before the gradient step, shrinking it toward zero. This is mathematically identical to L2 regularization added to the loss.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="r-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> <img src="assets/logistic_regression.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="Logistic regression diagram"/> ! Logistic Regression !! A Linear Model for Classification Logistic regression is a classification algorithm that answers: "what is the probability this input belongs to class 1?" Despite the name, it classifies — the "regression" refers to the fact that it models a continuous probability. The idea is elegant: compute a linear function of the inputs, then ''squash'' the result into the range [0, 1] using the sigmoid function. The output is a calibrated probability. !! The Sigmoid Function The sigmoid maps any real number to (0, 1): $\sigma(z) = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-z}}$ Near $z = 0$, the output is 0.5 — maximum uncertainty. As $z \to +\infty$, output approaches 1 (confident class 1). As $z \to -\infty$, output approaches 0 (confident class 0). The S-shape ensures smooth, differentiable transitions. The full model: $\hat{p} = \sigma(\mathbf{w}^\top \mathbf{x} + b)$ The learned weights $\mathbf{w}$ determine which features increase or decrease the predicted probability, and by how much. !! The Decision Boundary Classify as class 1 when $\hat{p} > 0.5$, which means $\mathbf{w}^\top \mathbf{x} + b > 0$. This defines a hyperplane in feature space — the ''decision boundary''. Everything on one side is predicted class 1, the other side class 0. Logistic regression is a ''linear'' classifier: its decision boundary is always a straight line (in 2D), plane (in 3D), or hyperplane. This is a strength (interpretable, stable) and a limitation (can't learn curved boundaries without feature engineering). !! Training via Maximum Likelihood We want to find weights that make the observed labels as probable as possible. For a single example, the contribution is $\hat{p}$ if $y=1$ or $(1-\hat{p})$ if $y=0$. The total log-likelihood over $n$ examples is: $\ell(\mathbf{w}) = \sum_{i=1}^n \left[ y_i \log\hat{p}_i + (1-y_i) \log(1-\hat{p}_i) \right]$ Maximizing this is equivalent to minimizing the binary cross-entropy loss. Unlike linear regression, there's no closed-form solution — we use gradient descent. !! Log-Odds Interpretation Logistic regression models the log-odds (logit) as linear in features: $\log\frac{\hat{p}}{1-\hat{p}} = \mathbf{w}^\top \mathbf{x} + b$ Each weight $w_j$ has a direct interpretation: a one-unit increase in feature $x_j$ multiplies the odds by $e^{w_j}$. A weight of 0.5 means the feature increases the odds by $e^{0.5} \approx 1.65\times$. This interpretability is one reason logistic regression remains the dominant model in medicine, economics, and any field where explainability matters. !! Multiclass Extension For $K > 2$ classes, use ''softmax regression'': learn one weight vector per class, compute unnormalized scores, normalize with softmax. This is the foundation of the final layer in most neural network classifiers. Regularization (L1 or L2) is almost always used to prevent overfitting and improve calibration. L1 produces sparse weights (feature selection); L2 keeps all features but shrinks them. !! Technical Details | Component | Formula | |-----------|---------| | Linear score | $z = \mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x} + b$ | | Predicted probability | $\hat{p} = 1/(1+e^{-z})$ | | Loss (binary CE) | $-[y\log\hat{p} + (1-y)\log(1-\hat{p})]$ | | Gradient w.r.t. $\mathbf{w}$ | $(\hat{p}-y)\mathbf{x}$ | | Decision boundary | $\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x} + b = 0$ | !! Quiz <<button "Q1: Why is logistic regression called 'regression' if it's a classifier?">><<replace "#lr-q1">>It regresses on the probability — modeling a continuous value (P(y=1|x) ∈ [0,1]) rather than a discrete label. The classification step (threshold at 0.5) comes after the probability is estimated.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lr-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: What does the sigmoid function do and why use it?">><<replace "#lr-q2">>It maps any real number to (0,1), converting an unbounded linear score into a valid probability. Its S-shape ensures smooth gradients everywhere, making it differentiable and suitable for gradient descent.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lr-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: Why is logistic regression's decision boundary always linear?">><<replace "#lr-q3">>The boundary is defined by w·x + b = 0, which is a linear equation in x. The sigmoid warps the output space but the boundary itself — where ŷ = 0.5 — is always a hyperplane in the feature space.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lr-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: How do you interpret a logistic regression weight?">><<replace "#lr-q4">>Each weight w_j represents the change in log-odds per unit increase in feature x_j. Equivalently, e^w_j is the multiplicative factor on the odds. Positive = increases probability; negative = decreases it.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lr-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: What loss function is minimized and why?">><<replace "#lr-q5">>Binary cross-entropy: −[y log ŷ + (1−y) log(1−ŷ)].
