Minimizing it is equivalent to maximizing the likelihood of the observed labels under the model — the standard maximum likelihood estimation framework.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lr-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Sequential Neural Networks A sequential (feedforward) neural network is the foundational deep learning architecture — layers of transformations stacked in a line, with information flowing in one direction from input to output. ''Architecture'' $$\mathbf{h}^{(l)} = \sigma\!\left(\mathbf{W}^{(l)}\mathbf{h}^{(l-1)} + \mathbf{b}^{(l)}\right)$$ Each layer applies a weight matrix $\mathbf{W}$, adds bias $\mathbf{b}$, then passes through activation $\sigma$. ''Layer types'' - ''Input layer'' — receives raw features - ''Hidden layers'' — learn intermediate representations - ''Output layer'' — produces predictions (sigmoid for binary, softmax for multiclass) ''Activations'' [[activations]] — ReLU is default for hidden layers; sigmoid/softmax for outputs [[ReLU]] — $f(x) = \max(0, x)$; avoids vanishing gradient, fast to compute ''Training'' [[Backpropogation and Chain Rule]] — how gradients flow backward through layers [[Stocastic Gradient Descent]] — the optimiser that updates weights [[Loss Functions]] — cross-entropy for classification, MSE for regression ''Failure modes'' [[vanishing gradient]] — gradients shrink to zero in deep networks; fixed by ReLU, batch norm [[exploding gradient]] — gradients grow uncontrollably; fixed by gradient clipping [[Overfitting]] — memorising training data; fixed by [[Regularization]], dropout ''When to use something else'' - Sequences (text, time series) → [[RNN]] / [[LSTM]] - Images → [[CNN]] - Long-range dependencies → [[Transformers]] --- [[Deep Learning]] ← back | [[Backpropogation and Chain Rule]] | [[activations]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Where to Eat Lunch — Decision Example'' A simple example illustrating decision-making under uncertainty, used to motivate reinforcement learning concepts. ''Setup'' Three nearby restaurants: A (reliable, always good), B (variable — occasionally great), C (unknown — never tried). This is the ''multi-armed bandit problem'': each restaurant is an "arm" with an unknown reward distribution. Do you exploit what you know (always go to A) or explore (try B or C)? ''Optimal Strategy'' * In the short run: go to A (pure exploit) * Over many lunches: explore to update estimates, then exploit the best UCB would compute: visit the restaurant with the highest (estimated quality + exploration bonus for being understudied). Thompson Sampling would: sample from your uncertainty about each restaurant's quality and visit the highest sample — naturally balancing explore/exploit. ''RL Lesson'' Simple daily decisions like "where to eat" share the same mathematical structure as complex control problems. The same algorithms (UCB, Thompson Sampling, $\varepsilon$-greedy) apply at every scale. [[Multi Armed Bandit]] | [[Explore and Exploit]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Adversarial Attacks'' Small, often imperceptible perturbations to input data that cause a model to make dramatically wrong predictions. ''Key Finding (Szegedy et al. 2013)'' A state-of-the-art image classifier could be fooled by adding tiny pixel noise invisible to humans but causing completely wrong classifications. ''Attack Types'' * ''[[Evasion attacks]]'' — perturb inputs at test time to cause misclassification * ''[[Poisoning attacks]]'' — corrupt training data to degrade or backdoor the model * ''[[Backdoor attacks]]'' — embed hidden triggers during training ''Fast Gradient Sign Method (FGSM)'' The simplest white-box evasion attack: $$ \mathbf{x}_{\text{adv}} = \mathbf{x} + \varepsilon \cdot \text{sign}(\nabla_{\mathbf{x}} \mathcal{L}) $$ Step in the direction that maximally increases loss, constrained by $\ell_\infty$ norm $\varepsilon$. ''PGD Attack (Madry et al.)'' Iterative version of FGSM — the strongest known first-order attack and standard benchmark. ''Defences'' * ''Adversarial training'': include adversarial examples in training set * ''Certified defences'': provable robustness within a norm ball * ''Input preprocessing'': denoising, feature squeezing * No perfect defence yet — adversarial robustness remains an open problem <<button "Quiz: How does FGSM compute the adversarial perturbation?">> <<replace "#adv-q">> ''✓ It takes the sign of the gradient of the loss w.r.t. the input: $\varepsilon \cdot \text{sign}(\nabla_{\mathbf{x}} \mathcal{L})$. This moves the input in the direction that maximally increases the model's loss.'' [[Evasion attacks]] | [[Poisoning attacks]] | [[Robustness]] | [[Deep Learning]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="adv-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Matrix Transformations'' A matrix $A \in \mathbb{R}^{m \times n}$ defines a linear map $T(\mathbf{x}) = A\mathbf{x}$ from $\mathbb{R}^n$ to $\mathbb{R}^m$. ''Fundamental Types in 2D'' | Name | Matrix | Effect | | Scale | $\begin{pmatrix}s_x&0\\0&s_y\end{pmatrix}$ | Stretch/compress axes | | Rotate (by $\theta$) | $\begin{pmatrix}\cos\theta&-\sin\theta\\\sin\theta&\cos\theta\end{pmatrix}$ | Rotate CCW | | Reflect (over x-axis) | $\begin{pmatrix}1&0\\0&-1\end{pmatrix}$ | Flip y-coordinate | | Shear | $\begin{pmatrix}1&k\\0&1\end{pmatrix}$ | Slide parallel to axis | | Project onto x-axis | $\begin{pmatrix}1&0\\0&0\end{pmatrix}$ | Collapse y | ''Composition'' Applying $T_1$ then $T_2$: $T_2(T_1(\mathbf{x})) = (A_2 A_1)\mathbf{x}$. Matrix multiplication is composition. Order matters. ''Geometric Properties'' * $\det(A) > 0$: orientation preserved * $\det(A) = 0$: rank-deficient; collapses a dimension * $A^\top A = I$: isometry (distance-preserving rotation or reflection) ''Deep Learning Connection'' Each layer of a neural network applies a learned linear transformation (weight matrix) followed by a nonlinearity — stacking learned geometric transformations. <<button "Quiz: What geometric transformation does a rotation matrix perform?">> <<replace "#mt-q">> ''✓ It rotates vectors by angle $\theta$ counter-clockwise without changing their length — a distance-preserving (isometric) transformation with $\det = 1$.'' [[Eiganvectors and Eiganvalue]] | [[Change of Basis]] | [[Core Linear Algebra]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="mt-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Shear Transformation'' Slides one axis parallel to the other — like pushing the top of a parallelogram sideways while keeping the base fixed. 2D horizontal shear by factor $k$: $$ \begin{pmatrix}1 & k \\ 0 & 1\end{pmatrix}\begin{pmatrix}x\\y\end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix}x+ky\\y\end{pmatrix} $$ $\det = 1$: area is preserved, orientation unchanged. The transformation is invertible but not orthogonal — it distorts angles. ''Appearance in ML'': data augmentation for images (slight perspective distort); CSS transforms; projection geometry in computer vision. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Scaling / Stretching'' A diagonal scaling matrix stretches or compresses each axis independently: $$ S = \begin{pmatrix}s_x & 0 \\ 0 & s_y\end{pmatrix} $$ $|s| > 1$: stretches; $0 < |s| < 1$: compresses; $s < 0$: also reflects. $\det(S) = s_x s_y$ — the determinant is the signed volume scaling factor. ''Uniform scaling'' ($s_x = s_y$): preserves shape (circles stay circles). ''Non-uniform scaling'' distorts aspect ratios. ''ML uses'': feature normalisation is equivalent to scaling; convolutional strides implicitly scale spatial resolution; image resizing. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Squeeze Mapping'' A special area-preserving scaling where one axis is stretched and the other is compressed by the inverse factor: $$ \begin{pmatrix}k & 0 \\ 0 & 1/k\end{pmatrix}, \quad \det = 1 $$ The product of the scaling factors is 1 — volume (area in 2D) is conserved. Hyperbolas $xy = c$ are invariant under squeeze maps. ''Connection to SVD'': the diagonal matrix $\Sigma$ in $A = U\Sigma V^\top$ encodes axis-aligned stretches (by singular values) which may include squeezes. ''Normalising flows'': volume-preserving transformations are attractive because $\log|\det J| = 0$, simplifying the change-of-variables formula. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Rotation Matrix'' Rotates vectors by angle $\theta$ counter-clockwise without changing length or handedness: $$ R(\theta) = \begin{pmatrix}\cos\theta & -\sin\theta \\ \sin\theta & \cos\theta\end{pmatrix} $$ Key properties: $R^\top R = I$ (orthogonal), $\det(R) = 1$, $R(-\theta) = R(\theta)^{-1} = R(\theta)^\top$. Rotations preserve inner products and distances — they are isometries. In 3D, rotations are represented by $3\times3$ orthogonal matrices or quaternions (more numerically stable for composition). ''ML uses'': data augmentation, pose estimation, equivariant networks, 3D scene understanding. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Reflection Matrix'' Reflects vectors across a line (2D) or plane (3D). Reflection across the x-axis: $$ \begin{pmatrix}1 & 0 \\ 0 & -1\end{pmatrix} $$ Reflection across a unit vector $\hat{\mathbf{n}}$ (Householder): $$ H = I - 2\hat{\mathbf{n}}\hat{\mathbf{n}}^\top $$ Properties: $H^\top = H$, $H^2 = I$, $\det(H) = -1$ (flips orientation). Householder reflections are the building blocks of numerically stable QR decomposition. ''ML uses'': data augmentation (horizontal/vertical flip), Householder-based orthogonalisation, normalising flows. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Euler's Formula'' One of the most beautiful identities in mathematics, linking exponential functions and trigonometry: $$ e^{i\theta} = \cos\theta + i\sin\theta $$ ''Euler's Identity'' (special case $\theta = \pi$): $$ e^{i\pi} + 1 = 0 $$ ''Derivation'' From Taylor series: the even terms of $e^{i\theta}$ give $\cos\theta$ and the odd terms give $i\sin\theta$. ''Geometric Interpretation'' $e^{i\theta}$ traces the unit circle in the complex plane. Multiplying by $e^{i\theta}$ rotates a complex number by $\theta$ radians. ''Applications in ML/Signal Processing'' * ''Fourier Transform'': $\hat{f}(\omega) = \int f(t) e^{-i\omega t} dt$ — decomposes signals into frequency components * ''Rotary Position Embeddings (RoPE)'': use complex rotations to encode position in LLMs * ''Spectral methods'': eigenvectors of graph Laplacian as generalised Fourier modes * Wave equations, oscillator dynamics, quantum mechanics <<button "Quiz: What does multiplying a complex number by $e^{i\theta}$ do geometrically?">> <<replace "#ef-q">> ''✓ It rotates the complex number by $\theta$ radians counterclockwise in the complex plane without changing its magnitude.'' [[FFT]] | [[Core Linear Algebra]] | [[Branches of Mathmatics]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="ef-q"></span> <<include "Footer">>[[Multi Armed Bandit]] [[A/B Testing]] [[Q Learning]] ` [[Rich Sutton]] [[SARSA]] [[Actor-critic]] [[Proximal Policy Optimization ]] [[policy gradiant]] [[bellman equation]] [[Explore and Exploit]] [[Markov Decision Process]]<<include "Header">> ## Multi-Armed Bandit The multi-armed bandit is the simplest reinforcement learning problem: no state, just repeated choices between actions with unknown reward distributions. The challenge is balancing exploration (learning about arms) with exploitation (pulling the best known arm). ''The problem'' You have $K$ arms. At each round, pull one arm and observe a reward drawn from that arm's unknown distribution. Goal: maximise cumulative reward over $T$ rounds. ''Regret'' Regret measures how much you lose by not always pulling the optimal arm: $$\text{Regret}(T) = T \mu^* - \sum_{t=1}^T \mu_{A_t}$$ A good algorithm achieves sub-linear regret — the per-round loss decreases over time. ''Algorithms'' - ''$\epsilon$-greedy'' — with probability $\epsilon$ explore randomly; otherwise exploit the best arm. Simple but not optimal. - ''UCB (Upper Confidence Bound)'' — pull the arm with the highest upper confidence bound: $\hat{\mu}_k + \sqrt{\frac{2\ln t}{n_k}}$. Principled exploration. - ''Thompson Sampling'' — maintain a posterior over each arm's mean; sample from the posterior and pull the arm with the highest sample. Bayesian, often best in practice. ''Connections'' [[Explore and Exploit]] — the core tension; formalized here [[A/B Testing]] — a bandit framing of the classical A/B test [[Reinforcement Learning]] — bandits are stateless RL; full RL adds state transitions [[Bayesian Methods]] — Thompson sampling is a Bayesian bandit algorithm --- [[Reinforcement Learning]] ← back | [[Explore and Exploit]] | [[A/B Testing]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''A/B Testing'' A randomised controlled experiment comparing two variants (A = control, B = treatment) to determine which performs better on a measured outcome. ''Structure'' 1. Randomly assign users to group A or group B 2. Run both versions simultaneously 3. Measure the outcome metric (click rate, conversion, revenue) 4. Test statistical significance: is the observed difference due to the treatment or chance? ''Test Statistic'' For proportions (e.g. click-through rate): $$ z = \frac{\hat{p}_B - \hat{p}_A}{\sqrt{\hat{p}(1-\hat{p})(1/n_A + 1/n_B)}} $$ ''Key Concepts'' * ''Type I error'' ($\alpha$): false positive — detecting a difference that doesn't exist (typically 5%) * ''Type II error'' ($\beta$): false negative — missing a real difference * ''Statistical power'' ($1-\beta$): probability of detecting a real effect (typically 80%) * ''Sample size'': calculate before running — determines how long to run the test ''Common Pitfalls'' * ''Peeking'': stopping early when significant — inflates Type I error * ''Multiple comparisons'': testing many metrics simultaneously (use Bonferroni correction) * ''Simpson's paradox'': overall result can reverse when sliced by subgroups * ''Novelty effect'': users behave differently to new UI only temporarily <<button "Quiz: Why is 'peeking' at results during an A/B test problematic?">> <<replace "#ab-q">> ''✓ Checking significance repeatedly and stopping when $p < 0.05$ inflates the false positive rate far above 5%. Sequential testing methods (e.g. sequential probability ratio test) handle this properly.'' [[hypothesis test]] | [[null hypothesis]] | [[P-Value]] | [[Multi Armed Bandit]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="ab-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Deep Q-Network (DQN)'' DQN (Mnih et al., DeepMind 2013/2015) was the first algorithm to learn human-level control across dozens of Atari games directly from pixels — combining deep learning with Q-learning. ''Core Idea'' Replace the Q-table (infeasible for large state spaces) with a deep neural network $Q(s, a; \theta)$ that approximates Q-values. ''Two Critical Innovations'' 1. ''Experience Replay'': store transitions $(s,a,r,s')$ in a replay buffer; sample random mini-batches to break temporal correlations. 2. ''Target Network'': a separate, periodically-frozen copy $\theta^-$ of the network for computing TD targets, stabilising training: $$ y = r + \gamma \max_{a'} Q(s', a'; \theta^-) $$ Loss: $\mathcal{L} = \left(y - Q(s,a;\theta)\right)^2$ ''Why These Innovations Matter'' * Without replay: consecutive samples are highly correlated → unstable training * Without target network: both the prediction and target move simultaneously → divergence ''Extensions'' * ''Double DQN'': decouple action selection from evaluation to reduce overestimation * ''Dueling DQN'': separate streams for state value $V(s)$ and advantage $A(s,a)$ * ''Prioritised Experience Replay'': sample transitions with higher TD error more frequently * ''Rainbow'': combines all improvements <<button "Quiz: Why does DQN use a separate target network?">> <<replace "#dqn-q">> ''✓ The target $y = r + \gamma\max_{a'} Q(s',a';\theta^-)$ must be stable while the network trains. Using the same parameters for both target and prediction causes both to move simultaneously — like chasing a moving target, leading to divergence.'' [[Q Learning]] | [[bellman equation]] | [[Reinforcement Learning]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="dqn-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> [[Shear Transformation]] [[Streching]] [[Squeezing]] [[Rotation]] [[Reflection]] [[Translation]] [[Scaling]] [[projection]] [[matrix inverse]] https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transformation_matrix <<include "Header">> ''Q-Learning: Code Sketch'' Core update loop for tabular Q-learning (e.g. GridWorld): <pre> import numpy as np # Q-table: states x actions Q = np.zeros((n_states, n_actions)) alpha, gamma, epsilon = 0.1, 0.99, 0.1 for episode in range(N_EPISODES): s = env.reset() done = False while not done: # epsilon-greedy action selection if np.random.rand() < epsilon: a = env.action_space.sample() else: a = np.argmax(Q[s]) s_next, r, done, _ = env.step(a) # Bellman update td_target = r + gamma * np.max(Q[s_next]) Q[s, a] += alpha * (td_target - Q[s, a]) s = s_next </pre> ''Key hyperparameters'' * $\alpha$ (learning rate): how fast Q-values update * $\gamma$ (discount): how much future rewards are valued * $\varepsilon$ (exploration rate): often decayed over training See [[Q Learning]] for theory. For deep version see [[Deep Q Learning]]. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> <img src="assets/gan.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="GAN architecture diagram"/> ! Generative Adversarial Networks !! The Adversarial Idea Most ML models are discriminative: they learn $P(y|\mathbf{x})$ — given an input, what's the output? GANs (Goodfellow et al., 2014) take a different aim: learn the data distribution itself, $P(\mathbf{x})$, so you can ''generate'' new samples indistinguishable from real ones. The mechanism is a competition. Two networks train simultaneously: a ''generator'' that tries to produce convincing fakes, and a ''discriminator'' that tries to catch them. Each improves by trying to defeat the other. The generator gets better at fooling; the discriminator gets better at detecting. In the limit, the generator produces samples the discriminator can't distinguish from real data. !! The Minimax Game Formally, the two players optimize opposing objectives: $\min_G \max_D \; \mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x} \sim p_\text{data}}[\log D(\mathbf{x})] + \mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{z} \sim p_z}[\log(1 - D(G(\mathbf{z})))]$ The discriminator $D$ wants to output 1 for real data and 0 for generated data — maximizing this expression. The generator $G$ maps random noise $\mathbf{z}$ to a sample and wants $D(G(\mathbf{z}))$ to be close to 1 — fooling the discriminator into calling its output real. At the Nash equilibrium, $G$ perfectly reproduces $p_\text{data}$ and $D$ outputs 0.5 everywhere — it can't do better than random guessing. !! Training in Practice Training is notoriously unstable. The two networks must stay roughly balanced: if the discriminator is too strong, it gives the generator no useful gradient to learn from. If the generator is too strong, the discriminator can't catch up and the generator produces a single "convincing" output (mode collapse). Key techniques: ''gradient penalty'' (WGAN-GP) for stable training; ''spectral normalization'' to control discriminator capacity; ''progressive growing'' (ProGAN) — start with low-resolution images and gradually increase resolution; ''label smoothing'' on discriminator targets. Training is monitored by loss curves and visual inspection. Quantitative metrics: ''Fréchet Inception Distance (FID)'' measures similarity between the distribution of generated images and real images using a pre-trained feature extractor. Lower FID = better. !! Variants and Applications ''DCGAN'': deep convolutional GAN for images, one of the first stable architectures. ''Conditional GAN (cGAN)'': condition both generator and discriminator on a class label or other signal — generate images of a specific class, translate images between domains. ''Pix2Pix'': image-to-image translation — satellite photo to map, sketch to photo, black-and-white to color. ''CycleGAN'': translate between unpaired domains (horses↔zebras, summer↔winter) without corresponding image pairs. ''StyleGAN'': the architecture behind photorealistic face generation; separates high-level style (identity, pose) from fine detail. !! Why GANs Matter GANs demonstrated that neural networks can learn to produce high-quality, novel content — not just classify existing content. This shifted the field's understanding of what learning a distribution means, and opened the era of generative AI. The GAN framework also introduced adversarial training as a general principle, now used in domain adaptation, data augmentation, and robustness research. Diffusion models have since surpassed GANs for image quality and training stability, but GANs remain influential and widely used in video, audio, and domains where fast sampling matters. !! Technical Details | Component | Role | |-----------|------| | Generator $G$ | Maps noise $\mathbf{z} \sim \mathcal{N}(0,I)$ → fake samples | | Discriminator $D$ | Outputs $P(\text{real})$ for input | | Nash equilibrium | $p_G = p_\text{data}$, $D(\mathbf{x}) = 0.5$ everywhere | | FID | Distance between real/fake feature distributions | | Mode collapse | Generator produces limited variety | !! Quiz <<button "Q1: What is the Nash equilibrium of GAN training?">><<replace "#gan-q1">>The generator perfectly reproduces the real data distribution (p_G = p_data), and the discriminator outputs 0.5 everywhere — it can do no better than random guessing because real and fake samples are indistinguishable.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="gan-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: What is mode collapse and why does it happen?">><<replace "#gan-q2">>Mode collapse is when the generator learns to produce only a few types of outputs that fool the discriminator, ignoring the full diversity of the real distribution. It happens when the generator finds a "safe" output the discriminator consistently accepts and stops exploring.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="gan-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: Why can't you just train the generator to minimize cross-entropy loss directly?">><<replace "#gan-q3">>The generator has no direct access to the true data distribution to compute a loss against it. The discriminator acts as a learned, adaptive loss function that tells the generator which directions to improve — something a fixed loss cannot do.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="gan-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: What does FID measure and why is it useful?">><<replace "#gan-q4">>Fréchet Inception Distance measures the distance between the distribution of real images and generated images in feature space (using a pre-trained InceptionNet). Lower FID means generated images are more realistic and diverse. It correlates well with human perception of quality.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="gan-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: How does a conditional GAN differ from a standard GAN?">><<replace "#gan-q5">>A conditional GAN feeds a conditioning signal (class label, text, another image) to both the generator and discriminator. The generator learns to produce samples matching the condition; the discriminator learns to detect fake samples for that specific condition. This enables controlled generation.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="gan-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">>Lower Dimensional Representation is a foundational concept in various fields of advanced study, such as Machine Learning, Data Analysis, and Pattern Recognition. In high-dimensional data, which is frequently encountered in these fields, the relationships and patterns can often be difficult to discern due to the so-called "curse of dimensionality". This refers to the phenomenon where the volume of the data space grows exponentially with the increase in dimensions, making the data increasingly sparse and the computational complexity prohibitive. Lower Dimensional Representation is a technique employed to combat these issues. It aims to simplify the data by reducing its dimensionality, hence transforming the data into a domain where relevant information is more easily extracted and interpreted. This is achieved while preserving critical properties, such as the relative distances between data points or the topology of the data manifold. Numerous algorithms and methods have been developed for dimensionality reduction, including Principal Component Analysis (PCA), t-Distributed Stochastic Neighbor Embedding (t-SNE), and Autoencoders, among others. Each of these has unique assumptions, strengths, and weaknesses, and their deployment depends on the nature of the data and the task at hand. Through effective application of Lower Dimensional Representation techniques, researchers can perform efficient data analysis and develop powerful predictive models, greatly enhancing the capabilities of machines to understand and learn complex data patterns - a fundamental aim of artificial intelligence. <<include "Header">> ''The Exploration-Exploitation Trade-off'' A fundamental dilemma in reinforcement learning and decision-making: should the agent use its current best knowledge (exploit) or gather new information (explore)? ''The Tension'' * ''Exploit'': choose the action with the highest current estimated reward → maximise short-term performance * ''Explore'': choose a less certain action → possibly discover a better option Always exploiting = greedy; may get stuck in a local optimum. Always exploring = never benefits from past learning. ''Strategies'' * $\varepsilon$-greedy: exploit with probability $1-\varepsilon$, random with $\varepsilon$ * ''UCB (Upper Confidence Bound)'': add exploration bonus $\sqrt{\frac{2\ln t}{n_a}}$ to reward estimate * ''Thompson Sampling'': maintain a posterior over rewards; sample and act greedily * ''Softmax/Boltzmann'': sample action $a$ with probability $\propto e^{Q(a)/\tau}$ ''UCB Intuition'' $$ \text{Select}: \arg\max_a \left[ \hat{\mu}_a + c\sqrt{\frac{\ln t}{n_a}} \right] $$ Actions tried rarely get a large bonus — forcing exploration of uncertain options. ''Regret'' Cumulative regret measures total reward lost by not always taking the optimal action. Theoretically optimal algorithms (UCB, Thompson) achieve $O(\sqrt{T\log T})$ regret. <<button "Quiz: What is the key problem with always being greedy ($\varepsilon=0$)?">> <<replace "#ee-q">> ''✓ Pure exploitation converges to the first action that looks good — never discovering potentially better options. It gets stuck at a suboptimal choice because it never explores.'' [[Multi Armed Bandit]] | [[Q Learning]] | [[Reinforcement Learning]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="ee-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Linear Programming (LP)'' Optimise a linear objective subject to linear constraints: $$ \min_{\mathbf{x}}\; \mathbf{c}^\top \mathbf{x} \quad \text{s.t.} \quad A\mathbf{x} \leq \mathbf{b},\; \mathbf{x} \geq 0 $$ ''Geometric Interpretation'' The feasible region is a ''convex polytope'' (intersection of half-spaces). The objective level sets are parallel hyperplanes. The optimum always occurs at a ''vertex'' (corner) of the polytope. ''Simplex Method'' Traverses vertices of the polytope along improving edges. Worst case exponential, but extremely fast in practice. ''Interior Point Methods'' Solve LP in polynomial time $O(n^{3.5})$ by following a path through the interior of the feasible region. Preferred for large-scale problems. ''Strong Duality'' LP always has strong duality (when feasible and bounded). The dual solution provides ''shadow prices'' — value of relaxing each constraint by one unit. ''ML Applications'' * L1-regularised regression (Lasso) can be reformulated as LP * Margin-based classifiers (SVMs with hinge loss via quadratic programming) * Transportation / assignment problems in data pipelines * Fairness constraints in ML * Markov Decision Processes (policy computation) <<button "Quiz: Why does the LP optimum always occur at a vertex?">> <<replace "#lp-q">> ''✓ The objective is linear over a convex polytope. Any interior point can be improved by moving in the gradient direction until hitting a boundary. This process terminates at a vertex.'' [[Convex Optimization]] | [[Lagrangian Duality]] | [[Optimization]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="lp-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Estimating Model Performance'' Getting an honest estimate of how well your model will perform on unseen data is one of the most practically important problems in ML. ''The Fundamental Problem'' Training error is always an optimistic estimate of generalisation error — the model has seen the training data. You need held-out data that played no role in model development. ''Methods'' * ''Single train/val/test split'': fast; noisy estimate for small datasets * ''K-fold CV'': lower variance estimate; averages over K splits * ''Nested CV'': outer CV for performance estimate, inner CV for hyperparameter selection — avoids double-dipping * ''Bootstrap'': resample with replacement; useful for confidence intervals ''Pitfalls that inflate performance estimates'' | Pitfall | Description | | Data leakage | Future data or target used as feature | | Test set reuse | Tuning on test set across experiments | | Improper normalisation | Fit scaler on all data before splitting | | Non-i.i.d. splits | Shuffling time series; related samples in both sets | ''Reporting Results'' Report mean ± standard deviation across folds. Report confidence intervals on the final test metric. Specify the exact evaluation protocol so results are reproducible. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ! Cross-Validation <img src="assets/cross_validation.svg" alt="5-fold cross-validation — rotating held-out validation fold" style="width:100%;max-width:620px;display:block;margin:16px auto 24px auto;"> You've trained a model. How good is it? You can measure training error, but that's biased — the model has already seen that data. You need to estimate how well it generalises to data it has never seen. Cross-validation is the principled way to do that without wasting data on a permanent held-out set. --- !! The Core Problem If you evaluate on the same data you trained on, you get an optimistically biased estimate — the model has memorised that data to some degree. If you set aside a fixed test set, you're throwing away data that could have improved the model. Cross-validation resolves this by rotating which data is held out. --- !! K-Fold Cross-Validation Split the dataset into $k$ equal folds. Train $k$ separate models, each time using $k-1$ folds for training and holding out one fold for validation. Average the $k$ validation scores. Every data point is used for validation exactly once, and for training $k-1$ times. The result is a nearly unbiased estimate of generalisation error that uses all available data. Typical choice: $k = 5$ or $k = 10$. Larger $k$ gives a less biased estimate but more variance (and more compute). $k = n$ — using one example at a time as validation — is ''Leave-One-Out Cross-Validation (LOOCV)'', which is nearly unbiased but expensive and high-variance. --- !! What Cross-Validation Is Actually For Cross-validation estimates generalisation error. Its main uses are: ''Model selection'' — compare different architectures, feature sets, or algorithms. The one with the best CV score is likely to generalise best. ''Hyperparameter tuning'' — grid-search or random-search over hyperparameter settings, using CV score to pick the winner. ''Performance reporting'' — give an honest estimate of how well your final model will perform on new data. A common mistake: tuning hyperparameters using the test set. This leaks information and makes the test set useless as an honest estimate. The test set should be touched exactly once, at the very end. --- !! Stratified Cross-Validation In classification with imbalanced classes, a random fold might end up with very few examples of a rare class — making the fold's evaluation noisy or misleading. ''Stratified k-fold'' preserves the class proportions in each fold, ensuring every fold is a fair representative sample of the full dataset. --- !! The Train / Validation / Test Split In practice, data is split into three sets: * ''Training set'' — the model is fitted on this * ''Validation set'' (or CV folds) — used to tune hyperparameters and select models * ''Test set'' — touched once at the very end for final honest evaluation Cross-validation replaces the fixed validation set with rotating folds, making better use of limited data. The test set remains fixed and untouched throughout. --- !! Technical Details ''Bias-variance of CV:'' $k$-fold CV has lower bias than a single train/val split (more data used for training) but higher variance than a very large validation set. 5 or 10 folds is a practical sweet spot. ''Time series warning:'' for sequential data, standard k-fold is invalid — future data leaks into training. Use ''time-series split'' (train on past, validate on future) to preserve temporal order. ''Nested CV:'' when both model selection and hyperparameter tuning happen simultaneously, use nested CV — outer loop for model selection, inner loop for hyperparameter search — to avoid optimistic bias. ''Computational cost:'' $k$-fold requires training $k$ models. For expensive models, this is prohibitive. Cheaper alternatives: single train/val split, or use a validation set and rely on theoretical guarantees. --- !! Quiz ''1. Why is training error a biased estimate of generalisation error?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#cv-q1">>'✓ The model is optimised to minimise training error — it has adapted to the specific noise and patterns in the training set. Evaluating on training data measures how well the model memorised, not how well it learned. Even a model that memorises every training point (zero training error) can fail badly on new data.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="cv-q1"></span> ''2. In 5-fold CV, how many times is each data point used for training? For validation?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#cv-q2">>'✓ Each data point is used for training in 4 of the 5 folds, and for validation in exactly 1 fold. This is the key property of k-fold CV: every point contributes to training most of the time, and every point gets evaluated exactly once without the model having seen it during that fold's training.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="cv-q2"></span> ''3. Why should the test set be touched only once?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#cv-q3">>'✓ Every time you look at test performance and adjust your model in response, you implicitly optimise for the test set. After enough rounds of this, you have effectively trained on the test set — it no longer gives an honest estimate of generalisation. Reserve the test set for a single final evaluation after all decisions are made.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="cv-q3"></span> ''4. What is stratified k-fold and when do you need it?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#cv-q4">>'✓ Stratified k-fold ensures each fold has the same class proportions as the full dataset. Without it, random splits might put all examples of a rare class into one fold, making that fold's evaluation unreliable (or the validation set empty for that class). Use it whenever your classes are imbalanced — which is most real-world classification problems.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="cv-q4"></span> ''5. Why can't you use standard k-fold CV on time-series data?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#cv-q5">>'✓ Standard k-fold randomly assigns data to folds, so training folds can contain data from the future relative to the validation fold. This is data leakage — the model effectively sees the future during training, giving an optimistically biased CV score. Time-series split always trains on the past and validates on the future, respecting the temporal structure.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="cv-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Transformers'' "Attention Is All You Need" (Vaswani et al., 2017) introduced the Transformer — an architecture that replaced recurrence with pure attention mechanisms and became the backbone of essentially all modern language, vision, and multimodal models. --- ''Why Not RNNs?'' RNNs process sequences token-by-token. Problems: * Cannot parallelise training over sequence positions * Vanishing gradients over long contexts * Information must "flow" through a bottleneck hidden state Transformers fix all three by allowing every token to attend directly to every other token. --- ''Architecture Overview'' An encoder-decoder Transformer (for translation): <pre> Input → [Embed + Positional Encoding] ↓ ┌─────────────────────────┐ │ Encoder Block × N │ │ ┌──────────────────┐ │ │ │ Multi-Head Attn │ │ │ │ + Residual + LN │ │ │ ├──────────────────┤ │ │ │ Feed-Forward │ │ │ │ + Residual + LN │ │ │ └──────────────────┘ │ └─────────────────────────┘ ↓ (encoder output) ┌─────────────────────────┐ │ Decoder Block × N │ │ Masked Self-Attn │ │ Cross-Attention │ │ Feed-Forward │ └─────────────────────────┘ ↓ Linear + Softmax → Token probabilities </pre> ''Encoder-only'' (BERT): bidirectional; for understanding tasks. ''Decoder-only'' (GPT): causal; for generation. ''Encoder-decoder'' (T5, original Transformer): seq2seq. --- ''Scaled Dot-Product Attention'' $$ \text{Attn}(Q,K,V) = \text{softmax}\!\left(\frac{QK^\top}{\sqrt{d_k}}\right)V $$ For a sequence of $n$ tokens, each with embedding dimension $d$: * Project into queries $Q = XW^Q$, keys $K = XW^K$, values $V = XW^V$ ($\in \mathbb{R}^{n \times d_k}$) * Compute $n \times n$ attention scores: which tokens should attend to which? * Weight the values by the scores ''Intuition'': each token asks a ''query'', every token broadcasts a ''key''; the dot product scores compatibility; the output is a weighted average of ''values''. --- ''Multi-Head Attention'' Run $h$ attention heads in parallel with different learned projections: $$ \text{MultiHead}(X) = \text{Concat}(\text{head}_1,\ldots,\text{head}_h) W^O $$ $$ \text{head}_i = \text{Attn}(XW_i^Q,\, XW_i^K,\, XW_i^V) $$ Each head specialises: one head might track syntactic dependencies; another co-reference; another semantic similarity. In practice, heads are often hard to interpret but collectively achieve rich representations. --- ''Positional Encoding'' Attention is permutation-equivariant — without position information, "the dog bit the man" and "the man bit the dog" look identical. Positional encodings break this symmetry. ''Sinusoidal (original)'': $$ PE_{(pos, 2i)} = \sin\!\left(\frac{pos}{10000^{2i/d}}\right), \quad PE_{(pos, 2i+1)} = \cos\!\left(\frac{pos}{10000^{2i/d}}\right) $$ ''Learned absolute positions'' (BERT, GPT-2): a lookup table trained end-to-end. ''Relative positions'' (RoPE, ALiBi): encode relative distance rather than absolute position — better extrapolation to longer sequences than seen in training. --- ''Feed-Forward Sublayer'' After attention, each position independently goes through a two-layer MLP: $$ \text{FFN}(x) = \max(0, xW_1 + b_1)W_2 + b_2 $$ Typically $d_{\text{ff}} = 4d$ (e.g. $d=512, d_{\text{ff}}=2048$). This is where most of the parameters live — and recent interpretability work suggests the FFN stores factual knowledge as "key-value memories." --- ''Residual Connections + Layer Norm'' Each sublayer is wrapped: $$ x \leftarrow \text{LayerNorm}(x + \text{Sublayer}(x)) $$ Residual connections: gradient highways that prevent vanishing gradients in 100+ layer networks. Layer norm: normalises each token's feature vector independently — unlike batch norm, works correctly on variable-length sequences. --- ''Computational Complexity'' | Component | Time | Space | | Self-attention | $O(n^2 d)$ | $O(n^2)$ | | FFN | $O(n d^2)$ | $O(d^2)$ | The $O(n^2)$ attention is the bottleneck for long sequences. Sparse attention (Longformer), linear attention (Performer), and sliding window methods address this. --- ''Key Variants'' * ''BERT'' (Google 2018): encoder-only, masked language model pretraining * ''GPT family'' (OpenAI): decoder-only, autoregressive * ''T5'' (Google): encoder-decoder, text-to-text * ''Vision Transformer (ViT)'': patches as tokens, applied to images * ''Llama, Mistral'': decoder-only with RoPE, grouped-query attention, SwiGLU FFN <<button "Quiz: Why is $\sqrt{d_k}$ used to scale the dot products?">> <<replace "#tr-q">> ''✓ As $d_k$ grows, dot products grow in variance (sum of $d_k$ products of unit Gaussians has variance $d_k$). Without scaling, softmax saturates — one dimension dominates and gradients vanish. Dividing by $\sqrt{d_k}$ keeps variance at 1.'' <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="tr-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> <img src="assets/embeddings.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="Word embeddings diagram"/> ! Embeddings !! The Discrete-to-Continuous Problem Computers are comfortable with numbers. But language, user identities, product categories, graph nodes — these are ''discrete symbols'' with no natural numeric representation. How do you feed the word "king" into a neural network? The naive solution is one-hot encoding: a vector of zeros with a single 1 at the word's index. A vocabulary of 50,000 words → 50,000-dimensional sparse vectors. Two problems: (1) this is computationally wasteful; (2) every word is equally distant from every other — the representation captures no semantic relationship. Embeddings solve both problems by mapping each symbol to a dense, low-dimensional vector learned from data. !! What Geometry Encodes Meaning The magic of embeddings is that the learned vector space reflects semantic relationships. Words with similar meanings cluster together. Relationships between words correspond to directions in the space. The classic demonstration: ''king'' − ''man'' + ''woman'' ≈ ''queen''. The direction from "man" to "king" is roughly the same as the direction from "woman" to "queen" — an axis encoding royalty. Similarly, Paris − France + Italy ≈ Rome encodes the capital-of relationship. This happens because the embedding is trained to predict context. Words that appear in similar contexts get pushed toward similar vectors. "Dog" and "cat" appear near "pet", "vet", "food" — so they end up close together, even if they never appear together. !! Word2Vec: Learning from Context Word2Vec (Mikolov et al., 2013) introduced efficient methods for learning word embeddings. The ''Skip-gram'' model trains a shallow network to predict which words appear in the context window around a target word. The ''CBOW'' (Continuous Bag of Words) model does the reverse: predict the center word from its context. Neither task is the real goal — they're pretexts. The embeddings are the byproduct: the learned weight matrix is the embedding table. Each word's row is its embedding vector. After training on billions of words, the 300-dimensional vectors capture syntax (noun vs. verb), semantics (animal, royalty, geography), and even sentiment. !! Beyond Words: Universal Embeddings The idea generalizes far beyond language. ''Item embeddings'' in recommendation systems map products or movies to vectors where proximity predicts co-engagement. ''User embeddings'' place users in the same space — a user vector near a cluster of action-movie vectors suggests they'll enjoy action movies. ''Graph embeddings'' (Node2Vec, DeepWalk) generate random walks through a graph, then apply Word2Vec-style training — neighboring nodes in the graph end up close in vector space. ''Learned embeddings'' in neural networks treat any categorical feature (zip codes, user IDs, product categories) as a lookup into a trainable embedding table. !! Modern Contextual Embeddings Word2Vec gives each word a ''single'' vector regardless of context. "Bank" has one vector whether it means a riverbank or a financial institution. Transformers (BERT, GPT) produce ''contextual embeddings'' — the representation of each word depends on all surrounding words. "Bank" near "river" gets a different vector than "bank" near "loan". These contextual representations are the foundation of modern NLP. Pre-training a large transformer on massive text corpora, then fine-tuning on a small task-specific dataset, has become the dominant paradigm. !! Technical Details | Method | Type | Key idea | |--------|------|----------| | Word2Vec skip-gram | Static | Predict context from center | | Word2Vec CBOW | Static | Predict center from context | | GloVe | Static | Factorize co-occurrence matrix | | FastText | Static | Subword embeddings (handles OOV) | | BERT | Contextual | Bidirectional transformer | | GPT | Contextual | Autoregressive transformer | Embedding dimension is a hyperparameter: 50–300 for static; 768–4096 for transformer hidden states. !! Quiz <<button "Q1: Why is one-hot encoding a poor representation for words?">><<replace "#emb-q1">>It's extremely high-dimensional (one dimension per word), sparse, and treats all words as equally dissimilar — no notion of "dog" and "cat" being more similar to each other than to "democracy."<</replace>><</button>> <span id="emb-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: What does it mean for 'king − man + woman ≈ queen'?">><<replace "#emb-q2">>The embedding space encodes semantic relationships as geometric directions. The direction from man→king (royalty) is approximately the same as woman→queen. Arithmetic on vectors corresponds to semantic analogies.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="emb-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: How does Word2Vec learn embeddings without explicit labels?">><<replace "#emb-q3">>It uses a self-supervised pretext task: predict nearby words from a center word (skip-gram) or vice versa. The embedding vectors are the learned weights — semantic structure emerges as a byproduct of predicting context.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="emb-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: What is the key difference between static and contextual embeddings?">><<replace "#emb-q4">>Static embeddings (Word2Vec, GloVe) give each word one fixed vector. Contextual embeddings (BERT, GPT) produce different vectors for the same word depending on its surrounding context — handling polysemy naturally.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="emb-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: Why do recommendation systems use embeddings?">><<replace "#emb-q5">>Embedding users and items in the same vector space allows similarity to drive recommendations. A user vector near action-movie vectors predicts action-movie preference. Dot products or cosine similarity in embedding space approximate interaction probability.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="emb-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Word Embeddings'' A word embedding maps a discrete token to a dense real-valued vector in $\mathbb{R}^d$ ($d$ is typically 50–1024). ''Why Dense Vectors?'' One-hot encoding gives vectors of size $|V|$ (vocabulary) with a single 1. Problems: * No notion of similarity — $\cos(\mathbf{cat}, \mathbf{dog}) = 0$ * High dimensional and sparse Dense embeddings fix both: ''king − man + woman ≈ queen'' ''Training Approaches'' | Method | Idea | | ''Word2Vec (skip-gram)'' | Predict surrounding words from centre | | ''GloVe'' | Factorise word co-occurrence matrix | | ''FastText'' | Word2Vec + subword n-grams | | ''ELMo'' | Context-sensitive via biLSTM | | ''BERT/GPT'' | Transformer; embeddings are layer outputs | ''Geometric Properties'' * Semantic similarity → cosine similarity * Analogy arithmetic works for gender, country-capital, tense * Cluster structure reflects topic similarity ''Modern Usage'' In transformer models, the embedding layer is the first lookup; positional encodings are added. The final layer representations are ''contextualised embeddings'' and supersede static word vectors. <<button "Quiz: Why are dense embeddings preferred over one-hot?">> <<replace "#we-q">> ''✓ Dense embeddings encode semantic similarity — similar words have similar vectors. One-hot vectors are orthogonal, making all words equally distant from each other.'' [[Word2Vec]] | [[Bert]] | [[El-Mo]] | [[Tokenizer]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="we-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Word2Vec Word2Vec learns dense vector representations of words by training a shallow neural network to predict context. The resulting embeddings encode semantic relationships geometrically — similar words land close together in vector space. ''Two architectures'' - ''Skip-gram'' — given a word, predict its surrounding context words - ''CBOW'' (Continuous Bag of Words) — given context words, predict the centre word Both are trained on the same objective: maximise the probability of observed context pairs. ''The geometry of meaning'' After training, vector arithmetic captures semantic relationships: $$\text{king} - \text{man} + \text{woman} \approx \text{queen}$$ $$\text{Paris} - \text{France} + \text{Italy} \approx \text{Rome}$$ [[Cosine Similarity]] — the standard measure for comparing word vectors ''Training trick: negative sampling'' The full softmax over the vocabulary is expensive. Negative sampling trains only on the target word plus a few randomly sampled non-context words — much faster and still effective. ''Limitations'' Word2Vec gives each word a single vector regardless of context. "Bank" has one vector whether it means a river bank or a financial institution. [[El-Mo]], [[Bert]], and [[Transformers]] produce contextual embeddings that solve this. ''Connections'' [[Embeddings]] — Word2Vec is the foundational word embedding method [[Word Embeddings]] — broader survey of embedding approaches [[Lower Dimensional Representation]] — embeddings compress a large vocabulary into dense vectors [[Tokenizer]] — text must be tokenised before embeddings can be looked up --- [[Embeddings]] ← back | [[Bert]] | [[Cosine Similarity]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Tokenizer A tokenizer converts raw text into a sequence of tokens — the atomic units a model operates on. The choice of tokenizer affects vocabulary size, out-of-vocabulary handling, and model performance. ''Why tokenization matters'' Neural networks operate on fixed-size vocabularies of vectors. Text must be mapped to integer IDs before embedding lookup. The tokenizer defines what counts as one token. ''Approaches'' - ''Word-level'' — one token per word. Simple, but huge vocabulary; rare words become unknown. - ''Character-level'' — one token per character. Small vocabulary; sequences become very long. - ''Subword'' — the practical standard: split rare words into pieces, keep common words whole. ''Subword algorithms'' - ''Byte Pair Encoding (BPE)'' — used by GPT: merge the most frequent character pairs iteratively - ''WordPiece'' — used by [[Bert]]: similar to BPE but maximises language model likelihood - ''SentencePiece'' — language-agnostic; treats text as a stream of Unicode characters ''Vocabulary size tradeoff'' Larger vocabulary → fewer tokens per sentence, but more parameters in embedding table. Typical sizes: 32K–100K tokens. ''Special tokens'' Models use special tokens to mark structure: [CLS], [SEP], [MASK] in BERT; <|endoftext|> in GPT; <s> and </s> in translation models. ''Connections'' [[Bert]] — uses WordPiece tokenizer [[Transformers]] — all transformer models begin with tokenization [[Embeddings]] — token IDs are looked up in an embedding table [[Word2Vec]] — preceded subword tokenization; operated at word level --- [[Transformers]] ← back | [[Bert]] | [[Embeddings]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## BERT BERT (Bidirectional Encoder Representations from Transformers) learns deep contextual representations by training on two self-supervised tasks: masked language modelling and next sentence prediction. ''Pre-training tasks'' - ''Masked LM'' — randomly mask 15% of tokens; train the model to predict them using both left and right context - ''Next Sentence Prediction'' — given two sentences, predict whether the second follows the first in the corpus ''Why bidirectional matters'' Earlier models like GPT read left-to-right. BERT reads in both directions simultaneously — the representation of each token is informed by its full surrounding context. "I went to the bank to fish" vs. "I went to the bank to deposit money" — BERT gives "bank" different vectors in each. ''Fine-tuning'' Pre-train once on a large corpus, then fine-tune on a small task-specific dataset: $$\text{[CLS] sentence A [SEP] sentence B [SEP]}$$ Add a task head (classification, span extraction, etc.) and train end-to-end. [[Transfer Learning]] — fine-tuning is the canonical example of transfer learning in NLP ''Impact'' BERT set state-of-the-art on 11 NLP benchmarks when published (2018). It established the pre-train/fine-tune paradigm now universal in NLP and increasingly in vision. ''Connections'' [[Transformers]] — BERT is a transformer encoder stack [[Attention]] — self-attention is the core of BERT's representations [[Word2Vec]] / [[El-Mo]] — predecessors that led to BERT [[Tokenizer]] — BERT uses WordPiece tokenisation --- [[Transformers]] ← back | [[Transfer Learning]] | [[Tokenizer]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''ELMo — Embeddings from Language Models'' ELMo (Peters et al. 2018, AllenNLP) was a breakthrough: ''context-sensitive'' word representations produced by a deep bidirectional LSTM language model. ''Key Insight'' Traditional embeddings (Word2Vec, GloVe) give the word "bank" the same vector regardless of context. ELMo produces ''different'' vectors for each occurrence depending on surrounding text. $$ \text{ELMo}_k = \gamma \sum_{j=0}^{L} s_j \mathbf{h}_{k,j} $$ Each layer $j$ captures different linguistic phenomena: * Layer 0 (character CNN) → morphology, spelling * Layer 1 (biLSTM 1) → syntax, POS * Layer 2 (biLSTM 2) → semantics, co-reference The weights $s_j$ are learned per task. ''Historical Position'' * Pre-dates BERT but established the ''pre-train → fine-tune'' paradigm * BERT replaced the LSTM backbone with Transformers and joint bidirectional training * ELMo proved that deep language model representations transfer across NLP tasks ''Legacy'' ELMo showed the community that ''representations matter more than task-specific architectures''. This philosophy drove the entire era of foundation models. <<button "Quiz: What made ELMo different from Word2Vec?">> <<replace "#elmo-q">> ''✓ ELMo embeddings are context-dependent — the same word gets different vectors in different sentences. Word2Vec produces a single static vector per word.'' [[Bert]] | [[Word2Vec]] | [[Transformers]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="elmo-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> <img src="assets/transfer_learning.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="Transfer learning diagram"/> ! Transfer Learning !! Learning to Learn Training a deep neural network from scratch requires enormous amounts of labeled data and compute. For most real problems — medical imaging, specialized document classification, niche object detection — you simply don't have millions of labeled examples. Transfer learning is the insight that a model trained on one large task has already learned something useful for another. The features it extracted — edges, textures, semantic concepts — transfer because the world has shared structure. A model that learned what "fur" looks like from cat photos can help identify dogs, even though it never trained on dogs. !! The Paradigm: Pre-train, Then Fine-tune Modern transfer learning follows a two-stage workflow: ''Pre-training'': Train a large model on a massive, general dataset. For vision: ImageNet (1.2M images, 1000 classes). For language: internet-scale text (GPT, BERT). The model learns general-purpose representations — visual features, grammatical structure, world knowledge. ''Fine-tuning'': Take the pre-trained weights as a starting point. Replace the final layer (which predicted ImageNet classes) with a new layer matching your task (e.g., 2 classes: tumor/no-tumor). Train on your small labeled dataset. The pre-trained features provide the foundation; fine-tuning adapts the top of the network. !! What Transfers and Why Visualization studies of early network layers reveal a remarkable universality. The first few layers of any vision model learn Gabor-like edge detectors and color blobs — essentially the same features regardless of what the model was trained on. These are the visual primitives underlying all natural images. Middle layers detect textures and patterns. Later layers become task-specific (dog breeds vs. medical pathologies). This hierarchy means: ''freeze'' the early general layers, ''fine-tune'' the later task-specific ones. The same principle applies in NLP. BERT's lower layers encode syntax; upper layers encode semantics. Fine-tuning all layers on a new task usually helps, but freezing lower layers preserves linguistic knowledge and requires less data. !! Techniques: How Much to Fine-tune ''Freeze all, replace head'': The fastest option. Use the pre-trained network as a fixed feature extractor; train only the new classification head. Best when your dataset is tiny and similar to the source task. ''Fine-tune top layers'': Unfreeze the last few layers and train those along with the head. More flexible; better when your task differs somewhat from the source. ''Fine-tune entire network'': Unfreeze everything; use a very small learning rate to avoid catastrophic forgetting. Best when you have substantial data and your task differs significantly. ''Learning rate schedules'': Use layer-wise learning rate decay — lower rates for early (general) layers, higher rates for later (task-specific) layers. This preserves general features while adapting task-specific ones. !! Domain Adaptation When the source and target datasets have different distributions (domain shift), naive fine-tuning may fail. ''Domain adaptation'' techniques align the feature distributions. Adversarial domain adaptation trains a feature extractor to fool a domain classifier — producing features that work for the target task but look the same regardless of which domain they came from. !! Foundation Models Recent years have brought ''foundation models'' — massively pre-trained models (GPT-4, CLIP, Segment Anything) trained on internet-scale data with self-supervised objectives. Fine-tuning a foundation model for a new task requires minimal labeled data and can approach or exceed task-specific specialist performance. Transfer learning is now the default, not the exception. !! Technical Details | Strategy | When to use | Labeled data needed | |---------|------------|---------------------| | Feature extraction | Very small dataset, similar domain | ~100s | | Fine-tune top layers | Small dataset, moderate domain shift | ~1K | | Fine-tune all layers | Medium dataset, large domain shift | ~10K+ | | Train from scratch | Huge dataset, very different domain | ~1M+ | !! Quiz <<button "Q1: Why do early layers of vision networks transfer across tasks?">><<replace "#tl-q1">>Early layers learn edge detectors and color features that appear in all natural images, regardless of the specific task. These low-level visual primitives are universal, not task-specific, so they transfer directly.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="tl-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: What is catastrophic forgetting and how do you avoid it during fine-tuning?">><<replace "#tl-q2">>When fine-tuning on a new task overwrites previously learned knowledge — the model "forgets" the pre-trained representations. Avoid it by using a small learning rate, freezing lower layers, or using elastic weight consolidation (EWC) to penalize large changes to important weights.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="tl-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: When should you freeze layers vs. fine-tune all layers?">><<replace "#tl-q3">>Freeze when your dataset is small and your task is similar to the source (risk of overfitting outweighs benefit of adaptation). Fine-tune all layers when you have substantial data and your task differs meaningfully from the source domain.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="tl-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: What is domain shift and why is it a problem?">><<replace "#tl-q4">>Domain shift means the distribution of training data (source domain) differs from the deployment data (target domain) — e.g., photos vs. medical scans, news text vs. social media. Features learned in the source domain may not transfer well, degrading performance.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="tl-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: What makes foundation models so powerful for transfer learning?">><<replace "#tl-q5">>Foundation models are pre-trained on internet-scale data with self-supervised objectives, learning extremely rich, general representations. Fine-tuning them requires very little labeled data for the target task, since most needed knowledge is already encoded in their weights.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="tl-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Deep Learning Libraries'' ''PyTorch'' (Meta AI) — the dominant research framework. Dynamic computation graph ("define-by-run") makes debugging natural. De facto standard in academic ML. ''TensorFlow / Keras'' (Google) — production-oriented. TF 2.x adopted eager execution; Keras provides a high-level API. Dominant in industry deployment. ''JAX'' (Google DeepMind) — NumPy-compatible; pure functions; just-in-time compilation via XLA; automatic vectorisation (`vmap`) and differentiation (`grad`). Growing rapidly for research. ''Ecosystem comparison'' | | PyTorch | TensorFlow | JAX | | Graph | Dynamic | Static/eager | Functional | | Debug | Easy | Moderate | Functional-style | | Deployment | TorchServe/ONNX | TF Serving / TFLite | XLA / TPUs | | Research use | Dominant | Declining | Growing | ''HuggingFace Transformers'': model hub + training utilities; wraps PyTorch/TF/JAX. ''Lightning / Fabric'': removes PyTorch boilerplate; handles distributed training. ''ONNX'': open format for model exchange across frameworks. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Dynamic Programming'' An algorithmic paradigm that solves complex problems by breaking them into overlapping subproblems, solving each once, and storing results. ''Two Key Properties'' 1. ''Optimal substructure'': optimal solution contains optimal solutions to subproblems 2. ''Overlapping subproblems'': same subproblems recur many times ''Classic Examples'' * Fibonacci: $F(n) = F(n-1) + F(n-2)$ with memoisation * Shortest path (Bellman-Ford, Floyd-Warshall) * Sequence alignment (Smith-Waterman) * Knapsack, edit distance, longest common subsequence ''DP in Reinforcement Learning'' Bellman equations define recursive relationships in RL: $$ V^*(s) = \max_a \left[ r(s,a) + \gamma \sum_{s'} P(s'|s,a) V^*(s') \right] $$ Value iteration and policy iteration are DP algorithms that solve MDPs exactly when the transition model is known. ''Approaches'' * ''Top-down (memoisation)'': recursion + cache * ''Bottom-up (tabulation)'': fill table from smallest subproblems up ''Complexity'' DP trades time for space — runs in polynomial time by storing solutions. The table has one entry per unique subproblem. <<button "Quiz: What are the two properties a problem must have to admit a DP solution?">> <<replace "#dp-q">> ''✓ Optimal substructure (the overall optimum decomposes into subproblem optima) and overlapping subproblems (the same subproblems are solved repeatedly, making memoisation worthwhile).'' [[bellman equation]] | [[Markov Decision Process]] | [[Q Learning]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="dp-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Eigenvectors and Eigenvalues An eigenvector of a matrix is a direction that the matrix only stretches — not rotates. The eigenvalue is the stretch factor. ''Definition'' For a square matrix $\mathbf{A}$, a non-zero vector $\mathbf{v}$ is an eigenvector if: $$\mathbf{A}\mathbf{v} = \lambda \mathbf{v}$$ $\lambda$ is the corresponding eigenvalue. The matrix transforms $\mathbf{v}$ by scaling it — not changing its direction. ''Finding eigenvalues'' Solve the characteristic equation: $$\det(\mathbf{A} - \lambda \mathbf{I}) = 0$$ The roots are the eigenvalues. Substitute each back to find the eigenvectors. ''Geometric intuition'' Most vectors get rotated when multiplied by $\mathbf{A}$. Eigenvectors are the special directions that stay aligned — they reveal the natural axes of the transformation. ''Applications in ML'' [[PCA]] — the principal components are eigenvectors of the covariance matrix [[Singular Value Decompostion]] — generalises eigendecomposition to rectangular matrices [[Spectral Clustering]] — clusters using eigenvectors of the graph Laplacian [[Page Rank]] — the dominant eigenvector of the web link matrix [[Covariance Matrix]] — its eigenvectors reveal directions of maximum spread ''Key properties'' - Symmetric matrices have real eigenvalues and orthogonal eigenvectors - The trace of a matrix equals the sum of its eigenvalues - The determinant equals the product of its eigenvalues [[trace]] | [[Determinant]] --- [[Core Linear Algebra]] ← back | [[PCA]] | [[Singular Value Decompostion]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''The Mean as a Minimiser'' The arithmetic mean minimises the sum of squared deviations: $$ \bar{x} = \arg\min_c \sum_i (x_i - c)^2 $$ ''Proof'': take derivative w.r.t. $c$, set to zero: $\sum_i -2(x_i - c) = 0 \Rightarrow c = \frac{1}{n}\sum_i x_i = \bar{x}$. ''Generalisation'' | Loss | Minimiser | | $\sum(x_i - c)^2$ (MSE) | Arithmetic mean | | $\sum|x_i - c|$ (MAE) | Median | | $\max|x_i - c|$ ($L^\infty$) | Midrange | This is why MSE-trained regression predicts the conditional mean, and MAE-trained regression predicts the conditional median — more robust to outliers. ''K-Means connection'': each centroid is the mean of its assigned cluster because minimising within-cluster SSE over centroid positions gives exactly the sample mean of each cluster. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Hidden Markov Model (HMM)'' A probabilistic model for sequences where the system's true state is hidden — only noisy observations are visible. ''Components'' * States $S = \{s_1,\ldots,s_N\}$ — latent/hidden * Observations $O = \{o_1,\ldots,o_T\}$ — visible * ''Transition matrix'' $A$: $A_{ij} = P(z_t = s_j | z_{t-1} = s_i)$ * ''Emission matrix'' $B$: $B_{ij} = P(x_t = o_j | z_t = s_i)$ * ''Initial distribution'' $\pi$: $\pi_i = P(z_1 = s_i)$ ''Three Classic Problems'' | Problem | Algorithm | | ''Evaluation'' — $P(X|\lambda)$ | Forward algorithm | | ''Decoding'' — most likely state sequence | Viterbi algorithm | | ''Learning'' — estimate $\lambda = (A,B,\pi)$ from $X$ | Baum-Welch (EM) | ''Applications'' * Speech recognition (phoneme → word) * Part-of-speech tagging * Genomics (CpG islands, gene prediction) * Financial regime detection ''Limitations'' HMMs assume the Markov property (state depends only on previous state) and that observations are conditionally independent given the state. CRFs and deep sequence models (LSTM, Transformer) relax these assumptions. <<button "Quiz: What algorithm finds the most likely hidden state sequence in an HMM?">> <<replace "#hmm-q">> ''✓ The Viterbi algorithm — a dynamic programming approach that efficiently computes $\arg\max_{z_{1:T}} P(z_{1:T} | x_{1:T})$ in $O(N^2 T)$ time.'' [[Markov Decision Process]] | [[Baum-Welch algorithm]] | [[Dynamic Programing]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="hmm-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> <img src="assets/mdp.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="Markov Decision Process diagram"/> ! Markov Decision Process !! The Language of Sequential Decisions Supervised learning assumes fixed input-output pairs. But many real problems involve ''sequences of decisions'': a chess engine chooses moves; a robot chooses motor commands; a recommendation system chooses what to show next. Each choice affects future choices. Each action has consequences that unfold over time. The Markov Decision Process (MDP) is the mathematical framework that formalizes this. Every reinforcement learning algorithm — Q-learning, policy gradients, PPO — is solving an MDP. Understanding MDPs means understanding the foundation of all RL. !! The Five Components An MDP is defined by a tuple $(\mathcal{S}, \mathcal{A}, P, R, \gamma)$: $\mathcal{S}$ — ''State space'': all possible configurations of the environment. A chess board position. A robot's joint angles and velocities. The current inventory level. $\mathcal{A}$ — ''Action space'': all possible choices available to the agent. Legal chess moves. Motor torques. Order quantities. $P(s' \mid s, a)$ — ''Transition dynamics'': the probability of landing in state $s'$ after taking action $a$ in state $s$. The "rules of the world." $R(s, a, s')$ — ''Reward function'': the immediate reward received after transitioning. Positive for reaching the goal; negative for falling into a pit; zero otherwise. $\gamma \in [0,1)$ — ''Discount factor'': how much future rewards are worth relative to immediate ones. $\gamma = 0.9$ means a reward 10 steps away is worth $0.9^{10} \approx 35\%$ of the same reward now. !! The Markov Property The defining assumption: the future depends only on the current state, not on the history of how you got there. $P(s_{t+1} \mid s_t, a_t, s_{t-1}, a_{t-1}, \ldots) = P(s_{t+1} \mid s_t, a_t)$. This is a strong but useful assumption. It means the agent doesn't need to remember its entire history — the current state contains all relevant information. In practice, if the raw observation doesn't satisfy the Markov property (e.g., velocity isn't observed), you stack several recent observations to approximate a Markov state. !! Value Functions and Optimality The ''value function'' $V^\pi(s)$ is the expected total discounted reward starting from state $s$ and following policy $\pi$: $V^\pi(s) = \mathbb{E}_\pi\left[\sum_{t=0}^\infty \gamma^t R_t \;\middle|\; S_0 = s\right]$ The ''optimal value function'' $V^*(s)$ is the maximum over all policies. The ''Bellman optimality equation'' gives a recursive characterization: $V^*(s) = \max_a \sum_{s'} P(s' \mid s, a) \left[ R(s,a,s') + \gamma V^*(s') \right]$ This says: the optimal value of a state is the immediate reward plus the discounted optimal value of the best next state. Every RL algorithm is, in some sense, trying to solve or approximate this equation. !! Policy and Planning A ''policy'' $\pi(a \mid s)$ maps states to actions (or distributions over actions). The optimal policy $\pi^*$ selects the action that maximizes expected future reward from every state. When the transition model $P$ and reward $R$ are known, dynamic programming algorithms (value iteration, policy iteration) can compute $V^*$ exactly. When they're unknown — the common case — the agent must learn by interacting with the environment: reinforcement learning. !! Technical Details | Term | Symbol | Meaning | |------|--------|---------| | Return | $G_t = \sum_{k=0}^\infty \gamma^k R_{t+k+1}$ | Total discounted future reward | | State-value | $V^\pi(s) = \mathbb{E}[G_t \| S_t=s]$ | Expected return from state $s$ | | Action-value | $Q^\pi(s,a) = \mathbb{E}[G_t \| S_t=s, A_t=a]$ | Expected return from $(s,a)$ pair | | Advantage | $A^\pi(s,a) = Q^\pi(s,a) - V^\pi(s)$ | How much better than average | | Bellman eq. | $V^*(s) = \max_a [R + \gamma V^*(s')]$ | Recursive optimality condition | !! Quiz <<button "Q1: What is the Markov property and why does it matter?">><<replace "#mdp-q1">>The future state depends only on the current state and action, not on the full history. This means the agent needs only the current state (not memory of all past states) to act optimally — making the problem tractable.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="mdp-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: What does the discount factor γ control?">><<replace "#mdp-q2">>How much the agent values future rewards relative to immediate ones. γ=0: completely myopic, only cares about immediate reward. γ→1: patient, values distant future rewards nearly as much as immediate ones. It also ensures the infinite sum converges.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="mdp-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: What is the Bellman optimality equation expressing?">><<replace "#mdp-q3">>The optimal value of a state equals the immediate reward plus the discounted optimal value of the best next state, maximized over actions. It recursively decomposes the long-horizon optimization into a one-step lookahead — the foundation of dynamic programming in RL.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="mdp-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: What is the difference between the value function V*(s) and Q*(s,a)?">><<replace "#mdp-q4">>V*(s) is the optimal value of being in state s (already maximized over actions). Q*(s,a) is the optimal value of taking a specific action a in state s, then acting optimally thereafter. Q* is more useful for learning because argmax_a Q*(s,a) gives the optimal action directly.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="mdp-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: When can you solve an MDP with dynamic programming vs. reinforcement learning?">><<replace "#mdp-q5">>Dynamic programming (value/policy iteration) requires knowing the transition model P(s'|s,a) and reward R(s,a) — the "rules of the world." Reinforcement learning is used when these are unknown: the agent must discover them by interacting with the environment and observing outcomes.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="mdp-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''P vs NP'' One of the seven Millennium Prize Problems (Clay Mathematics Institute) — unanswered since Cook (1971). ''Definitions'' * ''P'': decision problems solvable in polynomial time $O(n^k)$ by a deterministic Turing machine * ''NP'': decision problems whose solutions can be ''verified'' in polynomial time — but finding the solution may take exponential time * ''NP-complete'': the hardest problems in NP (all NP problems reduce to them) * ''NP-hard'': at least as hard as NP-complete (may not be in NP) ''The Question'' Does $P = NP$? Can every problem whose solution is quickly verifiable also be quickly solved? Almost everyone believes $P \neq NP$ — but no proof exists. ''NP-Complete Examples'' * Boolean satisfiability (SAT) — the first NP-complete problem (Cook-Levin theorem) * Travelling salesman (decision version) * Knapsack, graph colouring, Hamiltonian path ''ML Implications'' * Training neural networks is NP-hard in the worst case * Feature selection ($\ell^0$ optimisation) is NP-hard → use $\ell^1$ relaxation * Finding the globally optimal decision tree is NP-hard → use greedy heuristics * Graph neural network expressiveness is bounded by the Weisfeiler-Leman test ''Practical Workarounds'' Approximation algorithms, heuristics, average-case analysis, and problem-specific structure allow NP-hard problems to be solved practically. <<button "Quiz: What distinguishes NP from P?">> <<replace "#pnp-q">> ''✓ P problems can be ''solved'' in polynomial time. NP problems can have their solutions ''verified'' in polynomial time. Whether this difference is real ($P \neq NP$) or illusory ($P = NP$) is the great open question.'' [[Big O Notation]] | [[Dynamic Programing]] | [[Optimization]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="pnp-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Big-O Notation'' A mathematical notation for describing the ''asymptotic upper bound'' of an algorithm's time or space complexity as input size $n \to \infty$. ''Definition'' $f(n) = O(g(n))$ iff $\exists c > 0, n_0$ such that $f(n) \leq c \cdot g(n)$ for all $n \geq n_0$. Informally: $f$ grows no faster than $g$ (up to constants). ''Common Classes (fastest to slowest)'' | Class | Name | Example | | $O(1)$ | Constant | Array index lookup | | $O(\log n)$ | Logarithmic | Binary search | | $O(n)$ | Linear | Linear scan | | $O(n \log n)$ | Log-linear | Merge sort | | $O(n^2)$ | Quadratic | Bubble sort, dense attention | | $O(n^3)$ | Cubic | Matrix multiplication (naive) | | $O(2^n)$ | Exponential | Brute-force subset search | ''Related Notations'' * $\Omega(g)$: lower bound * $\Theta(g)$: tight bound (both upper and lower) ''ML Relevance'' * Transformer attention: $O(n^2 d)$ — quadratic in sequence length * Naive k-NN: $O(nd)$ per query * Training an $L$-layer neural net on $n$ examples: $O(nLd^2)$ <<button "Quiz: Why is $O(n\log n)$ better than $O(n^2)$ for large $n$?">> <<replace "#bigo-q">> ''✓ $\log n$ grows much slower than $n$ — for $n=10^6$, $n\log n \approx 2\times10^7$ while $n^2 = 10^{12}$. The difference becomes enormous at scale.'' [[P Versus NP]] | [[Search]] | [[Optimization]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="bigo-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Singular Value Decomposition SVD is the most important matrix decomposition in applied mathematics and machine learning. Every matrix — regardless of shape or rank — has an SVD. ''The decomposition'' Any matrix $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{R}^{m \times n}$ can be written as: $$\mathbf{A} = \mathbf{U}\boldsymbol{\Sigma}\mathbf{V}^\top$$ - $\mathbf{U} \in \mathbb{R}^{m \times m}$ — left singular vectors (orthonormal) - $\boldsymbol{\Sigma} \in \mathbb{R}^{m \times n}$ — diagonal matrix of singular values $\sigma_1 \geq \sigma_2 \geq \cdots \geq 0$ - $\mathbf{V} \in \mathbb{R}^{n \times n}$ — right singular vectors (orthonormal) ''Low-rank approximation'' Keep only the top $k$ singular values to get the best rank-$k$ approximation of $\mathbf{A}$: $$\mathbf{A}_k = \mathbf{U}_k \boldsymbol{\Sigma}_k \mathbf{V}_k^\top$$ This is the Eckart-Young theorem — SVD gives the optimal compression for any $k$. ''Applications in ML'' [[PCA]] — principal components are the left singular vectors of the centred data matrix [[Matrix Factorization]] — recommendation systems decompose a ratings matrix via SVD [[Embeddings]] — word embedding matrices are often analysed via SVD [[Spectral Norm]] — the largest singular value $\sigma_1$; used in network regularisation ''Connections'' [[Eiganvectors and Eiganvalue]] — singular values are square roots of eigenvalues of $\mathbf{A}^\top\mathbf{A}$ [[Core Linear Algebra]] ← broader context [[Reconstruction Error]] — measured by the discarded singular values --- [[Core Linear Algebra]] ← back | [[PCA]] | [[Eiganvectors and Eiganvalue]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Matrix Factorisation'' Decomposing a matrix $M$ into a product of (usually smaller) matrices that together approximate $M$. ''General Form'' $$ M \approx U V^\top, \quad U \in \mathbb{R}^{m \times k},\; V \in \mathbb{R}^{n \times k} $$ $k \ll \min(m,n)$: the rank of the approximation. ''Key Decompositions'' | Method | Constraint | Use Case | | SVD | None | Optimal low-rank approx | | NMF | $U,V \geq 0$ | Parts-based (face parts, topics) | | PCA | $V$ orthonormal | Dimensionality reduction | | ICA | Independent components | Source separation | ''Collaborative Filtering'' Factorising a user-item rating matrix $R \approx U V^\top$: * Each user $i$ → latent vector $\mathbf{u}_i \in \mathbb{R}^k$ * Each item $j$ → latent vector $\mathbf{v}_j \in \mathbb{R}^k$ * Predicted rating: $\hat{r}_{ij} = \mathbf{u}_i^\top \mathbf{v}_j$ Unobserved entries are predicted from the low-rank structure.
