This is how Netflix, Spotify, etc. generate recommendations. ''Optimisation'' Minimise reconstruction loss on observed entries via SGD or ALS (Alternating Least Squares). <<button "Quiz: In collaborative filtering, what do the latent vectors $\mathbf{u}_i$ represent?">> <<replace "#mf-q">> ''✓ Latent preference factors for user $i$ — abstract dimensions like genre affinity or quality preference. The dot product with an item vector predicts the user's rating for that item.'' [[Singular Value Decompostion]] | [[PCA]] | [[Non-negative matrix factorization]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="mf-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''LU Decomposition'' Factors a square matrix $A$ into a product of a Lower triangular matrix $L$ and an Upper triangular matrix $U$: $$ A = LU $$ (with partial pivoting: $PA = LU$ where $P$ is a permutation matrix) ''Structure'' $$ L = \begin{pmatrix}1&&&\\l_{21}&1&&\\l_{31}&l_{32}&1&\\\vdots&&&\ddots\end{pmatrix}, \quad U = \begin{pmatrix}u_{11}&u_{12}&\cdots\\&u_{22}&\cdots\\&&\ddots\end{pmatrix} $$ ''Why Triangular?'' Triangular systems $L\mathbf{y}=\mathbf{b}$ and $U\mathbf{x}=\mathbf{y}$ are solved in $O(n^2)$ by forward/back substitution. ''Solving $A\mathbf{x} = \mathbf{b}$'' 1. Factorise: $A = LU$ — costs $O(n^3)$ once 2. Solve $L\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{b}$ (forward substitution) 3. Solve $U\mathbf{x} = \mathbf{y}$ (back substitution) For multiple right-hand sides, the factorisation is reused — much more efficient than re-inverting. ''When to Use'' * Dense systems (not too sparse) * Repeated solves with the same $A$ * Computing $\det(A) = \prod_i u_{ii}$ ''Alternatives'' Cholesky ($A = LL^\top$) for symmetric positive definite — twice as fast as LU. QR for least-squares. Conjugate gradient for large sparse systems. <<button "Quiz: What is the computational advantage of LU decomposition for multiple right-hand sides?">> <<replace "#lu-q">> ''✓ The $O(n^3)$ factorisation is computed once. Each new right-hand side $\mathbf{b}$ is solved in $O(n^2)$ via forward/backward substitution — far cheaper than factorising again.'' [[matrix inverse]] | [[Determinant]] | [[Core Linear Algebra]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="lu-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Non-Negative Matrix Factorisation (NMF)'' Factorises a non-negative matrix $V \approx WH$ where $W, H \geq 0$ (element-wise). $$ V \in \mathbb{R}_{\geq 0}^{m \times n} \approx W \in \mathbb{R}_{\geq 0}^{m \times k} \cdot H \in \mathbb{R}_{\geq 0}^{k \times n} $$ ''Why Non-Negativity?'' Non-negativity forces ''parts-based'' decompositions. In contrast: * PCA: components can cancel → abstract, hard to interpret * NMF: components only add → interpretable parts Classic demo: NMF on face images discovers eyes, noses, mouths as components. PCA finds holistic eigenfaces. ''Objective'' $$ \min_{W,H \geq 0} \|V - WH\|_F^2 $$ Solved via Multiplicative Updates (Lee & Seung 1999): $$ H \leftarrow H \odot \frac{W^\top V}{W^\top W H}, \quad W \leftarrow W \odot \frac{V H^\top}{W H H^\top} $$ ''Applications'' * Topic modelling: $V$ = term-document matrix; $W$ = word-topic; $H$ = topic-document * Spectroscopy: identify chemical components from spectra * Audio source separation * Recommender systems with non-negative ratings ''NMF vs LDA'' Both discover topics, but LDA is probabilistic with Dirichlet priors; NMF is a matrix factorisation without probabilistic interpretation. <<button "Quiz: Why does NMF produce parts-based representations?">> <<replace "#nmf-q">> ''✓ Non-negativity means components can only ''add'' to each other — no cancellation. This forces the model to represent data as a sum of distinct parts rather than allowing cancelling linear combinations.'' [[Matrix Factorization]] | [[PCA]] | [[Clustering]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="nmf-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> In the field of mathematics and computer science, the concept of a transitivity matrix has profound implications, particularly in relation to graph theory and network analysis. A transitivity matrix typically represents transitive closure in a binary relationship - notably in a graph context. The binary relation R on a set X is transitive if whenever an element a is related to an element b, and b is in turn related to an element c, then a is also related to c. In simple terms, if we have a connection from a to b, and a connection from b to c, transitivity infers an indirect connection from a to c. Let's consider an n×n matrix - A representing directed edges in the graph. If A[i][j] = 1 denotes an edge from vertex i to vertex j, then graph transitivity is the process of making A[i][j] = 1 whenever there is a path from i to j in graph for all i,j. We compute the transitivity matrix - often referred to as the transitive closure of a graph - by applying methods like Warshall's Algorithm. This algorithm is efficient and utilizes dynamic programming principles to build the transitive closure, determining if there's a path between any two given nodes. In essence, the transitivity matrix provides a comprehensive pathway analysis within a graph, which has applications in areas like data mining, social network analysis, and artificial intelligence, among others. From an AI perspective, transitivity matrices can be used to infer semantic relationships, discover hidden patterns, or track possible navigation sequences in complex systems. <<include "Header">> ''PageRank'' Google's original link-analysis algorithm (Brin & Page, 1998) that ranks web pages by the importance of pages linking to them — a recursive definition of importance. ''Core Idea'' A page is important if important pages link to it. $$ PR(u) = \frac{1-d}{N} + d \sum_{v \in B_u} \frac{PR(v)}{L(v)} $$ * $d \approx 0.85$: damping factor (probability of following a link vs. jumping randomly) * $B_u$: set of pages linking to $u$ * $L(v)$: number of outbound links from $v$ ''Random Walk Interpretation'' PageRank = stationary distribution of a random surfer who follows links with probability $d$ and teleports to a random page with probability $1-d$. ''Matrix Form'' $$ \mathbf{r} = d \cdot A \mathbf{r} + \frac{1-d}{N} \mathbf{1} $$ Solved via power iteration. $\mathbf{r}$ is the principal eigenvector of the modified adjacency matrix. ''Applications Beyond Web'' * Citation ranking in academic papers * Social network influence scoring * Node importance in knowledge graphs * Protein interaction networks <<button "Quiz: What is the probabilistic interpretation of PageRank?">> <<replace "#pr-q">> ''✓ It equals the probability that a random surfer — who follows links with probability $d$ and teleports randomly with probability $1-d$ — is visiting a given page in the long run (stationary distribution).'' [[Eiganvectors and Eiganvalue]] | [[Transitivity Matrix]] | [[Search]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="pr-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Converting Time Series to Supervised Learning'' Reframe a time series forecasting problem as a standard regression/classification problem using a ''sliding window''. ''Sliding Window (Lag Features)'' Given series $x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_T$, create input-output pairs with window size $w$: | Input ($\mathbf{x}$) | Target ($y$) | | $[x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_w]$ | $x_{w+1}$ | | $[x_2, x_3, \ldots, x_{w+1}]$ | $x_{w+2}$ | | $\vdots$ | $\vdots$ | ''Steps'' 1. Choose lag window $w$ (use ACF/PACF to guide choice) 2. Optionally add calendar features (hour, day-of-week, month) 3. Optionally add rolling statistics (rolling mean, std, min, max) 4. Split temporally — never shuffle; test set = most recent data 5. Train any supervised model (XGBoost, LSTM, Transformer) ''Pitfalls'' * Shuffling destroys temporal structure and causes data leakage * Normalise per-series to handle non-stationarity * Multi-step forecasting: predict $h$ steps ahead by either direct multi-output or recursive one-step This approach powers modern forecasting libraries like LightGBM-based models and N-BEATS. <<include "Footer">> Sure, here is a simple Python script that uses NumPy and Matplotlib to plot a Gaussian (normal) distribution: ```python import numpy as np import matplotlib.pyplot as plt # mean and standard deviation mu, sigma = 0, 0.1 # draw random samples from a normal (Gaussian) distribution s = np.random.normal(mu, sigma, 1000) # Create the bins and histogram count, bins, ignored = plt.hist(s, 30, density=True) # Plot the distribution curve plt.plot(bins, 1/(sigma * np.sqrt(2 * np.pi)) * np.exp( - (bins - mu)**2 / (2 * sigma**2) ), linewidth=2, color='r') plt.show() ``` This script generates 1000 random numbers following a Gaussian distribution with mean 0 and standard deviation 0.1. It then plots a histogram of these numbers and overlays the expected probability density function in red. The `np.random.normal` function is used to generate the numbers, and `plt.hist` and `plt.plot` are used to create the plot.[[AI Topics]] [[Linear Programing]] [[Dynamic Programing]] [[Matrix Completion]] [[Operations research]] [[Core Linear Algebra]] [[Statistics]] [[Mathmatical Concepts]] Mathmatical Result and how you compute it. [[Optimization]] [[perturbation theroy]] [[chaos theory]] [[Bayesian Methods]] [[Information Thoery]] [[dynamical systems]] [[Complex systems]] https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Closed-form_expression` <<include "Header">> ''Closed-Form Solution'' An exact algebraic expression for the solution to an optimisation problem — no iterative algorithm required. ''Linear Regression: Normal Equations'' Minimise $\|\mathbf{y} - X\mathbf{w}\|^2$. Setting $\nabla_\mathbf{w} = 0$: $$ \hat{\mathbf{w}} = (X^\top X)^{-1} X^\top \mathbf{y} $$ This is exact — computed in one step. ''When Closed Forms Exist'' * Convex quadratic objectives (linear regression, Ridge regression) * Problems with linear constraints (Lagrangian KKT conditions) * Gaussian conjugate priors (Bayesian linear regression) ''When They Don't Exist'' * Non-linear models (neural networks, logistic regression) — no algebraic solution * Non-smooth objectives ($L^1$) * Complex constraints ''Computational Trade-off'' | Approach | Complexity | Pros | Cons | | Closed form | $O(p^3)$ for $(X^\top X)^{-1}$ | Exact, one-shot | Expensive for large $p$ | | Gradient descent | $O(np)$ per step | Scales to large $p,n$ | Iterative, approximate | For $p > 10^4$, gradient descent is usually preferred even when a closed form exists. <<button "Quiz: Why might you prefer gradient descent over the normal equations even for linear regression?">> <<replace "#cf-q">> ''✓ Inverting $X^\top X$ costs $O(p^3)$ — prohibitive for many features. Gradient descent costs $O(np)$ per iteration and scales to millions of features.'' [[Linear Regression]] | [[Stocastic Gradient Descent]] | [[Optimization]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="cf-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Core Linear Algebra Linear algebra is the language of machine learning. Data is a matrix. A model is a transformation. Training is finding the right transformation. ''Vectors and spaces'' [[Linear Combination]] — building vectors from other vectors [[inner product]] — measuring similarity and angle between vectors [[outer product]] — building matrices from vectors [[Norms]] — measuring the size of a vector [[Change of Basis]] — seeing the same data from a different frame ''Matrices and transformations'' [[Matrix Transformations]] — what matrices do geometrically [[Determinant]] — does this transformation preserve volume? [[matrix inverse]] — undoing a transformation [[Orthonormalization]] — building a clean, perpendicular basis [[Gram-Schmidt algorithm]] — the algorithm for orthonormalization ''Structure and decomposition'' [[Singular Value Decompostion]] — the most important decomposition in ML [[Eiganvectors and Eiganvalue]] — directions that survive transformation [[LU decompostion]] — factoring for efficient computation [[Sparsity]] — when most entries are zero ''Advanced topics'' [[random matrix theory]] — statistics of large random matrices [[Frobenius norm]] — measuring the size of a matrix [[Spectral Norm]] — largest singular value, key in deep learning [[Messures]] — generalizing length and volume --- [[Overview]] ← back | [[Matrix Transformations]] | [[Singular Value Decompostion]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Determinant'' The determinant $\det(A)$ (or $|A|$) is a scalar that encodes how much a linear transformation scales volume. ''Geometric Meaning'' If $A$ is $n\times n$, $|\det(A)|$ is the factor by which $A$ multiplies $n$-dimensional volume. The sign indicates orientation: * $\det > 0$: orientation preserved * $\det < 0$: orientation flipped * $\det = 0$: the transformation collapses space to a lower dimension (matrix is singular) ''2×2 Formula'' $$ \det\begin{pmatrix}a&b\\c&d\end{pmatrix} = ad - bc $$ ''Properties'' * $\det(AB) = \det(A)\det(B)$ * $\det(A^\top) = \det(A)$ * $\det(A^{-1}) = 1/\det(A)$ * Swapping two rows negates the determinant ''Relation to Eigenvalues'' $$ \det(A) = \prod_{i=1}^n \lambda_i $$ The determinant is zero iff at least one eigenvalue is zero. ''ML Uses'' * Checking invertibility ($\det \neq 0$) * Change-of-variables formula in probability densities (normalising flows) * Log-determinant in Gaussian likelihoods: $\log|\Sigma|$ <<button "Quiz: What does $\det(A) = 0$ imply?">> <<replace "#det-q">> ''✓ $A$ is singular (not invertible). The columns are linearly dependent; the transformation collapses at least one dimension to zero.'' [[matrix inverse]] | [[Eiganvectors and Eiganvalue]] | [[Core Linear Algebra]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="det-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Causal Modeling'' Moving beyond "correlation" to ask: ''what happens if we intervene?'' ''The Ladder of Causation'' (Pearl) | Rung | Question | Example | | 1. Association | What is? | $P(Y|X)$ | | 2. Intervention | What if we do $X$? | $P(Y|\text{do}(X))$ | | 3. Counterfactual | What if we had done $X$? | $P(Y_x | X=x', Y=y')$ | ''Structural Causal Models (SCMs)'' A graph where nodes are variables and directed edges represent causal relationships. An arrow $X \to Y$ means $X$ causally produces $Y$. ''Identification via do-calculus'' $$ P(Y|\text{do}(X)) \neq P(Y|X) \text{ in general} $$ The back-door criterion tells you which variables to condition on to block confounding paths. ''Why It Matters for ML'' * Models trained on observational data learn $P(Y|X)$ — they break under distribution shift caused by interventions * Causal models are more robust and generalisable across environments * Key to fairness: disparate impact vs. disparate treatment require causal framing ''Tools'' DoWhy (Microsoft), CausalML, EconML, dagitty (R) <<button "Quiz: What is the difference between $P(Y|X)$ and $P(Y|\text{do}(X))$?">> <<replace "#causal-q">> ''✓ $P(Y|X)$ conditions on observing $X$ (passive). $P(Y|\text{do}(X))$ models actively setting $X$, cutting all incoming arrows to $X$ in the causal graph — eliminating confounding.'' [[Spurious Correlations]] | [[Regression to the Mean]] | [[Bayesian Methods]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="causal-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> [[autoregressive]] [[sgd]] [[unversality class]] [[Student Teacher Model]] [[point estimate]] [[emprical methods]] [[finite state machine]] [[diffusion]] [[Maximum Likelihood Estimation]] [[Monty Hall]] [[KL Divergence]] [[Levy flight]] [[Recruitment Paradox]] [[active inference]] [[transitivity matrix]] [[Skipped connections]] [[penalty term]] [[objective function ]] [[penalty term]] [[ Dataset Destillation]] [[chain rule]] [[Perceptron]] [[scaled dot-product attention]] p<<include "Header">> ! Gradient Descent <img src="assets/gradient_descent.svg" alt="Gradient descent — contour plot with descent path (left) and loss curve (right)" style="width:100%;max-width:620px;display:block;margin:16px auto 24px auto;"> Virtually every model in machine learning is trained by solving the same underlying problem: find the parameter values that minimise a loss function. Gradient descent is how we solve it. The update rule fits on one line — $\theta \leftarrow \theta - \eta \nabla L(\theta)$ — and yet it underlies everything from logistic regression to GPT. --- !! The Core Idea Imagine you're standing on a hilly landscape in thick fog. You want to reach the lowest valley, but you can't see far. The one thing you //can// do is feel the slope beneath your feet and take a step downhill. Do that repeatedly, and you'll eventually find a valley. That slope is the gradient $\nabla L(\theta)$ — a vector pointing in the direction of //steepest ascent// of the loss. We step in the //opposite// direction. The step size is controlled by the ''learning rate'' $\eta$, which determines how far we move each iteration. This is gradient descent. It's not guaranteed to find the global minimum, it doesn't use curvature information, and it can be slow — but it scales to hundreds of billions of parameters and works. --- !! The Learning Rate: The Most Important Hyperparameter $\eta$ controls the trade-off between speed and stability. Too ''large'': each step overshoots the valley floor. The loss bounces back and forth and may diverge entirely. Too ''small'': steps are tiny, convergence is glacially slow, and you waste computation. Just right: the loss decreases steadily each iteration, slowing as it approaches a minimum. In practice $\eta$ is rarely "set and forgotten" — learning rate schedules decay $\eta$ over training, and adaptive methods adjust it automatically per parameter. --- !! Stochastic and Mini-Batch Gradient Descent Full gradient descent computes $\nabla L$ over the entire dataset every step. For $n = 10^6$ examples, that's expensive. ''Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD)'' approximates the gradient using a single randomly drawn example: $$\theta \leftarrow \theta - \eta \nabla L_i(\theta)$$ This estimate is noisy but cheap. ''Mini-batch SGD'' — the standard in deep learning — uses a small batch (typically 32–256 examples) to balance noise against cost. Crucially, the noise introduced by sampling is not purely a nuisance: it acts as implicit regularisation, helping the model escape sharp minima and find flatter, better-generalising solutions. --- !! Momentum and Adaptive Methods Vanilla SGD has two practical problems: it zigzags on elongated loss surfaces (as the diagram shows), and it uses the same learning rate for every parameter regardless of how frequently or rarely that parameter receives useful gradient signal. ''Momentum'' addresses the first problem. Rather than taking a step directly in the gradient direction, it accumulates a velocity vector — an exponential moving average of past gradients. This smooths out the zigzag and accelerates progress along consistent directions: $$v \leftarrow \beta v + (1-\beta)\nabla L, \quad \theta \leftarrow \theta - \eta v$$ ''Adam'' (Adaptive Moment Estimation) addresses both problems simultaneously. It maintains per-parameter estimates of the first moment (mean gradient) and second moment (uncentred variance), and divides the step by the root of the second moment — effectively giving each parameter its own adaptive learning rate. Adam is the default choice for most deep learning tasks. --- !! What Can Go Wrong ''Local minima'' — in high dimensions, most critical points are saddle points rather than true local minima, and SGD noise typically pushes the optimiser off saddle points. True local minima are less of a problem in practice than early literature suggested. ''Vanishing and exploding gradients'' — in deep networks, gradients can shrink to zero or grow without bound as they propagate backward. [[Vanishing gradient|Vanishing]] gradients starve early layers of signal; [[Exploding gradient|exploding]] gradients destabilise training. Residual connections, normalisation layers, and gradient clipping are the standard remedies. ''Flat regions and plateaux'' — areas where the gradient is near zero for many steps, causing training to stall. Momentum and adaptive methods help escape these. --- !! Technical Details ''Update rules:'' * Batch GD: $\theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - \eta \nabla L(\theta_t)$ * SGD: $\theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - \eta \nabla L_i(\theta_t)$, $i$ drawn uniformly * Momentum: $v_{t+1} = \beta v_t + \nabla L$; $\theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - \eta v_{t+1}$ * Adam: $m_t = \beta_1 m_{t-1} + (1-\beta_1)\nabla L$; $v_t = \beta_2 v_{t-1} + (1-\beta_2)(\nabla L)^2$; $\hat{m} = m_t/(1-\beta_1^t)$; $\hat{v} = v_t/(1-\beta_2^t)$; $\theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - \eta\hat{m}/(\sqrt{\hat{v}}+\epsilon)$ ''Convergence:'' For convex $L$ with Lipschitz gradients, GD with $\eta < 2/L$ converges. SGD converges with decaying learning rate $\sum \eta_t = \infty$, $\sum \eta_t^2 < \infty$. For non-convex deep networks, convergence guarantees are weaker — we rely on empirical behaviour. ''Learning rate schedules:'' step decay, cosine annealing, warmup-then-decay (common in transformers), cyclic LR. --- !! Quiz ''1. What does the gradient tell you, and why do we move in the opposite direction?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#gd-q1">>'✓ The gradient $\nabla L(\theta)$ points in the direction of steepest increase of the loss. We move in the opposite direction — steepest decrease — to minimise $L$. The update $\theta \leftarrow \theta - \eta \nabla L$ moves one step downhill on the loss surface.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="gd-q1"></span> ''2. What happens when the learning rate is too large? Too small?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#gd-q2">>'✓ Too large: steps overshoot the minimum — the loss oscillates or diverges. Too small: convergence is extremely slow, requiring many more gradient evaluations. The right $\eta$ balances speed against stability, and in practice schedules decay it over training.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="gd-q2"></span> ''3. Why does SGD use mini-batches instead of single examples or the full dataset?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#gd-q3">>'✓ Full-batch GD is expensive at large $n$. Single-example SGD is cheap but very noisy. Mini-batches (32–256 examples) balance the two: gradient estimates are stable enough to make consistent progress, cheap enough to compute many steps per second, and noisy enough to act as implicit regularisation.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="gd-q3"></span> ''4. How does momentum improve on vanilla SGD?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#gd-q4">>'✓ Momentum accumulates a velocity vector — an exponential moving average of past gradients. This smooths out oscillations (the zigzag on elongated surfaces) and accelerates movement along directions where gradients consistently agree. It effectively gives GD "inertia" to push through flat regions and past noise.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="gd-q4"></span> ''5. What does Adam do that SGD with momentum does not?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#gd-q5">>'✓ Adam adapts the learning rate per parameter. It tracks both the mean gradient ($m_t$) and the uncentred variance ($v_t$), dividing the step by $\sqrt{\hat{v}}$. Parameters with large, consistent gradients get smaller steps; sparse or infrequent gradients get larger steps. This makes Adam well-suited to problems with sparse features or varying gradient scales.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="gd-q5"></span> ''6. Why are saddle points more problematic than local minima in deep networks?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#gd-q6">>'✓ In high dimensions, most critical points (where $\nabla L = 0$) are saddle points — flat in some directions, curved in others. SGD noise helps escape saddle points because the noisy gradient has a component pointing away from them. True local minima that are significantly worse than the global minimum are rare in overparameterised networks.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="gd-q6"></span> ''7. What causes vanishing gradients and why does it matter?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#gd-q7">>'✓ In deep networks, gradients are multiplied through many layers during backpropagation. If those multipliers are consistently less than 1 (e.g. sigmoid saturated at extremes), gradients shrink exponentially toward the input layers. Early layers receive near-zero gradient signal and barely update, making deep networks hard to train. Residual connections and ReLU activations largely solve this.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="gd-q7"></span> ''8. What is a learning rate schedule and why use one?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#gd-q8">>'✓ A schedule changes $\eta$ during training. Large $\eta$ early allows fast, exploratory movement; decayed $\eta$ later allows fine-grained convergence to a good minimum. Common schedules: step decay (multiply by 0.1 every $k$ epochs), cosine annealing (smooth sinusoidal decay), warmup-then-decay (common in transformers — ramp up first to avoid instability).'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="gd-q8"></span> ''9. Why does mini-batch SGD noise help generalisation?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#gd-q9">>'✓ Gradient noise from small batches tends to push the optimiser toward flatter minima — wide, bowl-shaped regions of the loss surface. Flat minima generalise better because a small shift in parameters changes the loss less; sharp minima are brittle. This is the "implicit regularisation" of SGD — the noise acts as a beneficial perturbation.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="gd-q9"></span> ''10. When would you choose Adam over SGD with momentum?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#gd-q10">>'✓ Adam is preferred when: (1) features have very different scales or sparsity (NLP embeddings, sparse inputs); (2) you want reliable training with less hyperparameter tuning; (3) using transformers (Adam with warmup is standard). SGD + momentum is often preferred for computer vision — with careful tuning it frequently achieves better final accuracy than Adam, especially with batch normalisation.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="gd-q10"></span> <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## L1 Regularization L1 regularization adds the sum of absolute values of the weights to the loss. Its key property is sparsity: it drives some weights to exactly zero, performing automatic feature selection. ''The penalised loss'' $$\mathcal{L}_{\text{L1}} = \mathcal{L}_{\text{data}} + \lambda \sum_j |w_j|$$ $\lambda$ controls the strength of the penalty. Larger $\lambda$ → more weights zeroed out. ''Why L1 produces sparsity'' The L1 ball $\{w : \|w\|_1 \leq t\}$ has corners on the axes. When the loss contours touch the L1 ball, they are most likely to touch at a corner — where some coordinates are exactly zero. Compare to [[L2]]: the L2 ball is smooth (a sphere) — no corners, so the solution is unlikely to be exactly zero on any axis. L2 shrinks all weights but rarely zeros any. ''Applications'' [[Lasso]] — linear regression with L1 penalty Feature selection — useful when you have many features and suspect most are irrelevant [[Sparsity]] — L1 is the standard way to induce sparsity in model weights ''Proximal gradient'' L1 is not differentiable at zero. It is minimised using the subgradient or the proximal operator: $$\text{prox}_{\lambda|\cdot|}(w) = \text{sign}(w)\max(|w| - \lambda, 0)$$ This is the ''soft-thresholding'' operator. --- [[Regularization]] ← back | [[Lasso]] | [[L2]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## L2 Regularization L2 regularization adds the sum of squared weights to the loss. It shrinks all weights smoothly toward zero, penalising large weights without eliminating any entirely. ''The penalised loss'' $$\mathcal{L}_{\text{L2}} = \mathcal{L}_{\text{data}} + \lambda \sum_j w_j^2$$ ''Why L2 shrinks but doesn't zero'' The L2 ball is a smooth sphere. The loss contours are likely to touch it on a smooth face, not at a corner — so the optimal weights are small but non-zero. Compare to [[L1]], which has corners and produces exact zeros. ''Bayesian interpretation'' L2 regularisation is equivalent to placing a Gaussian prior on the weights: $$p(w_j) = \mathcal{N}(0, 1/2\lambda)$$ MAP estimation under this prior gives the L2-regularised solution. ''Applications'' [[Ridge regression]] — linear regression with L2 penalty [[Logistic Regression]] — L2 is the default regularisation Deep learning — weight decay in [[adam]] and SGD is L2 regularisation ''Effect on training'' L2 adds a gradient $2\lambda w_j$ at each step — equivalent to multiplying weights by $(1 - 2\alpha\lambda)$ before each update. This is why it is also called ''weight decay''. --- [[Regularization]] ← back | [[Ridge regression]] | [[L1]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Lasso Lasso (Least Absolute Shrinkage and Selection Operator) is linear regression with an [[L1]] penalty. It produces sparse solutions — many coefficients are driven to exactly zero — making it a simultaneous regression and feature selection method. ''The objective'' $$\hat{\mathbf{w}} = \underset{\mathbf{w}}{\arg\min} \underbrace{\frac{1}{n}\|\mathbf{y} - \mathbf{X}\mathbf{w}\|_2^2}_{\text{MSE}} + \lambda\underbrace{\|\mathbf{w}\|_1}_{\text{L1 penalty}}$$ ''Key property: sparsity'' The L1 penalty creates corners on the constraint set. At the optimal solution, some weights are exactly zero — those features are effectively removed from the model. Lasso selects a subset of features automatically. ''When Lasso works best'' - Many features, few of which are truly relevant - You want an interpretable model with a small active set - $p > n$ (more features than samples) — Lasso is one of few methods that work here ''Limitation'' When features are correlated, Lasso tends to pick one and zero out the others arbitrarily. [[Elastic Net]] (L1 + L2) handles this better. ''Solution method'' No closed form (unlike [[Ridge regression]]). Solved via coordinate descent or the LARS algorithm. [[Closed Form Solution]] — Lasso does not have one. --- [[Regularization]] ← back | [[Ridge regression]] | [[Elastic Net]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Ridge Regression Ridge regression is linear regression with an [[L2]] penalty. It addresses multicollinearity and overfitting by shrinking all coefficients toward zero, while retaining all features. ''The objective'' $$\hat{\mathbf{w}} = \underset{\mathbf{w}}{\arg\min} \frac{1}{n}\|\mathbf{y} - \mathbf{X}\mathbf{w}\|_2^2 + \lambda\|\mathbf{w}\|_2^2$$ ''Closed form solution'' Unlike [[Lasso]], ridge has an exact solution: $$\hat{\mathbf{w}} = (\mathbf{X}^\top\mathbf{X} + \lambda\mathbf{I})^{-1}\mathbf{X}^\top\mathbf{y}$$ Adding $\lambda\mathbf{I}$ makes the matrix invertible even when $\mathbf{X}^\top\mathbf{X}$ is singular — this is the regularising effect in action. ''Bias-variance tradeoff'' - $\lambda = 0$: ordinary least squares — unbiased but high variance when features are correlated - $\lambda \to \infty$: all coefficients → 0 — high bias, zero variance - Optimal $\lambda$: chosen by [[Cross Validation]] ''When to use Ridge vs. Lasso'' - All features are potentially relevant → Ridge (shrinks all, keeps all) - Sparse true signal, many irrelevant features → [[Lasso]] (zeroes out irrelevant ones) - Correlated features, want both effects → [[Elastic Net]] --- [[Regularization]] ← back | [[Lasso]] | [[Elastic Net]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Elastic Net'' Elastic Net (Zou & Hastie 2005) combines $L^1$ and $L^2$ regularisation: $$ \mathcal{L}(\mathbf{w}) = \|\mathbf{y} - X\mathbf{w}\|_2^2 + \lambda_1\|\mathbf{w}\|_1 + \lambda_2\|\mathbf{w}\|_2^2 $$ Equivalently, with a single $\lambda$ and mixing parameter $\alpha \in [0,1]$: $$ \lambda\left[\alpha\|\mathbf{w}\|_1 + \frac{1-\alpha}{2}\|\mathbf{w}\|_2^2\right] $$ * $\alpha = 1$ → Lasso * $\alpha = 0$ → Ridge ''Why Both?'' * ''Lasso'' alone struggles when features are highly correlated — selects one arbitrarily * ''Ridge'' alone doesn't produce sparse solutions * Elastic Net: ''groups'' correlated features together (Ridge effect) and sets small groups to zero (Lasso effect) ''Geometry'' The constraint region is a rounded diamond — corners on axes (sparsity) but with smooth edges (stability under correlation). ''When to Use'' * $p > n$ (high-dimensional, few samples) * Many correlated features (genomics, text features) * Want interpretability with stability <<button "Quiz: What does the $L^2$ term add to Lasso?">> <<replace "#en-q">> ''✓ Stability. When features are correlated, Lasso randomly picks one and ignores others. The $L^2$ term encourages correlated features to be selected together with similar coefficients.'' [[Lasso]] | [[Ridge regression]] | [[L1]] | [[L2]] | [[Regularization]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="en-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> <img src="assets/loss_functions.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="Loss functions diagram"/> ! Loss Functions !! Why the Choice of Loss Matters Every machine learning model is ultimately an optimization: adjust parameters until predictions are "close enough" to truth. But ''close enough'' must be defined mathematically. The loss function is that definition — it converts prediction errors into a single number the optimizer can minimize. Choose the wrong loss and your model will optimize for the wrong thing. Mean squared error on noisy labels? The model learns to fit the outliers. Cross-entropy on class-imbalanced data? It learns to predict the majority class. Loss function choice is a modeling decision, not an afterthought. !! Regression Losses ''Mean Squared Error (MSE)'': $L = \frac{1}{n}\sum_i (y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2$ Squaring amplifies large errors. The model is strongly punished for any single bad prediction, which is desirable when outliers matter. Mathematically convenient — smooth everywhere with a clean gradient. ''Mean Absolute Error (MAE)'': $L = \frac{1}{n}\sum_i |y_i - \hat{y}_i|$ Linear in error magnitude. Much less sensitive to outliers — a prediction that's off by 10 contributes exactly 10× more than one off by 1. The gradient is ±1 everywhere (except zero, which is non-differentiable) — the model doesn't work harder on large errors. ''Huber Loss'': Quadratic when $|e| \leq \delta$, linear beyond. Best of both worlds: smooth and strongly-penalizing near the origin, robust to outliers at the tails. !! Classification Losses ''Binary Cross-Entropy'': $L = -[y\log\hat{p} + (1-y)\log(1-\hat{p})]$ For yes/no predictions. The $\log$ creates an asymptote at zero probability — the model is punished infinitely for being completely wrong and confident. This drives predictions away from certainty unless truly warranted. ''Categorical Cross-Entropy'': $L = -\sum_c y_c \log\hat{p}_c$ Generalizes to $k$ classes. The "true class" probability is the only term that survives (other $y_c = 0$), so the loss is $-\log\hat{p}_{true}$. Every unit increase in predicted probability for the true class decreases loss by $1/\hat{p}_{true}$. ''Hinge Loss'' (SVM): $L = \max(0, 1 - y\hat{f})$ Zero when the prediction is correct ''and'' confident (margin $> 1$). Flat beyond the margin — perfectly correct predictions incur no gradient, so only the hard examples drive learning. !! Information-Theoretic View Cross-entropy is the expected number of bits needed to encode the true label using the model's predicted distribution. Minimizing it is equivalent to maximizing the likelihood of observing the training data — the foundation of maximum likelihood estimation. KL divergence $D_{KL}(p\|q) = H(p,q) - H(p)$ measures how much extra information $q$ requires compared to the true distribution $p$. Since $H(p)$ is constant, minimizing cross-entropy = minimizing KL divergence. !! Technical Details | Loss | Use case | Sensitive to outliers? | |------|----------|------------------------| | MSE | Regression | Yes (squares errors) | | MAE | Robust regression | No | | Huber | Robust regression | No (beyond δ) | | Binary CE | Binary classification | Yes (log) | | Categorical CE | Multi-class | Yes | | Hinge | SVM / margin classifiers | No (flat beyond margin) | Gradient of MSE: $\partial L/\partial\hat{y} = -2(y-\hat{y})/n$ Gradient of CE: $\partial L/\partial\hat{p} = -y/\hat{p}$ (diverges as $\hat{p}\to 0$) !! Quiz <<button "Q1: Why does MSE penalize outliers more than MAE?">><<replace "#lf-q1">>MSE squares the error, so an error of 10 contributes 100 to the loss while MAE contributes only 10. This makes MSE pull the model strongly toward fitting even rare large errors.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lf-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: What does minimizing cross-entropy mean probabilistically?">><<replace "#lf-q2">>It maximizes the likelihood of the training data under the model's predicted distribution — equivalent to making the model assign the highest possible probability to the observed labels.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lf-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: Why does hinge loss produce sparse updates?">><<replace "#lf-q3">>Once a prediction is correct with margin > 1, hinge loss is exactly zero and its gradient is zero. Only misclassified or low-confidence examples produce non-zero gradients.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lf-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: When would you prefer MAE over MSE?">><<replace "#lf-q4">>When your data contains outliers you don't trust — sensor noise, mislabeled examples, extreme values. MAE treats all errors proportionally rather than amplifying large ones.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lf-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: What happens to cross-entropy loss when the model is completely wrong and confident?">><<replace "#lf-q5">>It approaches infinity. As predicted probability for the true class approaches zero, −log(p̂) → ∞. This infinite penalty prevents the model from being confidently wrong.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="lf-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Lagrangian Duality'' A technique that converts a constrained optimisation problem into an unconstrained one by incorporating constraints as penalty terms. ''Primal Problem'' $$ \min_\mathbf{w}\; f(\mathbf{w}) \quad \text{s.t.} \quad g_i(\mathbf{w}) \leq 0,\; h_j(\mathbf{w}) = 0 $$ ''Lagrangian'' $$ \mathcal{L}(\mathbf{w}, \boldsymbol{\alpha}, \boldsymbol{\nu}) = f(\mathbf{w}) + \sum_i \alpha_i g_i(\mathbf{w}) + \sum_j \nu_j h_j(\mathbf{w}) $$ $\alpha_i \geq 0$: Lagrange multipliers for inequality constraints. ''Dual Problem'' $$ \max_{\boldsymbol{\alpha} \geq 0, \boldsymbol{\nu}}\; d(\boldsymbol{\alpha},\boldsymbol{\nu}) = \max_{\boldsymbol{\alpha},\boldsymbol{\nu}}\;\min_\mathbf{w}\; \mathcal{L}(\mathbf{w},\boldsymbol{\alpha},\boldsymbol{\nu}) $$ The dual is always convex and provides a lower bound on the primal (weak duality). ''Strong Duality'' If Slater's condition holds (primal is feasible and strictly satisfies inequality constraints), primal = dual. Solving the dual solves the primal. ''SVM Connection'' The SVM dual is in terms of Lagrange multipliers $\alpha_i$ — one per training point. The solution is sparse: $\alpha_i > 0$ only for ''support vectors''. The kernel trick is applied in the dual. ''KKT Conditions'' Necessary (and for convex problems, sufficient) optimality conditions: stationarity, primal feasibility, dual feasibility, complementary slackness. <<button "Quiz: In the SVM, what do non-zero Lagrange multipliers correspond to?">> <<replace "#ld-q">> ''✓ Support vectors — the training points that lie on or inside the margin boundary. Only these points influence the decision boundary.'' [[Support Vector Machine]] | [[Convex Optimization]] | [[Primal and Dual]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="ld-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Convex Optimization Convex optimization is the study of minimising convex functions over convex sets. Its importance lies in a single guarantee: any local minimum is a global minimum. ''Convexity'' A function $f$ is convex if for all $x, y$ and $t \in [0,1]$: $$f(tx + (1-t)y) \leq tf(x) + (1-t)f(y)$$ The function lies below the chord between any two points. [[Convex versus Concave]] — visual intuition. ''Why it matters'' For convex problems, gradient descent is guaranteed to converge to the global optimum. No worrying about local minima, saddle points, or initialisation. ''Convex loss functions'' [[Mean Squared Error]] — convex in the weights (for linear models) [[Log Loss]] — convex in the weights (for logistic regression) [[Hinge Loss]] — convex; used in SVMs [[Support Vector Machine]] — the SVM optimisation problem is convex ''Duality and constrained optimisation'' [[Lagrangian Duality]] — every convex optimisation problem has a dual; solving the dual is often easier [[Primal and Dual]] — the two views of the same problem [[Linear Programing]] — linear objective over a convex polytope; always globally solvable ''Deep learning is not convex'' Neural network loss landscapes are highly non-convex. Yet SGD finds good solutions in practice — an active area of theory involving the lottery ticket hypothesis, loss landscape geometry, and implicit regularisation. --- [[Optimization]] ← back | [[Lagrangian Duality]] | [[Support Vector Machine]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Kernel Methods Kernel methods solve the problem of learning in high-dimensional or infinite-dimensional feature spaces without ever explicitly computing those features. They replace dot products in the original space with kernel function evaluations. ''The kernel trick'' [[Kernel Trick]] — if an algorithm only uses the data through dot products $\mathbf{x}_i^\top \mathbf{x}_j$, we can replace those with $K(\mathbf{x}_i, \mathbf{x}_j) = \phi(\mathbf{x}_i)^\top \phi(\mathbf{x}_j)$ for some (possibly infinite-dimensional) feature map $\phi$. ''Common kernels'' - ''Linear'': $K(x,y) = x^\top y$ — no transformation; equivalent to original space - ''Polynomial'': $K(x,y) = (x^\top y + c)^d$ — all polynomial features up to degree $d$ - ''RBF / Gaussian'': $K(x,y) = \exp(-\|x-y\|^2 / 2\sigma^2)$ — infinite-dimensional feature space; most widely used - ''String kernels'' — for sequences, graphs, structured data ''Mercer's theorem'' A function $K$ is a valid kernel if and only if the kernel matrix $\mathbf{K}_{ij} = K(\mathbf{x}_i, \mathbf{x}_j)$ is positive semi-definite for any set of inputs. ''Applications'' [[Support Vector Machine]] — SVMs are the canonical kernel method [[Kernel Trick]] — deeper dive into the mechanics [[PCA]] — kernel PCA extends PCA to nonlinear structure [[Gaussian Distribution]] — the RBF kernel is related to the Gaussian ''Limitations'' Kernel methods scale as $O(n^2)$ to $O(n^3)$ with the number of training points — impractical for large datasets. Deep learning has largely replaced them at scale. --- [[Statistical Machine Learning]] ← back | [[Support Vector Machine]] | [[Kernel Trick]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Maximum Likelihood Estimation MLE finds the parameters $\theta$ that make the observed data most probable. It is the foundation of nearly every classical estimation procedure in statistics and machine learning. ''The principle'' Given data $\mathcal{D} = \{x_1, \ldots, x_n\}$ and a model $p(x \mid \theta)$, maximise: $$\hat{\theta}_{\text{MLE}} = \underset{\theta}{\arg\max} \prod_{i=1}^n p(x_i \mid \theta)$$ In practice we maximise the log-likelihood (equivalent, but sums instead of products): $$\hat{\theta} = \underset{\theta}{\arg\max} \sum_{i=1}^n \log p(x_i \mid \theta)$$ ''MLE and common loss functions'' The choice of distribution implies a loss function: - Gaussian noise → minimise [[Mean Squared Error]] - Bernoulli output → minimise [[Log Loss]] (binary cross-entropy) - Categorical output → minimise [[Cross-entropy]] MLE makes explicit what every loss function assumes about the data-generating process. ''Properties'' - Consistent: converges to the true parameter as $n \to \infty$ - Asymptotically efficient: achieves the Cramér-Rao lower bound - Can overfit with small $n$ — regularisation corresponds to adding a prior ([[Bayesian Methods]]) ''Connections'' [[Bayesian Methods]] — MLE is the special case with a flat (uniform) prior [[Distribtuions]] — you must choose a distributional family before applying MLE [[Expectation Maximization]] — MLE with latent (hidden) variables [[Optimization]] — MLE is an optimisation problem; solved by gradient ascent --- [[Statistics]] ← back | [[Bayesian Methods]] | [[Log Loss]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> <img src="assets/bayesian.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="Bayesian inference diagram"/> ! Bayesian Methods !! Two Philosophies of Probability There are two ways to think about probability. The ''frequentist'' view: probability is the long-run frequency of an event over many repetitions. The ''Bayesian'' view: probability is a degree of belief — how confident you are in something, updated as evidence arrives. These aren't just philosophical differences — they lead to fundamentally different statistical procedures. Bayesian methods maintain a full probability distribution over unknowns, not just a single point estimate. This distribution encodes both the best guess and the uncertainty around it. !! Bayes' Theorem: The Update Rule The engine of Bayesian inference is Bayes' theorem: $P(\theta \mid \mathcal{D}) = \frac{P(\mathcal{D} \mid \theta)\, P(\theta)}{P(\mathcal{D})}$ - $P(\theta)$ — the ''prior'': your beliefs about the parameter before seeing data - $P(\mathcal{D} \mid \theta)$ — the ''likelihood'': how probable is the data if $\theta$ were true? - $P(\theta \mid \mathcal{D})$ — the ''posterior'': updated beliefs after seeing data - $P(\mathcal{D})$ — the ''evidence'': a normalizing constant (often intractable) The posterior combines what you knew before (prior) with what the data tells you (likelihood). With more data, the likelihood dominates and the posterior concentrates regardless of the prior. !! A Concrete Example: Coin Flips You have a coin of unknown fairness $\theta$ = P(heads). Your prior belief: $\theta$ is probably near 0.5, encoded as Beta(2,2) — a gentle peak at 0.5. You observe 3 heads, 1 tail. The posterior is Beta(2+3, 2+1) = Beta(5,3) — shifted toward heads but still uncertain. After 10 heads, 2 tails: Beta(12, 4) — now fairly confident the coin favors heads, posterior concentrated near 0.75. The Beta-Binomial conjugate pair is a beautiful special case: the prior and posterior have the same functional form, making the update analytical. ''Conjugate priors'' exist for many model families. !! From Posterior to Decisions The posterior distribution answers different questions depending on what you need: - ''MAP estimate'': the posterior mode (peak) — equivalent to maximum likelihood + regularization - ''Posterior mean'': the expected value — minimizes mean squared error - ''Credible interval'': the region containing 95% of posterior probability — unlike frequentist confidence intervals, this literally means "θ is in this range with 95% probability" Bayesian predictions integrate over the full posterior rather than committing to one parameter value: $P(y_{new} \mid \mathcal{D}) = \int P(y_{new} \mid \theta) P(\theta \mid \mathcal{D}) \, d\theta$ This ''posterior predictive'' naturally captures parameter uncertainty in predictions. !! Bayesian Machine Learning Bayesian methods extend to neural networks (Bayesian deep learning), giving uncertainty estimates alongside predictions — crucial for medical diagnosis, autonomous driving, and any high-stakes application. ''Gaussian Processes'' are a fully Bayesian approach to regression: instead of learning parameters, place a prior directly over functions and compute the posterior distribution over functions given data. Every prediction comes with a calibrated confidence interval. In practice, exact Bayesian inference is intractable for complex models. ''Variational inference'' approximates the posterior with a simpler distribution; ''Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC)'' samples from it. These methods trade off computational cost against approximation quality. !! Technical Details | Term | Symbol | Description | |------|--------|-------------| | Prior | $P(\theta)$ | Beliefs before data | | Likelihood | $P(\mathcal{D}\|\theta)$ | Probability of data given params | | Posterior | $P(\theta\|\mathcal{D})$ | Updated beliefs | | MAP | $\arg\max_\theta P(\theta\|\mathcal{D})$ | Most probable parameter | | Marginal likelihood | $P(\mathcal{D})$ | Normalizing constant | Conjugate pairs: Beta-Binomial, Dirichlet-Multinomial, Normal-Normal, Gamma-Poisson !!
