Bayesian probability is a subjective degree of belief — how confident you are — which is updated as new evidence arrives. Bayesian methods can assign probabilities to one-time events.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="bay-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: What does the prior represent and how does it affect the posterior?">><<replace "#bay-q2">>The prior encodes your beliefs about parameters before seeing data. With little data, the posterior stays close to the prior. With lots of data, the likelihood dominates and the posterior concentrates near the true value regardless of the prior.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="bay-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: What is a conjugate prior and why is it useful?">><<replace "#bay-q3">>A prior that, when combined with a given likelihood, produces a posterior of the same functional form. For example, Beta prior + Binomial likelihood = Beta posterior. This makes the Bayesian update analytical (no numerical integration needed).<</replace>><</button>> <span id="bay-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: How is a Bayesian credible interval different from a frequentist confidence interval?">><<replace "#bay-q4">>A 95% credible interval literally means "the parameter is in this interval with 95% probability" — a direct probability statement about θ. A 95% confidence interval means "if we repeated this procedure many times, 95% of intervals would contain the true θ" — θ is fixed, not random.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="bay-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: What is the MAP estimate and how does it relate to regularization?">><<replace "#bay-q5">>MAP (Maximum A Posteriori) is the posterior mode — the most probable parameter value given the data. Maximizing the posterior is equivalent to maximizing the likelihood with a penalty term for unlikely parameters. An L2 prior (Gaussian) gives L2 regularization; an L1 prior (Laplace) gives L1 regularization.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="bay-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Clustering Clustering is unsupervised learning — finding groups in data without labels. The algorithm decides which examples belong together based on similarity or density. ''When to use clustering'' - Exploratory analysis: what structure is in my data? - Customer segmentation, document grouping, anomaly detection - Preprocessing: cluster then classify within clusters ''Algorithms'' [[K-means Clustering]] / [[KMeans]] — assign each point to one of $k$ centroids; minimise within-cluster variance: $$\underset{\{\mu_k\}}{\min} \sum_{k=1}^K \sum_{x_i \in C_k} \|x_i - \mu_k\|^2$$ Fast and scalable, but assumes spherical clusters and requires choosing $k$. [[Gaussian Mixture Models]] — soft assignment; each point has a probability of belonging to each cluster; fit via [[Expectation Maximization]] [[Spectral Clustering]] — uses the graph Laplacian; handles non-convex cluster shapes; good for manifold-structured data ''Choosing the number of clusters'' [[Ebow Curve]] — plot within-cluster variance vs. $k$; look for the elbow [[Reconstruction Error]] — gap statistic, silhouette score ''Connections'' [[Lower Dimensional Representation]] — cluster in reduced space for better results [[PCA]] — reduce dimensions before clustering [[Distribtuions]] — GMM is a probabilistic view of clustering [[Expectation Maximization]] — the algorithm that fits GMMs --- [[learning Paradigms]] ← back | [[K-means Clustering]] | [[Gaussian Mixture Models]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> <img src="assets/gmm.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="Gaussian Mixture Model diagram"/> ! Gaussian Mixture Models !! K-Means, Made Probabilistic K-means assigns each point to exactly one cluster. But real data is often ambiguous — a point equidistant between two clusters doesn't firmly belong to either. Gaussian Mixture Models (GMMs) take a softer stance: every point belongs to every cluster, just with different probabilities. A GMM models the data as if it were generated by $K$ Gaussian distributions, each with its own mean and covariance, mixed together with weights $\pi_k$. The result is a rich probabilistic model that can capture clusters of different shapes, sizes, and orientations — unlike K-means, which implicitly assumes spherical clusters. !! The Model The probability density of a data point under a GMM: $p(\mathbf{x}) = \sum_{k=1}^K \pi_k \, \mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x} \mid \boldsymbol{\mu}_k, \boldsymbol{\Sigma}_k)$ The mixing weights $\pi_k \geq 0$ sum to 1 and represent the prior probability of a point belonging to component $k$. Each component is a Gaussian with mean $\boldsymbol{\mu}_k$ and covariance $\boldsymbol{\Sigma}_k$. The ''responsibility'' of component $k$ for point $\mathbf{x}_i$ — how much credit it takes for generating that point: $r_{ik} = \frac{\pi_k \, \mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}_i \mid \boldsymbol{\mu}_k, \boldsymbol{\Sigma}_k)}{\sum_{j} \pi_j \, \mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}_i \mid \boldsymbol{\mu}_j, \boldsymbol{\Sigma}_j)}$ This is the E-step of EM: compute soft assignments. !! Expectation-Maximization (EM) GMMs are fit with the EM algorithm, which alternates between two steps: ''E-step (Expectation)'': Compute responsibilities $r_{ik}$ using the current parameters. Each point is softly assigned to all components. ''M-step (Maximization)'': Update parameters to maximize the expected log-likelihood given those responsibilities: $\boldsymbol{\mu}_k \leftarrow \frac{\sum_i r_{ik} \mathbf{x}_i}{\sum_i r_{ik}}, \quad \pi_k \leftarrow \frac{\sum_i r_{ik}}{n}, \quad \boldsymbol{\Sigma}_k \leftarrow \frac{\sum_i r_{ik}(\mathbf{x}_i - \boldsymbol{\mu}_k)(\mathbf{x}_i - \boldsymbol{\mu}_k)^\top}{\sum_i r_{ik}}$ The log-likelihood is guaranteed to increase (or stay the same) at every EM step. K-means is the hard-assignment limit of GMM-EM, where responsibilities are 0 or 1. !! Covariance Structure The covariance matrix $\boldsymbol{\Sigma}_k$ controls the shape and orientation of each component. ''Full covariance'' allows arbitrary ellipsoids — most flexible, most parameters. ''Diagonal covariance'' assumes independent features — simpler, faster. ''Spherical covariance'' $\sigma_k^2 \mathbf{I}$ — one parameter per component, closest to K-means. Choosing covariance structure is a bias-variance trade-off. Full covariance can model any Gaussian cluster but overfits with little data; spherical is robust but inflexible. !! Model Selection: How Many Components? Unlike K-means, GMMs provide a proper likelihood, enabling principled model comparison. The ''Bayesian Information Criterion'' (BIC) penalizes likelihood by model complexity: $\text{BIC} = -2\log\hat{L} + (\text{num params}) \cdot \log n$ Choose $K$ minimizing BIC. Unlike the elbow method, BIC has a rigorous justification and naturally avoids overfitting. !! Applications Beyond Clustering GMMs are generative models — you can sample new data by sampling a component (according to $\pi_k$) then sampling from that Gaussian. This makes them useful for ''density estimation'', ''anomaly detection'' (low-likelihood points are anomalous), and as prior distributions in Bayesian models. In speech recognition, GMMs historically modeled acoustic feature distributions before deep learning took over. !! Technical Details | Parameter | Dimension | Description | |---------|-----------|-------------| | $\pi_k$ | $K$ | Mixing weights (sum to 1) | | $\boldsymbol{\mu}_k$ | $K \times d$ | Component means | | $\boldsymbol{\Sigma}_k$ | $K \times d \times d$ | Covariance matrices | | $r_{ik}$ | $n \times K$ | Responsibilities (E-step) | EM converges to a local maximum; run multiple times with different initializations (K-means++ init works well). !! Quiz <<button "Q1: What is the key difference between K-means and GMM clustering?">><<replace "#gmm-q1">>K-means makes hard assignments — each point belongs to exactly one cluster. GMMs make soft assignments — each point has a probability of belonging to each component (responsibility), naturally handling ambiguous points near cluster boundaries.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="gmm-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: What are responsibilities in a GMM?">><<replace "#gmm-q2">>r_{ik} = the posterior probability that component k generated data point x_i. Computed in the E-step using Bayes' theorem: it's the fraction of the probability at x_i attributable to component k.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="gmm-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: Why is EM guaranteed to converge for GMMs?">><<replace "#gmm-q3">>Each EM step increases (or maintains) the log-likelihood. The E-step computes a lower bound on the likelihood; the M-step maximizes that bound. Since the likelihood is bounded above (probabilities ≤ 1), the algorithm must converge.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="gmm-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: How does covariance structure affect what shapes of clusters GMM can model?">><<replace "#gmm-q4">>Full covariance allows arbitrary ellipsoidal clusters (any orientation and stretch). Diagonal covariance assumes axis-aligned ellipsoids (independent features). Spherical covariance produces circular/spherical clusters — equivalent to K-means with soft assignments.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="gmm-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: How do you choose K in a GMM, and why is this better than K-means' elbow method?">><<replace "#gmm-q5">>Use BIC or AIC, which balance log-likelihood against model complexity. Since GMMs provide a proper likelihood, you can compare models with different K directly. The elbow method is heuristic and subjective; BIC has a rigorous statistical justification.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="gmm-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> <img src="assets/kmeans.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="K-means clustering diagram"/> ! K-Means Clustering !! Clustering: Learning Without Labels Supervised learning has ground truth labels. But most data in the world has no labels. Clustering asks: can we discover structure in the data itself — groups of points that are somehow similar to each other and different from other groups? K-means is the simplest, fastest, and most widely used clustering algorithm. Given $K$ (the desired number of clusters), it partitions $n$ points into $K$ groups by minimizing total within-cluster variance. No labels required. !! The Algorithm: Assign and Update K-means alternates between two steps until convergence: ''Assignment step'': Assign each point to the nearest centroid. $c_i = \arg\min_k \|\mathbf{x}_i - \boldsymbol{\mu}_k\|^2$ ''Update step'': Move each centroid to the mean of its assigned points. $\boldsymbol{\mu}_k = \frac{1}{|C_k|} \sum_{\mathbf{x}_i \in C_k} \mathbf{x}_i$ Repeat. The algorithm is guaranteed to converge — each step can only decrease (or maintain) the total within-cluster sum of squares. But it may converge to a local minimum, not the global optimum. !! Why It Works (and When It Doesn't) The objective being minimized is the total within-cluster sum of squares (WCSS): $J = \sum_{k=1}^K \sum_{\mathbf{x}_i \in C_k} \|\mathbf{x}_i - \boldsymbol{\mu}_k\|^2$ Each step provably decreases $J$: assignment moves points to nearer centroids (decreasing distance), update moves centroids to the mean (the point minimizing squared distances to a set). K-means assumes clusters are ''spherical and roughly equal-sized''. It struggles with elongated clusters, clusters of very different densities, or non-convex shapes. These limitations motivate alternatives like DBSCAN or Gaussian Mixture Models. !! The K Problem and Initialization You must choose $K$ before running. Common approaches: the ''elbow method'' (plot WCSS vs. K and look for a kink), ''silhouette score'' (measures how tight clusters are vs. how separated), or domain knowledge. Initialization matters greatly. Random initialization can lead to poor local minima. ''K-means++'' solves this: choose the first centroid randomly, then choose each subsequent centroid with probability proportional to its squared distance from the nearest chosen centroid. This spreads initial centroids across the data, almost always finding better solutions. !! Practical Uses and Variants K-means is used everywhere: customer segmentation, image compression (replacing each pixel with its cluster's centroid color), document clustering, anomaly detection, and as a preprocessing step for supervised learning. ''Mini-batch K-means'' updates centroids using small random subsets of data — dramatically faster for large datasets with only slightly worse results. ''Soft K-means'' assigns fractional memberships to clusters rather than hard assignments, blending into Gaussian Mixture Models. !! Technical Details | Property | Value | |---------|-------| | Time complexity | $O(n \cdot K \cdot d \cdot T)$ per run | | Convergence | Guaranteed (to local minimum) | | Initialization | K-means++ recommended | | Hyperparameters | K, initialization, max iterations | | Metric assumed | Euclidean distance | Always run K-means multiple times with different initializations and keep the run with lowest WCSS. !! Quiz <<button "Q1: What objective does K-means minimize?">><<replace "#km-q1">>Total within-cluster sum of squares (WCSS): the sum of squared distances from each point to its assigned centroid. Equivalently, it minimizes total within-cluster variance across all K clusters.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="km-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: Why is K-means guaranteed to converge?">><<replace "#km-q2">>Each step (assignment then update) can only decrease or maintain the WCSS objective. Assignment moves points to nearer centroids (reduces distances); update moves centroids to their cluster mean (the minimizer of squared distances). WCSS is non-negative and strictly decreasing, so the algorithm must stop.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="km-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: What shapes of clusters does K-means struggle with?">><<replace "#km-q3">>K-means assumes spherical, equally-sized clusters (using Euclidean distance to centroids). It fails on elongated clusters, clusters of very different sizes or densities, and non-convex shapes like rings or crescents.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="km-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: What is K-means++ and why use it?">><<replace "#km-q4">>K-means++ initializes centroids spread across the data: the first is random, then each subsequent centroid is chosen with probability proportional to its squared distance from the nearest existing centroid. This avoids poor local minima and typically finds better clusterings than random initialization.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="km-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: How does the elbow method help choose K?">><<replace "#km-q5">>Plot WCSS vs. K. As K increases, WCSS always decreases (more clusters = better fit). The "elbow" is where additional clusters give diminishing returns — the curve bends sharply. That K captures real cluster structure without overfitting.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="km-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Expectation Maximization The EM algorithm finds maximum likelihood estimates when the model has latent (hidden) variables. It alternates between inferring the likely values of the hidden variables and updating parameters to fit those inferences. ''When to use EM'' When you observe $\mathbf{x}$ but the model also involves hidden variables $\mathbf{z}$ — and marginalising out $\mathbf{z}$ is intractable. ''The two steps'' Starting from parameters $\theta^{(t)}$, iterate: ''E-step'' — compute the expected complete-data log-likelihood: $$Q(\theta, \theta^{(t)}) = \mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{z}|\mathbf{x},\theta^{(t)}}[\log p(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{z} \mid \theta)]$$ ''M-step'' — maximise $Q$ with respect to $\theta$: $$\theta^{(t+1)} = \underset{\theta}{\arg\max}\; Q(\theta, \theta^{(t)})$$ EM is guaranteed to increase (or maintain) the marginal log-likelihood $\log p(\mathbf{x}\mid\theta)$ at each iteration. ''Canonical example: Gaussian Mixture Models'' [[Gaussian Mixture Models]] — E-step: compute soft cluster assignments; M-step: update means, covariances, mixing weights. ''Convergence'' EM converges to a local maximum — not necessarily the global one. Sensitive to initialisation. Run multiple times with different starting points. ''Connections'' [[Maximum Likelihood Estimation]] — EM is MLE for models with latent variables [[Bayesian Methods]] — variational EM connects to approximate Bayesian inference [[Clustering]] — EM for GMMs is the probabilistic counterpart of K-means [[Hidden Markov Machine]] — EM (Baum-Welch) is used to train HMMs --- [[Optimization]] ← back | [[Gaussian Mixture Models]] | [[Hidden Markov Machine]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ! Backpropagation and the Chain Rule <img src="assets/backprop.svg" alt="Backpropagation — forward pass (left) and backward gradient flow (right)" style="width:100%;max-width:620px;display:block;margin:16px auto 24px auto;"> Training a neural network means adjusting millions of weights so the output gets closer to the target. To do that, you need to know how much each weight contributed to the error. Backpropagation is the algorithm that computes this — efficiently, for every weight simultaneously — using the chain rule of calculus. --- !! The Problem It Solves After a forward pass, you have a loss value $L$. You need $\partial L / \partial w$ for every weight $w$ in the network. A network with a million weights has a million of these derivatives. Computing each one independently would be impossibly slow. Backprop computes all of them in a single backward pass — roughly the same cost as one forward pass. --- !! The Chain Rule If $L$ depends on $\hat{y}$, which depends on $h$, which depends on $w$, then: $$\frac{\partial L}{\partial w} = \frac{\partial L}{\partial \hat{y}} \cdot \frac{\partial \hat{y}}{\partial h} \cdot \frac{\partial h}{\partial w}$$ This is the chain rule. Backprop applies it systematically, layer by layer, from the output back to the input — reusing intermediate computations so each quantity is calculated only once. --- !! Forward Pass, Then Backward Pass The algorithm has two phases. ''Forward pass:'' feed the input through the network, computing and ''storing'' every intermediate activation. These stored values are needed for the backward pass. ''Backward pass:'' starting at the loss, compute the gradient of $L$ with respect to each layer's output, then use those to compute gradients with respect to the weights. Each layer receives a gradient from the layer ahead of it, multiplies by the local derivative, and passes the result backward. The key insight is that each layer only needs to know two things: the gradient flowing in from the right, and its own local derivative. It doesn't need to know anything about the rest of the network. --- !! What Each Layer Computes For a layer computing $h = \sigma(Wx + b)$: * The ''weight gradient'': $\partial L / \partial W = (\partial L / \partial h) \cdot x^\top$ — used to update $W$ * The ''input gradient'': $\partial L / \partial x = W^\top \cdot (\partial L / \partial h)$ — passed to the previous layer The activation function $\sigma$ contributes its own local derivative $\sigma'$. This is why activation function choice matters: if $\sigma'$ is often zero (saturated sigmoid), gradients stop flowing — the [[vanishing gradient]] problem. --- !! Why It Works at Scale Backprop's efficiency comes from dynamic programming. Each intermediate gradient is computed once and reused. The total computation is $O(\text{parameters})$ — linear in the number of weights — which is why it scales to networks with billions of parameters. Modern deep learning frameworks (PyTorch, JAX) implement backprop automatically via ''automatic differentiation'' (autograd). You define the forward computation; the framework builds a computation graph and runs backprop for you. --- !! Technical Details ''Computational graph:'' each operation in the forward pass becomes a node. Backprop traverses this graph in reverse topological order, accumulating gradients via the chain rule at each node. ''Gradient accumulation:'' when a node has multiple outputs (fan-out), gradients from all downstream paths are summed before passing backward. ''Numerical vs analytic gradients:'' you can verify backprop implementation by comparing to finite-difference estimates: $\partial L / \partial w \approx (L(w+\epsilon) - L(w-\epsilon)) / 2\epsilon$. This is called a gradient check. ''Memory cost:'' the forward pass must store all intermediate activations for use in the backward pass. For very deep networks this is expensive. ''Gradient checkpointing'' trades compute for memory by recomputing activations during the backward pass rather than storing them. --- !! Quiz ''1. Why does backprop need to store activations during the forward pass?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#bp-q1">>'✓ The backward pass uses the chain rule, which requires the local derivatives at each layer. These local derivatives depend on the activations computed during the forward pass (e.g. the derivative of sigmoid at a point depends on the sigmoid value at that point). Without storing them, you would have to recompute the entire forward pass for each gradient.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="bp-q1"></span> ''2. Write out the chain rule for $\partial L / \partial w$ through two layers.'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#bp-q2">>'✓ If $L$ depends on $\hat{y} = \sigma_2(h)$ and $h = \sigma_1(wx)$, then: $\frac{\partial L}{\partial w} = \frac{\partial L}{\partial \hat{y}} \cdot \frac{\partial \hat{y}}{\partial h} \cdot \frac{\partial h}{\partial w} = \frac{\partial L}{\partial \hat{y}} \cdot \sigma_2'(h) \cdot \sigma_1'(wx) \cdot x$. Each factor is a local derivative computed at the stored activation.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="bp-q2"></span> ''3. Why does the vanishing gradient problem occur?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#bp-q3">>'✓ The backward pass multiplies local derivatives across every layer. If activation functions like sigmoid have $\sigma' \in (0, 0.25]$ — always less than 1 — the product shrinks exponentially with depth. After 10 layers, a gradient can be $0.25^{10} \approx 10^{-6}$ of its original value. Early layers receive near-zero gradient and barely update. ReLU ($\sigma' = 1$ for positive inputs) and residual connections largely solve this.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="bp-q3"></span> ''4. What is automatic differentiation, and how does it differ from symbolic differentiation?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#bp-q4">>'✓ Automatic differentiation (autograd) tracks the computation graph at runtime and applies the chain rule numerically at each node — it produces exact gradients, not approximations. Symbolic differentiation (like a CAS) manipulates mathematical expressions to produce a closed-form derivative formula, which can be unwieldy for complex functions. Autograd is efficient because it reuses forward-pass values and never builds a symbolic expression.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="bp-q4"></span> ''5. What is gradient checkpointing and when would you use it?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#bp-q5">>'✓ Gradient checkpointing saves only a subset of activations during the forward pass (the "checkpoints") and recomputes the rest during backprop as needed. It reduces memory usage from $O(\text{layers})$ to $O(\sqrt{\text{layers}})$ at the cost of roughly 33% more compute. Used when training very deep networks or large batch sizes that would otherwise run out of GPU memory.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="bp-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Learning to Rank'' A family of supervised ML tasks that train a model to produce a ranked ordering of items (documents, products, results) relevant to a query. ''Problem Formulations'' * ''Pointwise'': predict a relevance score per item; sort by score. Standard regression. * ''Pairwise'': for each pair $(i,j)$, predict which is more relevant. RankSVM, RankNet. * ''Listwise'': directly optimise the ranking metric over the full list. LambdaMART, ListNet. ''Key Metrics'' * ''NDCG'' (Normalised Discounted Cumulative Gain): measures quality of ranked list with graded relevance, discounting lower positions: $$ \text{DCG}_k = \sum_{i=1}^k \frac{2^{\text{rel}_i} - 1}{\log_2(i+1)} $$ * ''MAP'' (Mean Average Precision): average precision across multiple recall levels * ''MRR'' (Mean Reciprocal Rank): $1/\text{rank of first relevant result}$ ''LambdaMART'' Gradient boosted trees trained on lambda gradients — the gold standard for web search ranking (used by Bing, Yahoo). Lambda gradients approximate NDCG gradients despite NDCG being non-differentiable. ''Applications'' * Web search (document retrieval) * Recommendation systems (item ranking) * Question answering (candidate ranking) * Information retrieval <<button "Quiz: Why is the listwise approach generally preferred over pointwise?">> <<replace "#rank-q">> ''✓ Pointwise methods ignore inter-document relationships and don't optimise the actual ranking metric. Listwise methods can directly (or approximately) optimise metrics like NDCG that measure the quality of the whole ranked list.'' [[Information Thoery]] | [[Embeddings]] | [[Feature Engineering]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="rank-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> Matrix completion refers to the problem of determining missing entries in a partially observed matrix given its observed entries. This problem is tremendously useful in numerous research fields, such as system identification, control, machine learning, data mining, and more prominently, the field of recommender systems. The basic assumption in matrix completion is that though the matrix is partially observed, it has a low-rank structure. This assumption is relatively reasonable in various scenarios. In the case of recommender systems, for instance, a user's preference (the matrix in question) can generally be represented by a small number of factors, indicating that the complete matrix is of a low rank. The challenge in matrix completion is that it's ill-posed as a mathematical problem because many matrices can be completed in a multitude of ways. The common approach to this challenge is regularization. The problem is usually posed as an optimization one: find the matrix of the lowest rank that agrees with the observed entries. One of the most popular methods for matrix completion is Singular Value Thresholding (SVT), applying a soft-thresholding operation to the singular values of the observed entries. Nevertheless, while deceptively simple, matrix completion problems can become quite intricate, particularly when encountering issues such as noise in the data, non-random missing data patterns, or a necessity for online updates. Lastly, it should be noted that the foundational principle of matrix completion relies on the assumption of low-rank approximation, which may not hold true in every practical situation. Therefore, the application of this method requires thoughtful understanding and validation. <<include "Header">> ''Primal and Dual Problems'' Every constrained optimisation problem (the ''primal'') has an associated ''dual'' problem obtained via Lagrangian relaxation. ''Weak Duality'' The dual objective always provides a lower bound on the primal minimum: $$ d^* \leq p^* $$ The gap $p^* - d^* \geq 0$ is the ''duality gap''. ''Strong Duality'' For convex problems satisfying constraint qualifications (e.g. Slater's condition): $d^* = p^*$. Solving either gives the same optimal value. ''Why Solve the Dual?'' * May be easier to solve (fewer variables, simpler structure) * Dual variables have interpretations (shadow prices, support vectors) * Enables the kernel trick in SVMs * Provides certificates of optimality ''LP Duality Example'' Primal: $\min \mathbf{c}^\top \mathbf{x}$ s.t. $A\mathbf{x} \geq \mathbf{b}$, $\mathbf{x} \geq 0$ Dual: $\max \mathbf{b}^\top \mathbf{y}$ s.t. $A^\top \mathbf{y} \leq \mathbf{c}$, $\mathbf{y} \geq 0$ Strong duality holds for linear programs (fundamental theorem of LP). ''Shadow Prices'' Dual variables $\boldsymbol{\lambda}$ measure the rate of change of the optimal primal objective with respect to constraint right-hand sides — how much relaxing a constraint is worth. <<button "Quiz: When does strong duality hold?">> <<replace "#pd-q">> ''✓ For convex problems satisfying constraint qualifications (e.g. Slater's condition: the primal is strictly feasible). In this case, the duality gap is zero and $p^* = d^*$.'' [[Lagrangian Duality]] | [[Convex Optimization]] | [[Support Vector Machine]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="pd-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> Continuous Represent infomation as single value Mean Median Mode Catagorical Represent info as catagory Linear lower dimensional Representation [[Lower Dimensional Representation]] Same size: Drawn from same distribtuion: <<include "Header">> ''Sparsity'' A vector, matrix, or tensor is sparse if most of its entries are zero (or near-zero). Sparsity is a form of structured simplicity. ''Measuring Sparsity'' * $\ell^0$ "norm": $\|\mathbf{w}\|_0 = |\{i : w_i \neq 0\}|$ — count of non-zeros * Sparsity ratio: fraction of zeros * A solution with $k$ non-zeros in $\mathbb{R}^p$ is called $k$-sparse ''Why Sparse Solutions Are Desirable'' * ''Interpretability'': few non-zero features → readable model * ''Computation'': sparse matrix-vector products are $O(\text{nnz})$ not $O(p)$ * ''Memory'': compressed storage formats (CSR, COO) * ''Generalisation'': fewer parameters → regularisation effect ''Inducing Sparsity'' * $L^1$ (Lasso) penalty pushes weights to exactly zero * $L^0$ penalty is NP-hard to optimise directly * Compressed sensing: recover $k$-sparse signal from $m \ll p$ measurements if $m = O(k \log p/k)$ ''Sparse Attention'' Standard attention is $O(n^2)$. Sparse attention patterns (Longformer, BigBird) limit each token to $O(n)$ connections, making long-context models feasible. <<button "Quiz: Why does $L^1$ regularisation promote sparsity while $L^2$ does not?">> <<replace "#sp-q">> ''✓ The $L^1$ penalty has a constant gradient of $\pm 1$ for non-zero weights, pulling them all the way to zero. The $L^2$ gradient shrinks to zero as the weight approaches zero, so it never fully zeroes weights out.'' [[L1]] | [[Lasso]] | [[penalty term]] | [[Kernel Methods]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="sp-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''The Kernel Trick'' Allows algorithms that depend only on inner products to implicitly operate in a high (or infinite) dimensional feature space without ever computing the feature map. ''Key Identity'' For feature map $\phi: \mathcal{X} \to \mathcal{H}$, define the kernel: $$ k(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{x}') = \langle \phi(\mathbf{x}), \phi(\mathbf{x}') \rangle_{\mathcal{H}} $$ The trick: compute $k(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{x}')$ directly without computing $\phi$ — often much cheaper. ''Example: Polynomial Kernel'' $$ k(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{x}') = (\mathbf{x}^\top \mathbf{x}' + c)^d $$ Corresponds to a feature space with all monomials up to degree $d$ — dimension $\binom{n+d}{d}$ — but computed in $O(n)$. ''RBF (Gaussian) Kernel'' $$ k(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{x}') = \exp\!\left(-\frac{\|\mathbf{x}-\mathbf{x}'\|^2}{2\sigma^2}\right) $$ Corresponds to an ''infinite''-dimensional feature space (Taylor expansion). ''Mercer's Theorem'' Any symmetric positive semi-definite function is a valid kernel (corresponds to an inner product in some Hilbert space). ''Algorithms that Kernelise'' SVM, kernel PCA, Gaussian processes, kernel ridge regression — any algorithm using only inner products can be kernelised. <<button "Quiz: What makes the kernel trick 'a trick'?">> <<replace "#kt-q">> ''✓ It computes inner products in a high-dimensional feature space using a simple scalar function, without explicitly constructing the (potentially infinite-dimensional) feature vectors.'' [[Kernel Methods]] | [[Support Vector Machine]] | [[inner product]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="kt-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Optimization Optimization is the process of finding parameters that minimize (or maximize) an objective function. In machine learning, we minimize a loss function over training data. Almost every ML algorithm is, at its core, a particular approach to optimization. ''Foundations'' [[Convex Optimization]] — when the loss landscape has a guaranteed global minimum [[Convex versus Concave]] — why convexity makes optimization tractable [[Lagrangian Duality]] — constrained optimization via dual problems [[Linear Programing]] — optimization with linear objective and constraints [[Primal and Dual]] — two views of the same optimization problem ''Gradient-based methods'' [[Stocastic Gradient Descent]] — the workhorse of deep learning; mini-batch updates [[adam]] — adaptive learning rates; momentum and variance correction [[sgd]] — pure stochastic gradient descent [[exploding gradient]] / [[vanishing gradient]] — instabilities in deep network training [[hessian]] — second-order information; used in Newton's method ''Landscape and convergence'' [[Convex]] — one valley; gradient descent is guaranteed to find it [[Concave]] — one peak; maximization problems [[objective function]] — what we are actually minimizing [[penalty term]] — adding constraints as soft penalties to the objective [[Closed Form Solution]] — when we can skip iteration entirely ''Practical tools'' [[Hyperparameter search]] — finding learning rate, batch size, schedule [[grid search]] — exhaustive search over hyperparameter combinations [[automl]] — automated hyperparameter and architecture search --- [[Branches of Mathmatics]] ← back | [[Stocastic Gradient Descent]] | [[Convex Optimization]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Synthetic Control'' A causal inference method for estimating the effect of a treatment (policy, event) applied to a single unit, by constructing a ''synthetic counterfactual'' from a weighted combination of untreated units. ''Setup'' One treated unit (e.g. California after a tax policy), several control units (other US states). No randomisation possible. ''Method'' Find weights $w_j \geq 0$, $\sum_j w_j = 1$ such that the pre-treatment outcomes of the synthetic control match those of the treated unit: $$ \hat{y}_{0t} = \sum_j w_j y_{jt} $$ Post-treatment: the gap $y_{1t} - \hat{y}_{0t}$ estimates the treatment effect. ''Identification'' Credible if the synthetic unit closely matches the treated unit in the pre-period and the parallel trends assumption holds in absence of treatment. ''Placebo Tests'' Apply the method to each control unit as if it were treated — the treated unit's gap should stand out as unusually large (p-value analogue). ''Connection to ML'': essentially a constrained linear regression problem; modern extensions use LASSO or matrix completion to handle many control units. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Beta Distribution'' A continuous distribution on $[0,1]$ — the natural choice for modelling probabilities and proportions. $$ p(x; \alpha, \beta) = \frac{x^{\alpha-1}(1-x)^{\beta-1}}{B(\alpha,\beta)}, \quad x \in [0,1] $$ where $B(\alpha,\beta) = \frac{\Gamma(\alpha)\Gamma(\beta)}{\Gamma(\alpha+\beta)}$ is the normalising constant. ''Parameters and Shapes'' | $\alpha, \beta$ | Shape | | $\alpha = \beta = 1$ | Uniform | | $\alpha = \beta > 1$ | Bell-shaped, centred at 0.5 | | $\alpha > \beta$ | Skewed right (mass near 1) | | $\alpha < 1, \beta < 1$ | U-shaped (bimodal) | ''Moments'' $$ E[X] = \frac{\alpha}{\alpha+\beta}, \quad \text{Var}(X) = \frac{\alpha\beta}{(\alpha+\beta)^2(\alpha+\beta+1)} $$ ''Conjugate Prior for Bernoulli/Binomial'' If prior is Beta$(\alpha, \beta)$ and we observe $k$ successes in $n$ trials: $$ \text{Posterior} = \text{Beta}(\alpha + k,\; \beta + n - k) $$ This closed-form update makes Beta/Binomial the canonical Bayesian model. $\alpha$ and $\beta$ can be interpreted as prior pseudo-counts. ''Applications'' Thompson sampling (multi-armed bandit), Bayesian A/B testing, topic model hyperparameters (Dirichlet is multivariate Beta), click-through rate modelling. <<button "Quiz: Why is the Beta distribution a conjugate prior for a coin flip?">> <<replace "#beta-q">> ''✓ Because Beta × Binomial likelihood = Beta posterior. The update rule is simple: add successes to $\alpha$ and failures to $\beta$. Conjugacy means we stay in the same family, enabling closed-form Bayesian inference.'' [[Bayesian Methods]] | [[Bayes&#39; Theorem]] | [[Distribtuions]] | [[Multi Armed Bandit]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="beta-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Measures and Distance Metrics'' A ''distance'' (metric) satisfies: non-negativity, symmetry, triangle inequality, and identity of indiscernibles. ''Common Distances'' | Metric | Formula | Notes | | Euclidean ($L^2$) | $\sqrt{\sum(x_i-y_i)^2}$ | Standard; sensitive to scale | | Manhattan ($L^1$) | $\sum|x_i-y_i|$ | Robust to outliers | | Chebyshev ($L^\infty$) | $\max|x_i-y_i|$ | Max single-dimension diff | | Cosine | $1 - \frac{\mathbf{x}\cdot\mathbf{y}}{\|\mathbf{x}\|\|\mathbf{y}\|}$ | Angle; scale-invariant | | Mahalanobis | $\sqrt{(x-y)^\top \Sigma^{-1}(x-y)}$ | Accounts for correlations | | Hamming | Count of differing positions | Binary strings; DNA | | Edit (Levenshtein) | Min insert/delete/substitute ops | Strings | ''Information-Theoretic Divergences'' Not metrics (asymmetric or violate triangle inequality): * [[KL Divergence]]: $D_{KL}(P\|Q) = \sum p \log(p/q)$ * Jensen-Shannon: symmetric, bounded version of KL * Wasserstein: optimal transport distance; used in [[GAN]]s ''Choosing a Measure'' The right distance depends on what "similar" means for your domain — not always Euclidean. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Cosine Similarity'' Measures the cosine of the angle between two vectors — the most widely used similarity measure in NLP and information retrieval. $$ \cos(\theta) = \frac{\mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{v}}{\|\mathbf{u}\|\,\|\mathbf{v}\|} = \frac{\sum_i u_i v_i}{\sqrt{\sum_i u_i^2}\,\sqrt{\sum_i v_i^2}} \in [-1, 1] $$ ''Interpretation'' * $+1$: identical direction (parallel) * $0$: orthogonal (no similarity) * $-1$: opposite direction ''Why Cosine Instead of Euclidean?'' Cosine is invariant to vector magnitude — a document with 1000 words and one with 100 words can have the same cosine similarity if they discuss the same topics. Euclidean distance would be large. ''Applications'' * Semantic search: find documents near a query embedding * Duplicate detection * Recommendation: user-item similarity * Word analogy: $\cos(\text{king}-\text{man}+\text{woman}, \text{queen}) \approx 1$ * Contrastive learning objectives (SimCSE, CLIP) ''Cosine Distance'' $d(\mathbf{u},\mathbf{v}) = 1 - \cos(\theta)$ — ranges $[0, 2]$ for real vectors. Not a proper metric (triangle inequality may fail). <<button "Quiz: Why is cosine similarity preferred over Euclidean distance for text?">> <<replace "#cs-q">> ''✓ Cosine similarity is invariant to vector length — it captures directional similarity regardless of document length. Long documents would always appear far from short ones under Euclidean distance.'' [[Embeddings]] | [[Word Embeddings]] | [[inner product]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="cs-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Norms'' A norm $\|\cdot\|$ measures the ''length'' or ''size'' of a vector (or matrix). Formally it must satisfy: 1. Non-negativity: $\|\mathbf{x}\| \geq 0$, $= 0$ iff $\mathbf{x}=0$ 2. Homogeneity: $\|c\mathbf{x}\| = |c|\|\mathbf{x}\|$ 3. Triangle inequality: $\|\mathbf{x}+\mathbf{y}\| \leq \|\mathbf{x}\|+\|\mathbf{y}\|$ ''$L^p$ Norms'' $$ \|\mathbf{x}\|_p = \left(\sum_i |x_i|^p\right)^{1/p} $$ | Norm | Formula | Shape of unit ball | | $L^1$ | $\sum |x_i|$ | Diamond (Lasso sparsity) | | $L^2$ | $\sqrt{\sum x_i^2}$ | Sphere (Ridge shrinkage) | | $L^\infty$ | $\max |x_i|$ | Cube | ''Matrix Norms'' * ''Frobenius'': $\|A\|_F = \sqrt{\sum_{ij} a_{ij}^2}$ — analogous to $L^2$ for vectors * ''Spectral'': $\|A\|_2 = \sigma_\max(A)$ — largest singular value; used in spectral normalisation for GANs * ''Nuclear'': $\|A\|_* = \sum_k \sigma_k$ — promotes low-rank solutions ''ML Context'' Regularisation adds a norm penalty to the loss. The choice of norm determines which structure is encouraged (sparsity vs. smoothness). <<button "Quiz: Why does the $L^1$ norm promote sparsity?">> <<replace "#norms-q">> ''✓ The $L^1$ ball is a diamond; its corners lie on axes. The loss function's level sets tend to touch the diamond at a corner where all but one coordinate is zero — producing sparse solutions.'' [[L1]] | [[L2]] | [[Regularization]] | [[inner product]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="norms-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> The Manhattan Distance, or L1-norm, is a metric extensively deployed in several fields of study, primarily in mathematics and computer science. It provides a quantitative measure of distances between vectors in a grid-based system, specifically in a one-dimensional space. The fundamental principle on which it is based is akin to the city block distances in a geographically laid out urban grid, such as those observed in the infrastructure of Manhattan, New York, hence its nomenclature. For any two points in an N-dimensional vector space with coordinates (x1, x2, ..., xN) and (y1, y2, ..., yN), the Manhattan distance is computed as the sum of the absolute differences between the corresponding elements of these two vectors. Mathematically, it is represented as: D = Σ |xi - yi| For i = 1 to N A significant attribute of this distance measure is its reflection of reality in certain circumstances more accurately than other distance metrics. An illustrative example would be within the confines of machine learning algorithms, where it assists in calculating proximity differently than Euclidean distance in high-dimensional spaces. It is pertinent to note its exceptional utility in applications involving cases where movement is restricted to a grid or across blocks, rather than unrestricted, direct paths. For example, in routing or pathfinding problems placed within a grid-based or geographical system. In conclusion, the Manhattan Distance is an essential mathematical concept with wide applicability and has fundamental relevance in several academic and professional fields, including but not limited to computer science, data mining, chemistry, and city planning.<<include "Header">> ## Information Theory Information theory quantifies information, uncertainty, and communication. Founded by Claude Shannon in 1948, it provides the mathematical language for talking about what a model learns and how much it can compress. ''Entropy'' The entropy of a random variable $X$ measures its average uncertainty: $$H(X) = -\sum_x P(x) \log_2 P(x) \quad \text{(bits)}$$ High entropy = high uncertainty = more information per observation. Uniform distribution over $n$ outcomes has entropy $\log_2 n$ bits. ''Cross-entropy'' [[Cross-entropy]] — the average number of bits needed to encode samples from $P$ using a code optimised for $Q$: $$H(P, Q) = -\sum_x P(x) \log Q(x)$$ Minimising cross-entropy (our training loss) is equivalent to making $Q$ as close to $P$ as possible. ''KL Divergence'' [[KL Divergence]] — $D_{KL}(P\|Q) = H(P,Q) - H(P)$: the extra bits needed because we use $Q$ instead of $P$. ''Mutual Information'' [[Mutual Information]] — $I(X;Y) = H(X) - H(X\mid Y)$: how much knowing $Y$ reduces uncertainty about $X$. Key in feature selection and representation learning. ''Applications in ML'' - Loss functions: cross-entropy is the standard classification loss - [[Decision trees]] — split on the feature that maximises information gain (reduction in entropy) - Representation learning — good representations maximise mutual information with labels - [[Compression]] — entropy sets the theoretical minimum code length --- [[Branches of Mathmatics]] ← back | [[KL Divergence]] | [[Mutual Information]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Mutual Information'' Measures how much knowing one variable reduces uncertainty about another — the most general measure of statistical dependence. $$ I(X;Y) = \sum_{x,y} p(x,y) \log \frac{p(x,y)}{p(x)p(y)} $$ For continuous variables: replace sums with integrals. ''Interpretations'' * $I(X;Y) = H(X) - H(X|Y)$ — reduction in entropy of $X$ given $Y$ * $I(X;Y) = H(X) + H(Y) - H(X,Y)$ — overlap in individual entropies * $I(X;Y) = D_{KL}(p(x,y) \| p(x)p(y))$ — KL divergence from independence ''Properties'' * $I(X;Y) \geq 0$, equals 0 iff $X \perp Y$ * Symmetric: $I(X;Y) = I(Y;X)$ * Invariant to bijective transformations of $X$ or $Y$ ''vs Correlation'' Correlation measures only linear dependence. MI captures any dependence — including non-linear, discontinuous, or complex relationships. ''ML Applications'' * Feature selection: select features with high $I(X_i; Y)$ * InfoMax principle: train representations to maximise MI with data * MINE (MI Neural Estimator): learn MI in high dimensions * Contrastive learning (SimCLR): maximise MI between augmented views <<button "Quiz: What does $I(X;Y) = 0$ imply?">> <<replace "#mi-q">> ''✓ $X$ and $Y$ are statistically independent — knowing $Y$ provides no information about $X$ and vice versa. This is stronger than zero correlation.'' [[Information Thoery]] | [[KL Divergence]] | [[Feature Engineering]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="mi-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Vladimir Vapnik (1936–)'' Russian-American computer scientist; co-inventor of the Support Vector Machine and VC dimension theory — two of the most foundational contributions to modern ML. ''Key Contributions'' * ''VC dimension'' (Vapnik-Chervonenkis theory, 1971): a combinatorial measure of model complexity that bounds generalisation error via PAC learning theory * ''Support Vector Machine'' (1963 linear; 1992 nonlinear with Bernhard Boser and Isabelle Guyon): maximum-margin classifier with kernel trick * ''Structural Risk Minimisation (SRM)'': framework for trading off training error and model complexity * ''Statistical Learning Theory'' — the book that established the mathematical foundations of ML ''VC Dimension'' The VC dimension of a hypothesis class $\mathcal{H}$ is the largest set of points that $\mathcal{H}$ can shatter (correctly classify in all $2^n$ labellings). Higher VC dimension → more expressive, higher generalisation error bound. ''Legacy'' Vapnik's work at AT&T Bell Labs in the 1990s and later at Facebook AI Research helped ML transition from a heuristic practice to a mathematically rigorous field. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Monty Hall Problem'' A famous probability puzzle that demonstrates how intuition about conditional probability can be spectacularly wrong. ''Setup'' Three doors. Behind one: a car. Behind two: goats. You pick door 1. The host (who knows what's behind each door) opens door 3, revealing a goat. Should you switch to door 2? ''Counterintuitive Answer: Yes — switch!'' Switching wins with probability $\mathbf{2/3}$. Staying wins with probability $\mathbf{1/3}$. ''Why? Bayesian Analysis'' $$ P(\text{car at 2} | \text{host opens 3}) = \frac{P(\text{opens 3}|\text{car at 2}) \cdot P(\text{car at 2})}{P(\text{opens 3})} = \frac{1 \cdot 1/3}{1/2} = \frac{2}{3} $$ The host's action is ''not random'' — he always reveals a goat and never opens your door. This information is transferred to door 2. ''Intuitive Explanation'' If you initially picked the car (1/3 chance), switching loses. If you initially picked a goat (2/3 chance), the host must open the other goat door, so switching wins. ''Lessons for ML'' * Prior information fundamentally changes posterior probabilities * Base rates matter (don't ignore the prior) * Conditional independence assumptions change everything <<button "Quiz: If you always switch, what fraction of the time do you win the car?">> <<replace "#mh-q">> ''✓ 2/3. You win by switching whenever you initially picked a goat (probability 2/3). You lose by switching only when you initially picked the car (probability 1/3).'' [[Bayes&#39; Theorem]] | [[Bayesian Methods]] | [[Expected Value]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="mh-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## KL Divergence KL divergence (Kullback-Leibler divergence) measures how much one probability distribution differs from another. It is not a distance — it is asymmetric — but it is the most important measure of distributional difference in machine learning. ''Definition'' For discrete distributions $P$ and $Q$: $$D_{KL}(P \| Q) = \sum_x P(x) \log \frac{P(x)}{Q(x)}$$ For continuous distributions, replace the sum with an integral. $D_{KL}(P\|Q) \geq 0$, with equality iff $P = Q$. ''Asymmetry'' $D_{KL}(P \| Q) \neq D_{KL}(Q \| P)$ in general. The interpretation matters: - $D_{KL}(P \| Q)$ — "forward KL": $P$ is the truth; penalises $Q$ for putting zero mass where $P$ has mass - $D_{KL}(Q \| P)$ — "reverse KL": $Q$ is what we control; penalises $Q$ for spreading into areas where $P$ is small ''Applications in ML'' - [[Cross-entropy]] — $H(P,Q) = H(P) + D_{KL}(P\|Q)$; minimising cross-entropy is minimising KL - Variational Autoencoders — the regularisation term is $D_{KL}(q(z\mid x) \| p(z))$ - [[Bayesian Methods]] — KL measures how much the posterior differs from the prior - [[Information Thoery]] — KL is the information gained when $Q$ is updated to $P$ ''Connections'' [[Information Thoery]] — KL is defined in terms of entropy and information [[Cross-entropy]] — cross-entropy = entropy + KL divergence [[Distribtuions]] — KL is the standard way to compare two distributions --- [[Information Thoery]] ← back | [[Cross-entropy]] | [[Bayesian Methods]] <<include "Footer">>In the realm of stochastic processes, a Lévy flight is a random walk wherein the step-lengths follow a stable probability distribution, that is, heavy-tailed. The term was first used by Benoît Mandelbrot for a specific definition of the step length distribution. The definition of a Lévy flight incorporates the characteristic of random walking in a space with more than one dimension; in such a scenario, the steps taken are in random, isotropic directions. Extensions of the term have come to include cases of random walks on a discrete grid, apart from a continuous space. Mandelbrot referred to other specific cases of step-length distribution, too: Cauchy flight where the distribution assumes a Cauchy distribution, and Rayleigh flight where it takes a normal distribution form -- though the latter doesn't fall under the heavy-tailed probability distribution category. The particular instance where Mandelbrot appointed the term "Lévy flight" is described by the survivor function of the step-length distribution, U, being Pr(U > u) = 1 : u < 1 , u^−D : u ≥ 1 . Here, D is a fractal dimension-related parameter and the distribution is a specific application of the Pareto distribution [^1]. [^1]: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L%C3%A9vy_flight <<include "Header">> ''A* Search'' An informed graph search algorithm that finds the shortest path from a start node to a goal by combining the actual path cost with a heuristic estimate of remaining cost. ''Evaluation Function'' $$ f(n) = g(n) + h(n) $$ * $g(n)$: actual cost from start to node $n$ * $h(n)$: heuristic estimate from $n$ to goal * $f(n)$: total estimated cost through $n$ ''Admissibility and Optimality'' If $h(n)$ is ''admissible'' (never overestimates the true cost: $h(n) \leq h^*(n)$), then A* is guaranteed to find the optimal path. ''Consistency (Monotonicity)'' $h(n) \leq c(n,n') + h(n')$ for every edge $(n,n')$. Consistency implies admissibility and efficient closed-list management. ''Common Heuristics'' * Grid navigation: Manhattan distance ($L^1$), Euclidean distance ($L^2$) * 8-puzzle: number of misplaced tiles (admissible but weak) * Routing: straight-line distance as $h$ ''Complexity'' Time/space: $O(b^d)$ worst case where $b$ = branching factor, $d$ = depth. Good heuristics dramatically reduce the effective search space. ''ML Connections'' * Beam search in sequence generation: approximate A* with limited beam width * Monte Carlo Tree Search (AlphaGo): combines search with learned value/policy networks * Planning in model-based RL <<button "Quiz: What property must a heuristic have for A* to guarantee optimal solutions?">> <<replace "#astar-q">> ''✓ Admissibility: $h(n) \leq h^*(n)$ — the heuristic must never overestimate the true cost to the goal. This ensures A* never rules out the optimal path prematurely.'' [[Dynamic Programing]] | [[Search]] | [[Reinforcement Learning]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="astar-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''The Inspection Paradox (Recruitment / Length-Biased Sampling)'' When you sample from a population where individuals have varying sizes/durations, you are more likely to catch larger or longer ones — creating systematic bias. ''Classic Example'' Bus arrivals are Poisson with average 10-minute intervals. A random passenger arrives at a random time. Their wait is ''not'' 5 minutes on average — it is 10 minutes, because they are more likely to arrive during a long gap. ''Length-Biased Sampling'' If you ask "how long have you been at this job?" in a cross-sectional survey, long-tenured employees are overrepresented — those who left quickly are not present to survey. $$ E[\text{observed size}] = E[X] + \frac{\text{Var}(X)}{E[X]} $$ ''ML/Data Science Relevance'' * User engagement surveys: heavy users more likely to respond → overestimate typical usage * Survival analysis: patients enrolled mid-study already survived to enrollment (left truncation) * Network sampling: random walk on a graph visits high-degree nodes more often — degree distribution is biased * Cohort effects in A/B tests: users enrolled early may behave differently ''Correction'': inverse-probability weighting; explicit survival/truncation models. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''One-Shot Learning'' Learning to recognise a new class from a ''single'' labelled example — the extreme case of few-shot learning. ''Challenge'' A single example contains almost no statistical information. Standard deep learning requires thousands of examples to learn a new class reliably. ''Siamese Networks'' Train a twin-network to answer: "are these two images the same class?" $$ f_\theta(\mathbf{x}_1), f_\theta(\mathbf{x}_2) \to \text{distance} \to \text{same/different} $$ Same parameters $\theta$ for both branches. At test time: compare the query to the single support example. ''One-Shot via Meta-Learning (MAML)'' Explicitly train the model to learn from one example: use many episodes of one-shot tasks, meta-update so that one gradient step on a single support example achieves good accuracy. ''Matching Networks'' Learn an embedding + attention-weighted nearest neighbour. The label is a weighted combination of support labels, with weights from a soft attention over support embeddings. ''Zero-Shot Extension'' Zero-shot learning: classify into classes never seen during training using class attribute vectors (e.g. "a zebra is a striped horse"). Common in NLP with language model prompting. ''Applications'' * Face verification (FaceNet, DeepFace): recognise a person from one photo * Medical imaging: rare diseases with few cases * Drug discovery: new molecular targets <<button "Quiz: How do Siamese networks enable one-shot recognition?">> <<replace "#osl-q">> ''✓ They learn a similarity metric rather than a classifier. At test time, compute the similarity between the query and the single support example — no retraining needed, just a forward pass comparison.'' [[Few Shot learning]] | [[Transfer Learning]] | [[Feature Learning]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="osl-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''ANCOVA — Analysis of Covariance'' Extends ANOVA by including continuous control variables (covariates), increasing statistical power and removing confounding. ''Model'' $$ y_{ij} = \mu + \tau_i + \beta(x_{ij} - \bar{x}) + \varepsilon_{ij} $$ * $\tau_i$: effect of group $i$ * $\beta(x_{ij}-\bar{x})$: linear adjustment for covariate $x$ * Assumes: covariate effect is the same across groups (homogeneity of slopes) ''Why Use It?'' * Removes variance explained by the covariate, leaving less residual error → more power * Controls for pre-existing group differences (e.g. pre-test scores before treatment) * Equivalent to linear regression with both categorical and continuous predictors ''Heterogeneity of Treatment Effects'' If the slope $\beta$ differs across groups, the homogeneity assumption is violated — run separate regressions or add an interaction term. This leads to the study of [[heterogeneity of treatment effects]]. ''ML connection'': ANCOVA is linear regression; the "covariate adjustment" idea extends to doubly-robust estimation and causal ML (DML — Double Machine Learning). <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Heterogeneity of Treatment Effects (HTE)'' The treatment effect varies across individuals or subgroups — the average treatment effect (ATE) hides important structure. ''Average vs Conditional Effects'' * ATE: $\tau = E[Y(1) - Y(0)]$ * CATE (Conditional Average Treatment Effect): $\tau(x) = E[Y(1)-Y(0)|X=x]$ The CATE is a function of covariates $x$ — the goal is to learn who benefits most. ''Why It Matters'' * Medical: a drug may help older patients but harm younger ones * Policy: an education intervention may work for disadvantaged children but not others * Marketing: discounts increase purchase for price-sensitive customers only ''Methods for Estimating CATE'' * ''Causal Forest'' (Wager & Athey 2018): random forests adapted for causal inference; honest trees, cross-fitting * ''Meta-learners'': S-learner, T-learner, X-learner — different ways to combine ML models for CATE * ''Double ML'' (Chernozhukov): partial out treatment and outcome using ML, then regress residuals ''Validation'' No ground truth CATE exists. Use uplift curves, Qini coefficient, or AUUC to evaluate. Subgroup analysis and placebo tests help. <<include "Footer">> ceteris paribus: all other things equal to read “Randomized Controlled Trials—A Matter of Design” (Spieth et al.), Trustworthy Online Controlled Experiments (Kohavi et al.), Emily Robinson’s “Guidelines for A/B Testing” “Mostly Harmless Randomization Checking”, “Does the ‘Table 1 Fallacy’ Apply if It Is Table S1 Instead?”, “Silly Significance Tests: Balance Tests”.<<include "Header">> ## Q-Learning Q-learning is a model-free RL algorithm that learns the value of taking action $a$ in state $s$ — the Q-function — without needing a model of the environment. ''The Q-function'' $$Q^*(s, a) = \mathbb{E}\!\left[R_{t+1} + \gamma \max_{a'} Q^*(S_{t+1}, a') \mid S_t=s, A_t=a\right]$$ $Q^*(s,a)$ is the expected discounted return from taking action $a$ in state $s$ and then acting optimally. ''The update rule'' After observing $(s, a, r, s')$: $$Q(s,a) \leftarrow Q(s,a) + \alpha\left[r + \gamma \max_{a'} Q(s', a') - Q(s,a)\right]$$ The term in brackets is the ''TD error'' — how wrong the current estimate is. ''Off-policy learning'' Q-learning is off-policy: it learns the optimal policy regardless of what policy generated the experience. This allows learning from replayed or old data. ''Deep Q-Learning'' [[Deep Q Learning]] — replace the Q-table with a neural network. Used in Atari games (DeepMind DQN, 2015). Key tricks: experience replay, target network. ''Connections'' [[bellman equation]] — the Q-update is the Bellman optimality equation applied iteratively [[Markov Decision Process]] — Q-learning solves MDPs without knowing $P$ or $R$ [[SARSA]] — on-policy variant: uses $(s,a,r,s',a')$ instead of $\max_{a'} Q(s',a')$ [[Q Learning Code Example]] — implementation reference --- [[Reinforcement Learning]] ← back | [[Deep Q Learning]] | [[bellman equation]] <<include "Footer">>! Research interviews often cover context and intuition that papers omit. [[Home]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Mean Squared Error MSE is the most common regression loss function. It measures the average squared difference between predictions and true values. ''Definition'' $$\mathcal{L}_{\text{MSE}} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2$$ ''Properties'' - Always non-negative; zero only when predictions are perfect - Squaring penalises large errors disproportionately — a residual of 10 costs 100× more than one of 1 - Sensitive to outliers: a single large error can dominate the loss - Differentiable everywhere — convenient for [[Stocastic Gradient Descent]] ''Probabilistic interpretation'' Minimising MSE is equivalent to [[Maximum Likelihood Estimation]] under the assumption that residuals are Gaussian: $$y_i = f(x_i) + \epsilon_i, \quad \epsilon_i \sim \mathcal{N}(0, \sigma^2)$$ ''Alternatives'' - ''MAE'' (Mean Absolute Error): $\frac{1}{n}\sum|y_i - \hat{y}_i|$ — robust to outliers; not differentiable at 0 - ''Huber loss'': MSE for small errors, MAE for large ones — best of both worlds - [[Log Loss]] — use instead of MSE when predicting probabilities ''Try it'' [[Linear Regression Quiz]] — test your understanding of how MSE penalises outliers --- [[Loss Functions]] ← back | [[Linear Regression]] | [[Maximum Likelihood Estimation]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Cross-Entropy Cross-entropy measures the difference between two probability distributions — specifically, how many bits are needed to encode samples from the true distribution $P$ using a code optimised for $Q$. ''Definition'' $$H(P, Q) = -\sum_x P(x) \log Q(x)$$ In the binary case (two classes): $$\mathcal{L} = -\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n \bigl[y_i \log \hat{p}_i + (1-y_i)\log(1-\hat{p}_i)\bigr]$$ ''Relation to KL divergence'' $$H(P,Q) = H(P) + D_{KL}(P \| Q)$$ Since $H(P)$ is fixed (it depends only on the true labels), minimising cross-entropy is equivalent to minimising [[KL Divergence]] between the predicted and true distributions. ''Multi-class cross-entropy'' With softmax output $\hat{\mathbf{p}}$ and one-hot true label $\mathbf{y}$: $$\mathcal{L} = -\sum_k y_k \log \hat{p}_k$$ This is the standard loss for classification in all major deep learning frameworks. ''Connections'' [[Log Loss]] — the binary special case of cross-entropy [[KL Divergence]] — cross-entropy = entropy + KL divergence [[Information Thoery]] — cross-entropy has its roots in information theory [[Maximum Likelihood Estimation]] — minimising cross-entropy is MLE under categorical likelihood --- [[Loss Functions]] ← back | [[KL Divergence]] | [[Log Loss]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Hinge Loss Hinge loss is the loss function used by Support Vector Machines. It penalises predictions that are on the wrong side of the decision boundary, and also penalises correct predictions that are too close to the boundary. ''Definition'' For a binary label $y \in \{-1, +1\}$ and score $\hat{y}$: $$\mathcal{L} = \max(0,\, 1 - y \cdot \hat{y})$$ - If $y \cdot \hat{y} \geq 1$: correct and confident — zero loss - If $0 < y \cdot \hat{y} < 1$: correct but not confident — small loss - If $y \cdot \hat{y} < 0$: wrong side — loss grows linearly ''The margin'' The hinge loss enforces a ''margin'' — predictions must not just be correct, but correct by at least 1. This is the geometric maximum-margin principle of the [[Support Vector Machine]]. ''Comparison with log loss'' [[Log Loss]] is smooth and always positive. Hinge loss is zero for well-classified points — it is ''sparse'' in the sense that most training points contribute nothing once they are clearly classified. ''Subgradient'' Hinge loss is not differentiable at $y \cdot \hat{y} = 1$, but has a subgradient: $$\frac{\partial \mathcal{L}}{\partial \hat{y}} = \begin{cases} -y & \text{if } y \cdot \hat{y} < 1 \\ 0 & \text{otherwise} \end{cases}$$ --- [[Loss Functions]] ← back | [[Support Vector Machine]] | [[Log Loss]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Log Loss Log loss (logarithmic loss, binary cross-entropy) is the standard loss function for binary classification. It heavily penalises confident wrong predictions. ''Definition'' $$\mathcal{L} = -\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n \bigl[y_i \log \hat{p}_i + (1-y_i)\log(1-\hat{p}_i)\bigr]$$ where $y_i \in \{0,1\}$ and $\hat{p}_i \in (0,1)$ is the predicted probability. ''Why log?'' Taking the log of probabilities converts the product of likelihoods (which would underflow for large datasets) into a sum. Minimising log loss is identical to [[Maximum Likelihood Estimation]] under a Bernoulli likelihood. ''Behaviour'' - Predicting $\hat{p} = 0.5$ when $y=1$: loss = $\log 2 \approx 0.693$ - Predicting $\hat{p} = 0.9$ when $y=1$: loss = $-\log 0.9 \approx 0.105$ - Predicting $\hat{p} = 0.01$ when $y=1$: loss = $-\log 0.01 \approx 4.6$ — heavily penalised ''Relation to cross-entropy'' Log loss is the binary special case of [[Cross-entropy]]. For multi-class problems, use categorical cross-entropy with a softmax output. ''Connections'' [[Logistic Regression]] — trained by minimising log loss [[sigmoid function]] — converts a score to a probability in $(0,1)$ [[Maximum Likelihood Estimation]] — log loss is the negative log-likelihood of a Bernoulli model --- [[Loss Functions]] ← back | [[Cross-entropy]] | [[Logistic Regression]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> <img src="assets/svm.svg" style="width:100%;max-width:820px;display:block;margin:0 auto 18px;" alt="SVM diagram"/> ! Support Vector Machines !! The Margin Intuition Many hyperplanes can separate two classes. Which one should you choose? Logistic regression picks whichever maximizes the likelihood — which often puts the boundary uncomfortably close to some training points. SVMs take a different stance: find the hyperplane with the ''maximum margin'' — the largest possible gap between the boundary and the nearest training examples. The intuition is compelling: a boundary far from all examples should generalize better to new examples that might fall anywhere nearby. !! Support Vectors and the Margin The training points closest to the decision boundary are the ''support vectors'' — they literally support (define) the optimal hyperplane. All other training points could be removed and the solution wouldn't change. The margin width is $2/\|\mathbf{w}\|$. Maximizing the margin means minimizing $\|\mathbf{w}\|^2$, subject to every training example being correctly classified ''and'' falling outside the margin: $y_i(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_i + b) \geq 1$ for all $i$ This is a quadratic program with a convex objective — it has a unique global optimum. !! Soft Margin: Allowing Violations Real data is rarely perfectly separable. The ''soft-margin SVM'' allows some examples to violate the margin or even be misclassified, penalizing each violation: $\min_{\mathbf{w},b,\boldsymbol{\xi}} \frac{1}{2}\|\mathbf{w}\|^2 + C\sum_i\xi_i \quad\text{subject to}\quad y_i(\mathbf{w}^\top\mathbf{x}_i + b) \geq 1-\xi_i$ The parameter $C$ controls the trade-off: large $C$ → few violations allowed (narrow margin, may overfit); small $C$ → many violations allowed (wide margin, may underfit). This is regularization built directly into the geometry. !! The Kernel Trick SVMs are linear classifiers, but many problems aren't linearly separable. The ''kernel trick'' sidesteps this: instead of mapping features to a high-dimensional space explicitly, use a kernel function $k(\mathbf{x}_i, \mathbf{x}_j)$ that computes the dot product ''as if'' you had mapped to that space — without ever computing the mapping. Common kernels: - ''RBF (Gaussian)'': $k(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{x}') = e^{-\gamma\|\mathbf{x}-\mathbf{x}'\|^2}$ — locally-similar points get high dot products - ''Polynomial'': $k(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{x}') = (\mathbf{x}^\top\mathbf{x}' + c)^d$ — captures feature interactions up to degree $d$ The RBF kernel implicitly maps to an infinite-dimensional space. Points very close together are treated as maximally similar; far-apart points as unrelated. !! Duality and Sparse Solutions The SVM dual formulation expresses the solution in terms of training examples, not just weights. The prediction for a new point depends only on kernel dot products with ''support vectors'': $f(\mathbf{x}) = \sum_i \alpha_i y_i k(\mathbf{x}_i, \mathbf{x}) + b$ Most $\alpha_i = 0$ — only support vectors have non-zero coefficients. This sparsity makes SVMs efficient at test time, regardless of how many training examples exist (only the support vectors matter). !! Technical Details | Quantity | Meaning | |---------|---------| | Margin | $2/\|\mathbf{w}\|$ — gap between class boundaries | | $C$ | Regularization: trade margin width vs. violations | | $\xi_i$ | Slack variable: how much example $i$ violates margin | | Kernel $k$ | Dot product in feature space (never computed explicitly) | | Support vectors | Training points with $\alpha_i > 0$ | SVMs scale as $O(n^2)$–$O(n^3)$ in training (quadratic program over $n$ examples). Preferred when $n$ is moderate and interpretability of the margin is valuable. !! Quiz <<button "Q1: Why does maximizing the margin improve generalization?">><<replace "#svm-q1">>A larger margin means the boundary is far from all training points, so small perturbations in new test data are less likely to cross it. The model is less sensitive to noise near the decision boundary.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="svm-q1"></span> <<button "Q2: What are support vectors and why do only they matter?">><<replace "#svm-q2">>Support vectors are the training points closest to the decision boundary — they define and constrain it. All other points could be removed and the solution would be identical. Only support vectors have non-zero dual coefficients.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="svm-q2"></span> <<button "Q3: What does the C hyperparameter control?">><<replace "#svm-q3">>The trade-off between margin width and training error. Large C penalizes violations heavily → narrower margin, fewer errors, risk of overfitting. Small C allows more violations → wider margin, simpler model, risk of underfitting.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="svm-q3"></span> <<button "Q4: What is the kernel trick and why is it powerful?">><<replace "#svm-q4">>Kernels compute dot products in a high-dimensional (or even infinite-dimensional) feature space without explicitly computing the mapping φ(x). This allows SVMs to learn nonlinear boundaries at the cost of only evaluating k(xᵢ, xⱼ).<</replace>><</button>> <span id="svm-q4"></span> <<button "Q5: Why are SVM predictions sparse?">><<replace "#svm-q5">>The dual solution assigns α_i = 0 to most training examples. Only support vectors (near the margin) have α_i > 0. Predictions use only kernel dot products with support vectors, making inference fast regardless of training set size.<</replace>><</button>> <span id="svm-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Goodhart's Law'' "When a measure becomes a target, it ceases to be a good measure." — Charles Goodhart (1975), popularised by Marilyn Strathern. ''Core Idea'' Optimising a proxy metric creates pressure to game that metric, decoupling it from the underlying goal you actually care about. ''Classic Examples'' * Soviet nail factory: quota by number → produces tiny nails; quota by weight → one giant nail * Test scores: teaching to the test improves scores without improving knowledge * Citation counts: salami-slicing papers to maximise publication count ''ML Manifestations'' * ''Reward hacking'' (RL): agents find unexpected strategies that maximise reward without achieving the intended goal — a boat race agent spins in circles collecting bonuses instead of racing * ''Metric gaming'' (NLP): optimising BLEU for translation can produce grammatical but nonsensical text * ''Specification gaming'': any gap between the proxy loss and the true objective will be exploited at sufficient capability ''Formal Decomposition (Krakovna et al.)'' Goodhart failure = proxy misspecification + optimisation pressure. More capable optimisers find Goodhart failures more reliably. ''Mitigations'' * Use multiple diverse metrics * Human evaluation alongside automated metrics * RLHF: use human preferences instead of a fixed reward function * Red-teaming: actively search for reward hacking before deployment <<button "Quiz: What is 'reward hacking' in RL?">> <<replace "#gl-q">> ''✓ An agent finds a strategy that achieves high reward according to the specified reward function but fails to accomplish the intended task — exploiting the gap between the proxy reward and the true goal.'' [[Reinforcement Learning]] | [[policy gradiant]] | [[Proximal Policy Optimization ]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="gl-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> Active Inference is a theoretical framework in neuroscience and cognitive science that states all living organisms act to minimize the divergence between their expectations and their sensory inputs. This principle, drawn from Bayesian inference, embodies the idea that cognitive and motor systems aim to minimize surprising or unexpected inputs. Here's a more detailed explanation: ## Simplified Concept Active inference suggests that our brains are continuously making predictions about the world, and that we act to make our predictions come true. For example, if you predict that moving your hand in a certain way will lead to you drinking coffee, then you will act out that prediction and take a sip from your coffee cup. ## Bayesian Brain The Bayesian brain hypothesis is fundamental to active inference. Bayesian inference is a statistical method that updates the probability for a hypothesis as more evidence or information becomes available. Simply put, it's a method of learning from experience. The Bayesian brain hypothesis suggests that the brain also updates its beliefs about the world in a Bayesian manner. The brain uses sensory inputs to update its beliefs about what causes these inputs. According to active inference, these beliefs then guide our action. ## Free Energy Principle The free energy principle, proposed by Karl Friston, is another critical component of active inference. This principle suggests that all adaptive life (at all scales and levels of organization) resists a natural tendency to disorder. In the context of active inference, free energy is synonymous with surprise. Therefore, reducing free energy is equivalent to reducing surprise or, in other words, making sensory inputs conform to our predictions. ## Perception and Action Perception and action under active inference are tied together through the minimization of surprise or prediction error. Perception minimizes surprise by updating beliefs (Bayesian belief updating), whereas action minimizes prediction errors by altering sensory input to fit prior expectations through a process known as 'active sampling'. ## Predictive Coding Predictive coding is a part of active inference where hierarchically organized neural systems exchange prediction and prediction error signals, fundamentally altering our understanding of perception and action. ## Application in Data Science In data science, the active inference framework can be used in various ways, most notably in machine learning and artificial intelligence systems. The guiding principle of these systems often resembles the active inference framework: they predict, act on their predictions, then update their predictions based on the results. This is a high-level explanation of active inference. It's a complex and robust framework with numerous theoretical and practical implications, touching areas such as behavioral economics, artificial intelligence, and cognitive robotics.Larry Alan Wasserman, born in 1959, is a prominent statistician and AI researcher. He serves as a professor in the Department of Statistics & Data Science and the Machine Learning Department at Carnegie Mellon University. His work spans various aspects of statistics and machine learning which makes him a notable contributor in the field of AI and data science. [^1] For a more detailed understanding of his work, I would recommend looking into his published papers and books, along with his contributions to the fields of statistics and machine learning. [^1]: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Larry_A._WassermanCosma Rohilla Shalizi is a renowned professor in the Department of Statistics at Carnegie Mellon University. His research interests include nonparametric prediction of time series, learning theory and nonlinear dynamics, information theory, stochastic automata, state space, and a certain emphasis on AI and machine learning concepts. Shalizi's work often touches on the stochastic aspects of nonlinear dynamical systems and unsupervised machine learning. His studies and discussions about AI's impact on our society, particularly concerning informational hierarchies and processing, have also been influential. Throughout his career, his research has been widely cited, reflecting his significant contributions to the field. Sources: - <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosma_Shalizi" target="_blank">Wikipedia - Cosma Shalizi</a> - <a href="https://www.stat.cmu.edu/~cshalizi/" target="_blank">Cosma Shalizi - Statistics & Data Science</a> - <a href="https://www.economist.com/by-invitation/2023/06/21/artificial-intelligence-is-a-familiar-looking-monster-say-henry-farrell-and-cosma-shalizi" target="_blank">Artificial intelligence is a familiar-looking monster</a> - <a href="https://scholar.google.com/citations?user=2i_mta4AAAAJ&hl=en" target="_blank">Cosma Rohilla Shalizi</a> - <a href="https://www.santafe.edu/people/profile/cosma-shalizi" target="_blank">Sante Fe Institute Profile - Cosma Shalizi</a><<include "Header">> ## Policy Gradient Policy gradient methods directly optimise the policy $\pi_\theta(a \mid s)$ — parameterised by $\theta$ — by computing gradients of expected reward with respect to $\theta$. They avoid the need for a value function or environment model. ''The objective'' Maximise the expected discounted return: $$J(\theta) = \mathbb{E}_{\tau \sim \pi_\theta}\!\left[\sum_t \gamma^t R_t\right]$$ ''The policy gradient theorem'' $$\nabla_\theta J(\theta) = \mathbb{E}_{\tau \sim \pi_\theta}\!\left[\sum_t \nabla_\theta \log \pi_\theta(a_t \mid s_t) \cdot G_t\right]$$ where $G_t$ is the return from timestep $t$. This remarkable result says: increase the log-probability of actions that led to high return; decrease it for actions that led to low return. ''REINFORCE'' The simplest policy gradient algorithm. Sample trajectories, compute returns, update: $$\theta \leftarrow \theta + \alpha \sum_t \nabla_\theta \log \pi_\theta(a_t \mid s_t) \cdot G_t$$ High variance — many trajectories needed for a stable gradient estimate. ''Variance reduction: baselines'' Subtract a baseline $b(s_t)$ from $G_t$ without introducing bias: $$\nabla_\theta J \propto \sum_t \nabla_\theta \log \pi_\theta(a_t \mid s_t) \cdot (G_t - b(s_t))$$ [[Actor-critic]] — use a learned value function as the baseline. ''Modern algorithms'' [[Proximal Policy Optimization ]] — PPO; clips the policy update to prevent large destabilising changes. The dominant practical algorithm.
