[[Actor-critic]] — combines policy gradient with a value function critic. --- [[Reinforcement Learning]] ← back | [[Actor-critic]] | [[Proximal Policy Optimization ]] <<include "Footer">>Andrew McCallum carries high significance in the field of computer science as a professor at the University of Massachusetts Amherst. His contributions and expertise mainly lie within machine learning, natural language processing, information extraction, information integration, and social network analysis. His work in these areas has greatly impacted research in computer science, making him a critical figure in this field. [^1] [^1]: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrew_McCallum <<include "Header">> ''Perturbation Theory'' A collection of techniques for finding approximate solutions to problems that cannot be solved exactly, by starting from an exactly-solvable problem and adding small corrections. ''Basic Idea'' Express the solution as a power series in a small parameter $\varepsilon$: $$ f(\varepsilon) = f_0 + \varepsilon f_1 + \varepsilon^2 f_2 + \cdots $$ $f_0$ is the exact solution to the unperturbed problem. Each $f_k$ corrects for the perturbation at order $\varepsilon^k$. ''Perturbation of Matrix Eigenvalues'' Given $A = A_0 + \varepsilon E$: $$ \lambda_i(A) \approx \lambda_i(A_0) + \varepsilon \mathbf{u}_i^\top E \mathbf{u}_i + O(\varepsilon^2) $$ First-order correction to eigenvalue $i$ = Rayleigh quotient of the perturbation. ''ML Applications'' * ''Influence functions'': how much does adding/removing one training point change the model predictions? A first-order perturbation analysis. * ''Sensitivity analysis'': effect of small data changes on model outputs * ''Neural tangent kernel'': infinite-width networks evolve according to perturbation theory around initialisation * ''Loss landscape'': curvature (Hessian eigenvalues) measures sensitivity to weight perturbations * ''Adversarial examples'': crafted perturbations that exploit the model's linear response <<button "Quiz: What does an influence function measure?">> <<replace "#pt-q2">> ''✓ How much a model's prediction on a test point would change if a specific training point were upweighted or removed — a first-order perturbation approximation of leave-one-out retraining.'' [[hessian]] | [[jacobian matrices]] | [[Adversarial Attacks]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="pt-q2"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Emergent Behaviours in AI'' Capabilities that appear ''unexpectedly'' at scale — not present in smaller models and not explicitly trained for. ''Wei et al. (2022): Emergent Abilities of LLMs'' As model scale (compute/parameters) crosses certain thresholds, performance on some tasks jumps from near-random to near-human level. Examples in GPT-style models: * Multi-step arithmetic * Chain-of-thought reasoning * Code generation * Language translation (with zero examples) ''Why Does This Happen?'' Competing theories: 1. ''Phase transitions'': underlying capability accumulates linearly; metric only becomes non-zero after a threshold 2. ''Metric artefacts'': discontinuous jumps are artefacts of coarse-grained evaluation metrics — continuous metrics may show smooth scaling 3. ''Genuine emergence'': complex sub-circuits appear only at sufficient capacity ''Examples Beyond LLMs'' * Grokking: networks suddenly generalise after long memorisation (small models) * In-context learning: ability to learn from examples in the prompt * Spontaneous task decomposition in multi-agent systems ''Implications for Safety'' Emergent capabilities are hard to predict and test for. A model may suddenly acquire new capabilities that weren't anticipated — motivating capability evaluations and safety testing at each scale increase. <<button "Quiz: What is the safety concern with emergent capabilities?">> <<replace "#em-q">> ''✓ They appear suddenly and unpredictably — a model may acquire a dangerous capability (e.g. deception, cyber capabilities) at a scale threshold that wasn't present in smaller versions, making pre-deployment safety testing incomplete.'' [[Deep Learning regimes]] | [[Deep Learning]] | [[Reinforcement Learning]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="em-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> Complex systems, in their essence, are systems composed of multiple components that may interact with one another. Key examples of complex systems include Earth's global climate, organisms, the human brain, infrastructure like power grid, transportation, or communication systems, social and economic organizations (like cities), ecosystems, living cells, and indeed, the entire universe. One of the predominant characteristics of complex systems is their inherent challenge to model due various factors including dependencies, competitions, relationships, or differing interactions between their constituent parts as well as the environment they exist within. These systems exhibit distinctive properties that arise from these intricate relationships, such as nonlinearity, emergence, spontaneous order, adaptation, and feedback loops. The field of complex systems refers to a scientific approach that focuses on how the relationships between a system's parts lead to its collective behaviors, and how this system in turn interacts with and establishes relationships with its environment. This approach pivots away from reductionism, instead favoring a holistic view that places importance on system-wide behaviors. It is an interdisciplinary domain, drawing from diverse fields such as physics, social sciences, mathematics, biology, and many others. It thus transcends many disciplines in a broad-spectrum approach to analyzing problems [^1]. [[Emergent behaviors]] [[Self-Organized Criticality]] [^1]: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_systemFinite state machines (FSMs) are a fundamental concept in the field of computer science and are widely used in various applications such as digital design, linguistics, and software development. They are abstract models used to represent systems with a finite number of states and transitions between these states. These transitions are typically determined by a set of inputs or stimuli. At any given moment, a finite state machine is in one of its states. When an input is received, the FSM transitions from its current state to another state based on a defined set of rules. These transitions are predetermined and are part of the design of the FSM. The behavior of the FSM is therefore entirely predictable and dependent on its current state and the input it receives. There are two main types of finite state machines: deterministic finite automata (DFA) and nondeterministic finite automata (NFA). In a DFA, for each state and input, there is exactly one resulting state. This means that the behavior of the machine is entirely predictable and deterministic. In contrast, an NFA allows for multiple possible states for a given input and state, or even transitions without any input (ε-transitions). Finite state machines can be represented in several ways, including state diagrams and transition tables. A state diagram is a graphical representation showing states, transitions, and inputs. Transition tables, on the other hand, list the transitions in a tabular form, indicating for each state and input the resulting state. FSMs are widely used in software for parsing and recognizing patterns in text (such as in compilers), controlling the behavior of digital circuits, and modeling sequential logic. They are also used in artificial intelligence, particularly in game development, for modeling the behavior of non-player characters (NPCs). The concept of finite state machines is integral to understanding the principles of computation and automata theory, forming a foundational aspect of theoretical computer science. <<include "Header">> ''Carlos Guestrin'' Computer scientist; Professor at University of Washington; co-founder of Turi (acquired by Apple, 2016); one of the creators of XGBoost. ''Key Contributions'' * ''XGBoost'' (with Tianqi Chen, 2016): gradient boosted trees with regularisation, parallel tree learning, and cache-aware computation. Dominated Kaggle competitions for years; still widely used in industry for tabular data. * Research in probabilistic graphical models, distributed ML, and model interpretability * Created Turi Create (now Apple's Create ML) for on-device machine learning ''XGBoost Impact'' XGBoost won more Kaggle competitions between 2015–2018 than any other algorithm. It introduced second-order Taylor expansion in the boosting objective, L1/L2 regularisation on leaf weights, and column subsampling — making it far more robust than earlier boosting approaches. ''At Apple'' Post-acquisition, Guestrin led ML efforts that became CoreML and on-device inference capabilities in iOS. Demonstrates the path from academic ML to mass deployment. ''Interpretability Work'' Co-created LIME (Local Interpretable Model-agnostic Explanations) with Marco Ribeiro and Sameer Singh — a foundational tool in ML explainability. <<include "Footer">> Semantics is a branch of linguistics and logic that is concerned with meaning. It encompasses the interpretation of expressions within languages, symbols in logic systems, and computational algorithms, among other things. At the heart of semantics is the relation between signifiers, such as words, phrases, signs, and symbols, and their denotations, or what they stand for in the real or a hypothetical world. It is through this study that we can interpret human expressions of languages universally. Semantics can be divided into various categories, including formal semantics, lexical semantics, and conceptual semantics. 1. Formal Semantics: This category focuses on understanding complex logical expressions by taking into account the sentence structure. In other words, it emphasizes the theoretical aspects tied to languages. 2. Lexical Semantics: This subfield involves the examination of word meanings and the ir relationships. It scrutinizes word meanings and inter-word relations beyond the syntax level. 3. Conceptual semantics: Conceptual semantics takes into account the cognitive structure of meanings. This form encompasses our mental vocabulary and how we organize and negotiate this knowledge. Semantic interpretation involves an understanding of meaning construction, representation, and implication in human languages. The exploration of semantics provides a critical foundation for various algorithmic processes in computers, such as natural language understanding and natural language processing. It aids in the development of technologies that can comprehend, translate, and generate human languages. To sum up, semantics is an indispensable branch of study for those aiming to understand communication, whether it's human language or machine language, and to develop systems oriented toward the understanding, representation, and processing of meanings.<<include "Header">> ## Hessian Matrix The Hessian is the matrix of second-order partial derivatives of a scalar function. It encodes the local curvature of the loss landscape — how the gradient itself changes as you move. ''Definition'' For a function $f: \mathbb{R}^d \to \mathbb{R}$: $$\mathbf{H}_{ij} = \frac{\partial^2 f}{\partial x_i \partial x_j}$$ For an $d$-parameter model, the Hessian is a $d \times d$ matrix. ''What curvature tells you'' - Positive definite $\mathbf{H}$ → local minimum (loss curves upward in all directions) - Negative definite $\mathbf{H}$ → local maximum - Indefinite $\mathbf{H}$ → saddle point (up in some directions, down in others) ''Newton's method'' Second-order optimisation uses the Hessian to take curvature-corrected steps: $$\theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - \mathbf{H}^{-1} \nabla f(\theta_t)$$ More efficient than gradient descent when $\mathbf{H}^{-1}$ is affordable. Impractical for deep networks (millions of parameters → $\mathbf{H}$ is too large to compute or invert). ''Approximations'' Adam's second moment $v_t$ approximates the diagonal of the Hessian. L-BFGS maintains a low-rank approximation. These make second-order information tractable. ''Connections'' [[Optimization]] — curvature is central to understanding convergence [[jacobian matrices]] — the Jacobian of the gradient is the Hessian [[adam]] — uses an approximation to diagonal Hessian information [[Convex Optimization]] — a function is convex iff its Hessian is positive semi-definite everywhere --- [[Mathmatical Concepts]] ← back | [[Optimization]] | [[adam]] <<include "Footer">>Random Matrix Theory (RMT) is an interdisciplinary subject in probability theory and mathematical physics, which considerably focuses on the properties of matrix-valued random variables. These variables constitute matrices wherein some or all of their elements are distinct random variables. Understanding the theory is invaluable since numerous vital properties of physical systems, spanning from condensed matter physics to quantum chaos, can be modeled mathematically as matrix problems. One illustration is that the thermal conductivity of a lattice may be deduced from the dynamical matrix representing the interactions amongst particles in the lattice. [^1] [^1]: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Random_matrix<<include "Header">> ## Jacobian Matrix The Jacobian generalises the derivative to vector-valued functions. Where a scalar derivative is a single number, the Jacobian is a matrix of all first-order partial derivatives. ''Definition'' For $\mathbf{f}: \mathbb{R}^n \to \mathbb{R}^m$, the Jacobian $\mathbf{J} \in \mathbb{R}^{m \times n}$ is: $$\mathbf{J}_{ij} = \frac{\partial f_i}{\partial x_j}$$ The $(i,j)$ entry: how much the $i$-th output changes when the $j$-th input changes. ''Role in backpropagation'' Each layer of a neural network is a vector function. The backward pass multiplies Jacobians via the [[chain rule]]: $$\frac{\partial \mathcal{L}}{\partial \mathbf{x}} = \mathbf{J}_{\mathbf{f}/\mathbf{x}}^\top \frac{\partial \mathcal{L}}{\partial \mathbf{f}}$$ In practice, we never form the full Jacobian — we only need Jacobian-vector products (JVPs) or vector-Jacobian products (VJPs), which is what modern autograd frameworks compute efficiently. ''The Hessian connection'' The [[hessian]] of $f: \mathbb{R}^n \to \mathbb{R}$ is the Jacobian of its gradient $\nabla f$. ''Applications'' - Neural network sensitivity analysis - Robustness to input perturbations ([[Adversarial Attacks]]) - Normalising flows — generative models that track how volume changes under transformations ($|\det \mathbf{J}|$) --- [[Mathmatical Concepts]] ← back | [[chain rule]] | [[hessian]] <<include "Footer">>In the realm of machine learning and deep learning models, the notion of 'rank collapse' pertains to an undesirable phenomenon wherein a model fails to learn a diverse and comprehensive representation of the input data. It converges instead towards a constrained view, typically magnifying certain features or aspects while ignoring others. The term 'rank' in machine learning signifies the dimensions of a tensor, or the number of unique latent factors or features it encapsulates. Therefore, when we encounter 'rank collapse', it signifies that the model is not utilising the full rank or spectrum of latent features. Simply put, the model is only capturing a small subset of the input data's features, limiting its ability to generate varied outputs. In Generative Adversarial Networks (GANs), 'mode collapse' is a prevalent example of 'rank collapse'. In this scenario, the generator model tends to create outputs limited to a few modes, or groups, of the input data, rather than representing the full range of input data diversity. This could result in a generator that produces images of only specific types of dogs, rather than the gamut of dog breeds found in the dataset. Rank collapse could lead to serious issues such as overfitting, lack of generalizability and a limited capacity to handle complex tasks. Consequently, various techniques and strategies are employed to mitigate this issue, such as different training methodologies, regularization techniques, and architectural improvements to the models themselves. <<include "Header">> ''Pareto Distribution'' A heavy-tailed power-law distribution that models phenomena where a small fraction of inputs account for a large fraction of outcomes. $$ P(X > x) = \left(\frac{x_m}{x}\right)^\alpha, \quad x \geq x_m > 0 $$ PDF: $f(x) = \frac{\alpha x_m^\alpha}{x^{\alpha+1}}$ ''The 80-20 Rule (Pareto Principle)'' Vilfredo Pareto observed that 80% of Italy's land was owned by 20% of the population. Generalises to: a small fraction of causes produces most of the effect. ''Heavy Tails'' For $\alpha \leq 2$: variance is infinite. For $\alpha \leq 1$: mean is infinite. Extreme events are far more likely than under Gaussian distributions. ''Power Law in ML/NLP'' * Word frequency (Zipf's law): word rank $\times$ frequency $\approx$ constant * Neural scaling laws: performance $\propto$ (compute/data/parameters)$^{-\alpha}$ * Degree distribution in social/citation networks * Income, city size, earthquake magnitudes ''Practical Implications'' * Rare tokens dominate vocabulary in LLMs → subword tokenisation needed * A few training examples may account for most of a model's knowledge * Long-tailed distribution of classes → class imbalance problem <<button "Quiz: What makes the Pareto distribution 'heavy-tailed'?">> <<replace "#pareto-q">> ''✓ Its tail decays as a power law $x^{-\alpha}$ rather than exponentially like the Gaussian. This means extreme values are far more probable — the tail is ''fat'' relative to Normal distributions.'' [[Distribtuions]] | [[Gaussian Distribution]] | [[Information Thoery]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="pareto-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Student-Teacher Model (Knowledge Distillation)'' A technique for transferring knowledge from a large, high-performing ''teacher'' model to a smaller, faster ''student'' model. ''Why Distill?'' Large models are accurate but expensive at inference. Distillation produces a compact student that approaches teacher performance at a fraction of the compute cost. ''Core Idea (Hinton et al. 2015)'' Train the student to match the teacher's ''soft predictions'' (logit probabilities) rather than just the hard ground-truth labels. $$ \mathcal{L}_{\text{KD}} = (1-\alpha)\mathcal{L}_{\text{CE}}(y, \sigma(z_S)) + \alpha \cdot T^2 D_{\text{KL}}(\sigma(z_T/T) \| \sigma(z_S/T)) $$ * $T$: temperature (softens the distribution; reveals more information in near-zero logits) * $\alpha$: balance between hard labels and soft targets ''Why Soft Labels Help'' A teacher assigns probabilities like: cat=0.7, tiger=0.2, dog=0.1. This reveals that cats and tigers are similar — richer information than the binary label "cat." ''Variants'' * ''Self-distillation'': student and teacher are the same architecture — earlier layers teach later layers * ''Feature distillation'': match intermediate representations, not just outputs * ''Born-again networks'': ensemble knowledge → single model ''Applications'' DistilBERT (60% of BERT size, 97% of performance), MobileNet (from EfficientNet teacher), TinyML for on-device inference. <<button "Quiz: Why does using a high temperature $T$ help in knowledge distillation?">> <<replace "#kd-q">> ''✓ High temperature softens the teacher's output distribution, revealing the relative similarities between classes (near-zero logits become informative). A hard argmax discards all this relational information.'' [[Transfer Learning]] | [[KL Divergence]] | [[Deep Learning]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="kd-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Trace of a Matrix'' The trace of a square matrix is the sum of its diagonal entries: $$ \text{tr}(A) = \sum_{i=1}^n a_{ii} $$ ''Properties'' * Linearity: $\text{tr}(A+B) = \text{tr}(A) + \text{tr}(B)$ * Cyclic: $\text{tr}(ABC) = \text{tr}(CAB) = \text{tr}(BCA)$ — but order within cycles matters! * Transpose: $\text{tr}(A) = \text{tr}(A^\top)$ * Sum of eigenvalues: $\text{tr}(A) = \sum_i \lambda_i$ ''Relation to Frobenius Norm'' $$ \|A\|_F^2 = \text{tr}(A^\top A) = \sum_{ij} a_{ij}^2 $$ ''Relation to Determinant'' For $2 \times 2$: $\text{tr}(A) = \lambda_1 + \lambda_2$, $\det(A) = \lambda_1 \lambda_2$ ''ML Uses'' * ''Gaussian log-likelihood'': $\log p(x|\mu,\Sigma) \propto -(x-\mu)^\top \Sigma^{-1}(x-\mu) - \log|\Sigma|$; trace appears via $\text{tr}(\Sigma^{-1}S)$ in the MLE update * ''Stein's Lemma'': divergence estimator $\hat{D}_{KL} = \text{tr}(J_f) + \ldots$ in normalising flows * ''Hutchinson trace estimator'': $\text{tr}(A) \approx \frac{1}{m}\sum_k \mathbf{z}_k^\top A \mathbf{z}_k$ with random vectors — avoids explicit matrix materialisation in second-order optimisation <<button "Quiz: Why is $\text{tr}(ABC) = \text{tr}(CAB)$ but NOT $\text{tr}(ABC) = \text{tr}(ACB)$ in general?">> <<replace "#tr-q">> ''✓ The cyclic property only allows cyclic permutations of the factors. $CAB$ is a cyclic rearrangement of $ABC$, but $ACB$ is not — it swaps $B$ and $C$ which changes the matrix product.'' [[Frobenius norm]] | [[Eiganvectors and Eiganvalue]] | [[Core Linear Algebra]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="tr-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Generalization Gap The generalization gap is the difference between a model's training error and its test (generalization) error. It is the primary diagnostic for overfitting. $$\text{Generalization gap} = \mathcal{L}_{\text{test}} - \mathcal{L}_{\text{train}}$$ ''Interpreting the gap'' - ''Small gap, low error'' — well-fitted, good generalisation - ''Small gap, high error'' — underfitting (high bias); model too simple for the problem - ''Large gap'' — overfitting (high variance); model memorising training data ''What drives the gap'' [[Bias-Variance tradeoff]] — variance is the dominant contributor to the gap [[Statsitical Learning Theory]] — VC theory bounds the gap in terms of model capacity and training set size: $$\mathcal{L}_{\text{test}} \leq \mathcal{L}_{\text{train}} + O\!\left(\sqrt{\frac{\text{VC-dim}}{n}}\right)$$ More parameters, fewer training examples → larger potential gap. ''Monitoring in practice'' Plot training and validation loss curves during training. The gap appears when validation loss stops decreasing while training loss continues to fall. This is the signal to stop training or add regularisation. ''Closing the gap'' [[Regularization]] — L1/L2, dropout, early stopping [[Cross Validation]] — detects the gap before deploying More data — the most reliable fix; gap decreases as $O(1/\sqrt{n})$ --- [[Statsitical Learning Theory]] ← back | [[Overfitting]] | [[Regularization]] <<include "Footer">>Empirical methods represent a systematic approach in research studies that fundamentally rely on direct or indirect observation or experience to collect data. These methods are quintessential in the array of scientific exploration because they introduce a dimension of objectivity, usually aimed at establishing quantifiable evidence. Empirical methods can be categorized broadly into qualitative and quantitative methods. Qualitative empirical methods primarily focus on obtaining in-depth understanding of human behavior and the reasons that govern such behavior. These adopt techniques like interviews, focus groups, and case studies. Quantitative empirical methods, on the other hand, gather numerical data, and typically apply statistical tools for analysis, aiming at providing data that can be transformed into usable statistics. Surveys, controlled experiments, and observational studies exemplify this approach. It is important to note, however, that qualitative and quantitative methods are not mutually exclusive; instead, they often complement each other within mixed methods research designs, providing a more comprehensive understanding of research problems. The rigorous application of empirical methods is invaluable in testing hypotheses or theories, and for enhancing the credibility and reliability of research findings. As such, these methods form an integral part of the scientific methodology, underpinning the advancement of knowledge across diverse academic disciples.Self-Organized Criticality (SOC) is a property of dynamical systems that have a critical point as an attractor. These systems inherently tune themselves as they evolve towards the criticality, displaying the spatial or temporal scale-invariance characteristic of the critical point of a phase transition but without the need for external control parameters. This concept has been applied across a broad spectrum of fields such as geophysics, economics, neurobiology, and many more. Systems exhibiting SOC are usually driven non-equilibrium systems with a high degree of freedom and strongly nonlinear dynamics. Despite numerous individual examples, a universal set of characteristics predicting SOC in a system remains elusive. [^1] [^1]: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-organized_criticalityDynamical systems, a prominent concept in mathematical and scientific studies, denote a system in which a function describes the time dependence of a point in a geometrical space. The system's objective is to determine or study the evolution of certain quantities over time. Two primary categories of dynamical systems exist: continuous dynamical systems and discrete dynamical systems. The former type involves systems that evolve over continuous time (e.g., differential equations), while the latter type evolves over discrete time (e.g., iterations). Dynamical systems are characterized by points known as "states", which describe the system's state at an instantaneous moment. Mathematically, these points reside in a state space — a manifold. The course traced out in state space as time advances represents the system's trajectory. This state space trajectory serves as the mathematical representation of the system's behavior. The field has virtually limitless applications and is pertinent to disciplines such as physics, engineering, economics, biology, and more. For instance, in biological systems, dynamical systems could describe populations' evolution over time with interactions such as predation or competition. In economics, they might depict the evolution of wealth in an economy, accounting for factors such as production, consumption, investment, and so on. Important concepts within the realm of dynamical systems include chaos, attractors, bifurcation, and fractals. These are pivotal in understanding the complex behavior of various systems, and heighten our understanding of natural phenomena ranging from weather patterns to population dynamics. Evidently, dynamical systems serve as an essential foundation for analysing and predicting the behavior of a vast array of systems across numerous disciplines. They offer a rich, robust framework for understanding the fundamental principles that guide evolution and change over time.A point estimate pertains to the concept in statistics where an approximation of parameter(s) is provided with a 'single' value derived from statistical analysis. Contrasting with interval estimation, a point representation does not account for the inherent uncertainty or variability in deriving this value. There exist various methods to generate point estimates, often classified as either a mode of maximum likelihood estimation or a method of moments. Each method's chosen application is dependent on the skewness, kurtosis, and overall distribution shape of the parameter under investigation. Maximum Likelihood Estimation (MLE) optimises the likelihood of observed data given a specific statistical model. MLE assumes that data and observed outcomes stem from specific known distributions e.g., Poisson, binomial, or normal distributions. Therefore, MLE's practical implementation necessitates comprehension of the underlying assumptions and limitations with each distribution used. On the other hand, the method of moments is founded on the principle of moment–and uses algebraic manipulations of expected values or moments to find parameter estimates. It is worth noting that the validity and accuracy of point estimates should always be viewed with due skepticism. Especially, inferences derived from singular data points may give misleading or incomplete representations of the parameter that is being estimated. Extreme values or outlier observations can particularly distort point estimates, making them an unreliable indicator of parameters. Interval estimation, which provides a range of possible values, may oftentimes provide a more comprehensive picture of parameter estimates, considering the inherent variability in the process of statistical estimation.The concept of a universality class is a vital framework within the territory of statistical mechanics and critical phenomena. This concept stems from the empirical observations and theoretical understanding that complex systems, despite their seemingly diverse details at the microscopic level, can exhibit identical behavior at their critical points. Universality classes denote the set of systems sharing relevant intrinsic attributes. Interestingly, ostensibly incongruent systems can fall into the same universality class if they share critical exponents, scaling functions, and underlying symmetries. This suggests a type of reduced order that emerges near critical points, which is a phenomena pervasive in nature. Physics, biology, and even social systems have revealed critical phenomena analogous to phase transitions. To practically discern universality classes, one must consider the properties of a given system. These include the system's dimensionality, the range of interactions (whether short or long-ranged), the symmetry of the order parameter, the nature of the disorder, and if the system experiences conservation laws or additional competing interactions. The discovery and understanding of the universality class is attributed to Kenneth Wilson's development of the renormalization-group theory, which allows for the theoretical computation of critical exponents. As such, the concept of a universality class represents a foundation for large scale behaviors in complex systems and offers a valuable perspective in the quest for understanding emergence in natural and artificial complex systems.Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD) is a fundamental optimization algorithm widely employed in machine learning and deep learning contexts. It is particularly instrumental in the training of large-scale models where the full dataset might be impractical to process simultaneously due to memory constraints or computational inefficiency. SGD is an iterative method for optimizing an objective function, commonly a loss function in a machine learning context, that is differentiable with respect to its parameters. Traditional gradient descent algorithms compute the gradient of the loss function with respect to the parameters for the entire training dataset to perform a single update. In contrast, SGD approximates this gradient by computing it for only a small sample (a batch) of data at each iteration. This batch is typically selected randomly, hence the term 'stochastic'. This approach has several advantages: Computational Efficiency: By using only a subset of the data to compute the gradient, SGD reduces the computational burden per iteration, enabling it to handle large datasets more efficiently. Frequent Updates: The frequent updates of the model parameters with smaller batches can lead to faster convergence in practice, especially in the presence of highly non-linear and complex data distributions. Generalization: SGD can help in mitigating overfitting—a common challenge in machine learning—by adding a degree of noise through its random sampling. This can potentially lead to better generalization on unseen data. However, SGD also has some challenges: Sensitivity to Hyperparameters: The learning rate and batch size are critical hyperparameters in SGD. Improper selection can lead to either slow convergence or divergence of the learning process. Non-uniform Convergence: Due to its stochastic nature, the path to convergence can be noisy and uneven. This necessitates careful monitoring and potentially more sophisticated variants of SGD, such as those incorporating momentum or adaptive learning rates. Local Minima and Saddle Points: In complex models, especially with high-dimensional parameter spaces, SGD may get trapped in local minima or saddle points. In advanced research contexts, modifications and extensions of SGD, such as mini-batch gradient descent, momentum-based methods (e.g., Nesterov accelerated gradient), and adaptive learning rate methods (e.g., Adam, RMSprop), are explored to address these challenges and improve the efficiency and effectiveness of the optimization process in machine learning models. <<include "Header">> ''Autoregressive Models'' A model is autoregressive if it generates each output conditioned on all previous outputs. ''Probability Factorisation'' $$ p(\mathbf{x}) = \prod_{t=1}^T p(x_t | x_1, \ldots, x_{t-1}) $$ This is the chain rule of probability — no approximation. Allows exact likelihood computation. ''Language Models (GPT family)'' $$ p(w_1, \ldots, w_T) = \prod_{t=1}^T p(w_t | w_1, \ldots, w_{t-1}) $$ At inference: sample $w_t$ from the conditional, append to context, repeat. This is ''autoregressive decoding''. ''ARIMA (Time Series)'' $$ X_t = c + \sum_{i=1}^p \phi_i X_{t-i} + \varepsilon_t $$ Each value is a weighted sum of $p$ previous values plus noise. The AR order $p$ is the autoregressive lag. ''Trade-offs'' | Property | Autoregressive | Non-Autoregressive | | Exact likelihood | ✓ | Usually ✗ | | Generation speed | Sequential | Parallel | | Quality | High | Lower (but improving) | ''Examples'' GPT, LLaMA, Mistral (language); WaveNet (audio); PixelCNN (images); AR diffusion <<button "Quiz: What makes a model 'autoregressive'?">> <<replace "#ar-q">> ''✓ It models each output token as conditioned on all previously generated tokens — generation is sequential, each step consuming its own past output.'' [[Transformers]] | [[LSTM]] | [[diffusion]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="ar-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''K-Fold Cross-Validation'' A resampling procedure that evaluates model performance more reliably than a single train/test split by using the data multiple times. ''Procedure'' 1. Randomly partition $n$ samples into $K$ equal folds 2. For $k = 1, \ldots, K$: * Train on $K-1$ folds * Evaluate on fold $k$ 3. Average the $K$ test scores $$ \text{CV score} = \frac{1}{K} \sum_{k=1}^K \text{score}_k $$ ''Choosing $K$'' | $K$ | Bias | Variance | Cost | | 5 | Moderate | Moderate | Low | | 10 | Low | Low | Moderate | | $n$ (LOOCV) | Very low | High | Very high | $K=5$ or $K=10$ are standard defaults. ''Bias-Variance of CV'' * More folds → less bias (training set closer to full data) * More folds → more variance (correlated folds; small test sets) ''Common Uses'' * Model selection: compare algorithms, architectures * Hyperparameter tuning: avoid test set contamination * Estimating generalisation error with confidence intervals [[Stratified K-Fold Cross Validation]] accounts for class imbalance by preserving class proportions in each fold. <<button "Quiz: Why is 10-fold CV better than a single 90/10 train/test split?">> <<replace "#kfcv-q">> ''✓ All data is used for both training and evaluation. The estimate is averaged over 10 different splits, reducing variance and giving a more reliable generalisation estimate — especially valuable for small datasets.'' [[Stratified K-Fold Cross Validation]] | [[Leave-One-Out Cross-Validation]] | [[Cross Validation]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="kfcv-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Stratified K-Fold Cross-Validation'' A variant of K-fold that preserves the class distribution in every fold — essential for imbalanced datasets. ''Why Stratify?'' In standard K-fold, random partitioning can create folds with very different class ratios. On a dataset with 5% positives, one fold might have 0% positives — making evaluation meaningless. ''Procedure'' For each class $c$: 1. Collect all samples from class $c$ 2. Distribute them roughly equally across $K$ folds Each fold ends up with approximately the same class proportions as the full dataset. ''Example'' Dataset: 90 negatives, 10 positives → 10-fold stratified: * Each fold: 9 negatives + 1 positive (exactly 10% positive) Standard K-fold might produce folds with 0 positives. ''When to Always Use Stratification'' * Class imbalance (any significant imbalance, not just extreme) * Rare event prediction (fraud, disease, defects) * Multi-class problems with unequal class sizes ''Regression Variant'' For regression, stratify by binning the target variable into quantiles — ensures each fold covers the full range of target values. <<button "Quiz: What problem does stratified K-fold solve?">> <<replace "#skfcv-q">> ''✓ It ensures each fold has the same class proportions as the full dataset. Without stratification, folds may have zero examples of rare classes, making validation metrics unreliable.'' [[K-Fold Cross-Validation]] | [[Leave-One-Out Cross-Validation]] | [[Cross Validation]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="skfcv-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Leave-One-Out Cross-Validation (LOOCV)'' The extreme case of K-fold where $K = n$ — each fold has exactly one test sample. ''Procedure'' For $i = 1, \ldots, n$: 1. Train on all data except sample $i$ 2. Predict on sample $i$ 3. Record error $e_i$ $$ \text{LOO score} = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n e_i $$ ''Properties'' * ''Nearly unbiased'': trains on $n-1$ samples (closest to true $n$-sample performance) * ''High variance'': the $n$ models are very correlated (share $n-2$ training points) * ''Expensive'': requires $n$ model fits — infeasible for large datasets or slow models ''Analytical Shortcut (Linear Regression)'' For linear regression with regularisation, LOOCV can be computed in closed form from a single fit: $$ e_i^{\text{LOO}} = \frac{y_i - \hat{y}_i}{1 - h_{ii}} $$ where $h_{ii}$ is the $i$-th diagonal of the hat matrix $H = X(X^\top X)^{-1}X^\top$. ''When to Use LOOCV'' * Very small datasets ($n < 50$) * When every sample matters * When model fitting is cheap <<button "Quiz: Why does LOOCV have high variance despite being nearly unbiased?">> <<replace "#loocv-q">> ''✓ All $n$ models share $n-2$ training points — they are highly correlated. Averaging highly correlated estimates barely reduces variance, unlike averaging independent estimates.'' [[K-Fold Cross-Validation]] | [[Stratified K-Fold Cross Validation]] | [[Cross Validation]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="loocv-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> The National Artificial Intelligence Research Resource (NAIRR) refers to a system designed to democratize access to AI research infrastructure. This concept for a shared national research infrastructure is aimed at connecting U.S. researchers to responsible and trustworthy AI resources. It is a product of a national, interagency task force that sought to create an AI research environment accessible to all. The overarching goal of NAIRR is to transform the U.S. national AI research ecosystem, facilitating the ability to address societal-level issues. Furthermore, it seeks to support innovation in AI R&D, as well as ensuring researchers across various fields and disciplines have access to critical AI resources. [^1] [^2] [^3] [^4] [^5] [^6] [^1]: https://www.nsf.gov/cise/national-ai.jsp [^2]: https://www.whitehouse.gov/ostp/news-updates/2023/01/24/national-artificial-intelligence-research-resource-task-force-releases-final-report/ [^3]: https://www.airesearchresource.com/ [^4]: https://www.ai.gov/wp-content/uploads/2023/01/NAIRR-TF-Final-Report-2023.pdf [^5]: https://hai.stanford.edu/policy/national-ai-research-resource [^6]: https://new.nsf.gov/news/nsf-partners-kick-nairr-pilot-program <<include "Header">> ''Chaos Theory'' The study of dynamical systems that are highly sensitive to initial conditions — small differences in starting state lead to exponentially diverging trajectories. ''Butterfly Effect'' "A butterfly flapping its wings in Brazil can cause a tornado in Texas." — E. Lorenz Formally: the largest Lyapunov exponent $\lambda > 0$. Trajectories starting $\delta_0$ apart diverge as: $$ \delta(t) \approx \delta_0 e^{\lambda t} $$ ''Lorenz Attractor'' A strange attractor arising from a 3D ODE system (Lorenz 1963), originally modelling atmospheric convection: $$ \dot{x} = \sigma(y-x), \quad \dot{y} = x(\rho-z)-y, \quad \dot{z} = xy-\beta z $$ For $\sigma=10, \rho=28, \beta=8/3$: chaotic behaviour with fractal attractor. ''Chaos vs Randomness'' Chaos is deterministic — the same initial conditions always produce the same trajectory. It just looks random due to sensitivity. True randomness is non-deterministic. ''ML Connections'' * ''SGD dynamics'': gradient noise and chaotic trajectories in loss landscape * ''RNN instability'': vanishing/exploding gradients related to Lyapunov exponents of the Jacobian * ''Neural ODEs'': continuous-depth networks as dynamical systems; stability analysis via Lyapunov theory * ''Reservoir computing'': exploit chaotic RNN dynamics for computation (edge of chaos hypothesis) <<button "Quiz: How does chaos differ from randomness?">> <<replace "#ct-q">> ''✓ Chaos is fully deterministic — identical initial conditions produce identical trajectories. It appears random because of extreme sensitivity to initial conditions (positive Lyapunov exponent), not because of non-determinism.'' [[dynamical systems]] | [[LSTM]] | [[vanishing gradient]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="ct-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''K-Fold Cross-Validation: Code'' <pre> from sklearn.model_selection import KFold, cross_val_score from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression import numpy as np X, y = load_data() kf = KFold(n_splits=5, shuffle=True, random_state=42) model = LogisticRegression() scores = cross_val_score(model, X, y, cv=kf, scoring='accuracy') print(f"Mean: {scores.mean():.3f} +/- {scores.std():.3f}") </pre> ''Stratified version (preferred for classification):'' <pre> from sklearn.model_selection import StratifiedKFold skf = StratifiedKFold(n_splits=5, shuffle=True, random_state=42) scores = cross_val_score(model, X, y, cv=skf) </pre> ''Manual loop (when you need more control):'' <pre> for fold, (train_idx, val_idx) in enumerate(kf.split(X)): X_train, X_val = X[train_idx], X[val_idx] y_train, y_val = y[train_idx], y[val_idx] model.fit(X_train, y_train) score = model.score(X_val, y_val) </pre> See [[K-Fold Cross-Validation]] for theory. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Feature Learning Feature learning (representation learning) is the ability of a model to automatically discover the features it needs from raw data, without manual engineering. It is one of the defining capabilities of deep learning. ''The core idea'' Instead of hand-crafting features and then training a classifier on them, an end-to-end system learns features and classifier jointly, optimising for the final task. Early layers learn low-level features (edges, n-grams). Later layers combine these into increasingly abstract representations (shapes, phrases, concepts). ''Why it matters'' Manual feature engineering requires deep domain expertise and is brittle to distribution shifts. Feature learning generalises across domains — the same CNN architecture works for faces, medical images, and satellites. ''Methods'' [[Deep Learning]] — CNNs, RNNs, Transformers all learn hierarchical features end-to-end [[Embeddings]] — discrete objects (words, users, items) mapped to dense learned representations [[Autoencoders]] — learn compressed representations in an unsupervised way [[Transfer Learning]] — features learned on one task transferred to another ''Self-supervised feature learning'' [[Bert]], GPT — learn rich language features from unlabelled text [[Word2Vec]] — learn word features by predicting context Contrastive learning — learn features by pulling together similar pairs and pushing apart dissimilar ones ''Connections'' [[Feature Engineering]] — the manual alternative; still dominant for tabular data [[Lower Dimensional Representation]] — feature learning often produces compact representations [[PCA]] — the linear version of feature learning --- [[learning Paradigms]] ← back | [[Embeddings]] | [[Transfer Learning]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Feature Engineering Feature engineering is the process of using domain knowledge to transform raw data into representations that make machine learning algorithms work better. It is often the difference between a mediocre and a state-of-the-art model on structured data. ''What it involves'' - ''Transformations'': log, sqrt, polynomial, binning of numeric features - ''Interactions'': products or ratios of features that capture relationships - ''Encoding'': [[one-hot encoding]] for categoricals, target encoding, embeddings - ''Aggregations'': mean, max, count over groups (critical for tabular data) - ''Time features'': extracting day-of-week, hour, lag features from timestamps ''Why it matters'' Most algorithms can only find patterns that are linearly separable in the input space. Feature engineering moves the problem into a space where the relevant patterns are more accessible. Linear regression with feature $x^2$ added can fit a parabola — the same model, now with a better feature. ''Feature engineering vs. feature learning'' [[Feature Learning]] — deep networks learn features automatically from raw data Feature engineering — human-designed; requires domain expertise; still dominant for structured/tabular data ''Feature selection'' [[Lasso]] — L1 regularisation zeros out irrelevant features automatically [[Random Forest]] — built-in feature importance scores [[PCA]] — creates new features that are linear combinations of the original ones ''Connections'' [[Curse of Dimensionality]] — too many features hurt; careful engineering keeps dimensionality manageable [[Regularization]] — penalty terms can substitute for explicit feature selection --- [[learning Paradigms]] ← back | [[Feature Learning]] | [[Lasso]] <<include "Footer">>In the realm of mathematics, particularly in the field of optimization, 'convexity' plays an instrumental role. A set within a real or complex vector space is characterized as convex if the vector that connects any two points within the set remains entirely within the set. To illustrate in mathematical terms, if S is a convex set in a real or complex vector space, two points x and y in S, and a real number λ between 0 and 1, the following applies: λx + (1 - λ)y is an element of S. Utilitarian in nature, convex sets embed a wealth of vital properties. The intersection of any collection of convex sets is inherently convex. Further, every point within a convex cone is a nonnegative weighted sum of a finite subset of extreme points. They lend ease to analysis and problem solving, an element of simplicity which non-convex optimization lacks. The concept of 'Convex Functions' extends the idea of convex sets. A function is deemed convex on an interval if the line segment between any two points on the function's graph does not fall below the graph itself. More formally, a real-valued function f(x) is convex on an interval I if for any x, y ∈ I and any λ ∈ [0, 1], we have: f(λx + (1 - λ)y) ≤ λf(x) + (1 - λ)f(y) Convexity in this sense arises in numerous fields including economics, artificial intelligence, computer vision, and machine learning, particularly in optimization algorithms where local minimums are also global minimums. The value of understanding convexity can not be understated in multidisciplinary research fields, as it serves foundational in several problem-solving areas, from linear programming to supporting vector machines in machine learning.The term "concave" serves a vital purpose in multiple academic disciplines but predominantly is used most frequently in mathematics, economics, and physical sciences. In a broad sense, a concave entity is characterized by an inward curvature, somewhat resembling a cave. From the perspective of mathematics, specifically in the context of functions, a function f defined on an interval is said to be concave if, for any distinct points x and y in the interval and for any t between 0 and 1, we have f(tx + (1 - t)y) ≥ tf(x) + (1 - t)f(y). This definition signifies that the function lies below the line segment connecting the points (x, f(x)) and (y, f(y)) for the entire interval for any distinct x and y. Next, considering a more geometric perspective, a polygon or polyhedron is concave if there exists at least one line segment that, when connecting two points within the region, extends outside the boundary of the object. Finally, in optimization, a set is said to be concave if the set formed by any line segment connecting two arbitrary points within the set resides completely within the set. This denotes the usefulness of concave functions in optimization problems, as local minimums are also global, simplifying the complexity of optimization tasks. These descriptions provide an academic-level overview of the concept of concavity. For a more profound understanding, one might want to explore its contrasts with convexity, its implications in computational economics, and its role in machine learning algorithms. <<include "Header">> ''Convex vs Concave'' ''Convex Function'' $f$ is convex if the line segment between any two points on the graph lies above or on the graph: $$ f(\lambda x + (1-\lambda)y) \leq \lambda f(x) + (1-\lambda)f(y), \quad \lambda \in [0,1] $$ Equivalently (for twice-differentiable): Hessian $H \succeq 0$ (positive semi-definite). ''Concave Function'' $f$ is concave iff $-f$ is convex: $$ f(\lambda x + (1-\lambda)y) \geq \lambda f(x) + (1-\lambda)f(y) $$ ''Why Convexity Matters for Optimisation'' * Any local minimum of a convex function is a global minimum * Gradient descent is guaranteed to converge to the global optimum * Duality is exact (strong duality under Slater's condition) ''Common Examples'' | Convex | Concave | | $x^2$, $e^x$, $|x|$ | $\log x$, $\sqrt{x}$, $-x^2$ | | Cross-entropy loss | Entropy function | | $\|\mathbf{w}\|_2^2$ | $-\|\mathbf{w}\|_2^2$ | ''Jensen's Inequality'' For convex $f$: $f(E[X]) \leq E[f(X)]$. Foundation of the EM algorithm (ELBO derivation). <<button "Quiz: Why is convexity so important in optimisation?">> <<replace "#cc-q">> ''✓ A convex function has no local minima that aren't also global minima. This guarantees that gradient descent (and other first-order methods) will find the globally optimal solution.'' [[Convex Optimization]] | [[Optimization]] | [[Lagrangian Duality]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="cc-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> Applied mathematics represents the application of mathematical techniques in various fields such as physics, engineering, medicine, biology, finance, business, computer science, and industry. The core of this discipline lies at the intersection of mathematical science and specialized knowledge. In many instances, practical applications have been the impetus for the development of mathematical theories, which in turn have become subjects of study in the realm of pure mathematics. Consequently, the activities within applied mathematics retain a close relationship with research pursuits in pure mathematics. The field of applied mathematics thereby embodies an interplay of practical problem-solving and the study of underlying abstract concepts. [^1] [^1]: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Applied_mathematics [[Search]] [[Ranking]] [[Deep Learning]] [[Kernel Methods]] [[Statistical Machine Learning]] <<include "Header">> ''Occam's Razor'' "Entities must not be multiplied beyond necessity." — William of Ockham (~1320) Prefer the simplest hypothesis consistent with the data. ''Formalisation in ML'' | Framework | Form | | Bias-Variance | Simple models have higher bias but lower variance — prefer them when data is limited | | MDL (Minimum Description Length) | Best model = shortest description of data + model | | Bayesian | Complex models receive lower prior probability | | PAC Learning | Generalisation bounds grow with model complexity | ''Structural Risk Minimisation (Vapnik)'' $$ \text{risk} \leq \text{empirical risk} + \underbrace{\sqrt{\frac{h(\ln(2n/h)+1) - \ln(\delta/4)}{n}}}_{\text{complexity penalty}} $$ where $h$ is the VC dimension. Minimise risk bound by balancing fit and complexity. ''Regularisation as Occam's Razor'' Adding $\lambda\|\mathbf{w}\|^2$ or $\lambda\|\mathbf{w}\|_1$ to the loss implements Occam's razor algorithmically — penalising complex models. ''Limits of Occam's Razor'' Modern deep learning challenges this: over-parameterised networks generalise well despite extreme complexity. The simplicity principle holds in a generalised sense (implicit regularisation of SGD, flat minima). <<button "Quiz: How does L2 regularisation implement Occam's Razor?">> <<replace "#or-q">> ''✓ It adds a penalty $\lambda\|\mathbf{w}\|^2$ that penalises large parameter magnitudes. This biases the optimiser toward simpler (smaller-weight) solutions — directly embodying the principle of preferring simpler explanations.'' [[Regularization]] | [[Bias-Variance tradeoff]] | [[Statsitical Learning Theory]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="or-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Reconstruction Error'' The loss used to train autoencoders and other self-supervised models — it measures how well the model can recreate its own input from a compressed representation. $$ \mathcal{L}_{\text{recon}} = \|\mathbf{x} - \hat{\mathbf{x}}\|^2 \quad \text{(MSE for continuous data)} $$ For binary inputs, binary cross-entropy is typical. ''Autoencoder Pipeline'' $$ \mathbf{x} \xrightarrow{\text{Encoder}} \mathbf{z} \xrightarrow{\text{Decoder}} \hat{\mathbf{x}} $$ Minimising reconstruction error forces $\mathbf{z}$ to be an informative compressed code. ''Trade-offs'' * ''Too low reconstruction error'': model memorises; $\mathbf{z}$ may not be useful for downstream tasks * ''Too high'': model ignores structure; $\mathbf{z}$ is uninformative * Bottleneck architecture forces meaningful compression ''Variants'' * ''Denoising AE'': reconstruct clean $\mathbf{x}$ from corrupted input — forces robustness * ''Variational AE (VAE)'': adds KL regularisation to $\mathbf{z}$; reconstruction + KL divergence * ''Masked AE (MAE)'': reconstruct masked patches — GPT / BERT for images ''In Clustering (Elbow Method)'' Within-cluster sum of squared distances is a form of reconstruction error — each point reconstructed by its centroid. <<button "Quiz: In a VAE, what is added to the reconstruction loss?">> <<replace "#re-q">> ''✓ A KL divergence term: $D_{KL}(q_\phi(\mathbf{z}|\mathbf{x})\|p(\mathbf{z}))$. It regularises the latent space to be close to a standard Gaussian, enabling smooth sampling.'' [[Ebow Curve]] | [[KL Divergence]] | [[GAN]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="re-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Elbow Curve'' A heuristic method for choosing the number of clusters $k$ in K-Means by looking for a "knee" or "elbow" in a plot of within-cluster inertia vs. $k$. ''Inertia (Within-Cluster SSE)'' $$ \text{Inertia} = \sum_{k} \sum_{\mathbf{x} \in C_k} \|\mathbf{x} - \boldsymbol{\mu}_k\|^2 $$ As $k$ increases, inertia always decreases (more clusters → smaller clusters → lower error). The elbow is the point of diminishing returns — where adding another cluster gives little benefit. ''Reading the Elbow'' * If inertia drops sharply from $k=1$ to $k=3$, then flattens → choose $k=3$ * Elbow is subjective and may not exist for complex data ''Alternative Methods for Choosing $k$'' * ''Silhouette score'': measures how similar a point is to its own cluster vs. others. Maximise. * ''Gap statistic'': compare inertia to reference random datasets * ''BIC/AIC'' (for Gaussian Mixture Models) * ''Calinski-Harabasz index'': ratio of between-cluster to within-cluster dispersion ''Practical Advice'' Domain knowledge often beats the elbow. If you're clustering customers, how many groups are actionable? <<button "Quiz: Why does inertia always decrease as $k$ increases?">> <<replace "#elbow-q">> ''✓ With more clusters, each cluster is smaller and points are closer to their centroid. At $k=n$, inertia = 0 (every point is its own cluster). More clusters always reduce the metric.'' [[K-means Clustering]] | [[Clustering]] | [[Gaussian Mixture Models]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="elbow-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Overfitting Overfitting occurs when a model learns the training data too well — including its noise and idiosyncrasies — and fails to generalise to new examples. It is the central failure mode in machine learning. ''The symptom'' - Training error: very low - Validation / test error: much higher - [[generalization gap]] is large ''Why it happens'' A model with enough capacity can memorise any training set. Given $n$ training points, a polynomial of degree $n-1$ fits them perfectly — but predicts nonsense between them. ''The bias-variance view'' [[Bias-Variance tradeoff]] — overfitting is high variance: small changes in training data cause large changes in the model ''How to detect it'' Plot training and validation loss over epochs. Overfitting shows as validation loss rising while training loss continues to fall. Always use [[Cross Validation]]. ''How to fix it'' [[Regularization]] — L1/L2 penalties, dropout, early stopping [[Cross Validation]] — honest evaluation guides model selection More data — the most reliable fix when available [[Bias-Variance tradeoff]] — simpler model if variance dominates [[Estimating Model Proformance]] — rigorous evaluation framework ''Related concepts'' [[Statsitical Learning Theory]] — formal bounds on when overfitting can occur [[Ockham's Razor]] — prefer simpler models; complexity must be justified by the data [[generalization gap]] — the quantitative measure of overfitting [[holdout]] — keeping a test set unseen until final evaluation --- [[Statistical Machine Learning]] ← back | [[Regularization]] | [[Bias-Variance tradeoff]] <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Precision and Recall Precision and recall measure different aspects of a classifier's performance. Precision asks "when you say positive, how often are you right?" Recall asks "of all the true positives, how many did you find?" ''Definitions'' $$\text{Precision} = \frac{TP}{TP + FP} \qquad \text{Recall} = \frac{TP}{TP + FN}$$ - $TP$ — true positives (correct positive predictions) - $FP$ — false positives (predicted positive, actually negative) - $FN$ — false negatives (predicted negative, actually positive) ''The tradeoff'' Lowering the classification threshold → more positives predicted → recall increases, precision decreases. Raising the threshold → fewer positives → precision increases, recall decreases. There is no free lunch: you cannot simultaneously maximise both without a better model. ''When each matters'' - ''High recall priority'': medical diagnosis, fraud detection — missing a true positive is costly - ''High precision priority'': spam filtering, legal discovery — false alarms are costly - [[F1]] — the harmonic mean; rewards a balanced tradeoff ''Precision-recall curve'' Plot precision vs. recall as threshold varies. The area under this curve (Average Precision) summarises performance. For imbalanced datasets this is more informative than [[ROC curve]]. ''Connections'' [[F1]] — combines precision and recall into one number [[ROC curve]] / [[AUC]] — alternative evaluation using TPR and FPR [[Estimating Model Proformance]] ← broader context --- [[Estimating Model Proformance]] ← back | [[F1]] | [[ROC curve]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Robustness in ML'' A model is robust if its performance degrades gracefully under distribution shift, corruptions, or adversarial perturbations. ''Types of Robustness'' * ''Adversarial robustness'': performance under worst-case $\varepsilon$-bounded input perturbations * ''Distribution robustness (OOD)'': performance on test distributions different from training * ''Corruption robustness'': performance under natural corruptions (blur, noise, weather, compression artefacts) * ''Algorithmic robustness'': performance under training hyperparameter or data variation ''Adversarial Training (Madry et al. 2018)'' The gold standard: train on adversarially perturbed examples: $$ \min_\theta \mathbb{E}_{(x,y)} \left[ \max_{\|\delta\|_p \leq \varepsilon} \mathcal{L}(f_\theta(x+\delta), y) \right] $$ Inner maximisation via PGD; outer minimisation via SGD. ''Certified Defences'' Randomised smoothing (Cohen et al. 2019): perturb input with Gaussian noise, majority-vote over predictions. Provides a provable $\ell_2$ robustness certificate. ''Accuracy–Robustness Trade-off'' Robust models typically have lower clean accuracy. Adversarial training on ImageNet: clean accuracy $-3{-}5\%$, but large adversarial robustness gain. ''Benchmarks'' RobustBench, ImageNet-C (corruptions), WILDS (distribution shift). <<button "Quiz: What is the core idea behind adversarial training?">> <<replace "#rob-q">> ''✓ Augmenting training with adversarial examples: for each batch, solve the inner maximisation (find worst-case perturbation), then update weights to reduce loss on those perturbed examples. The model learns to be robust to worst-case inputs.'' [[Adversarial Attacks]] | [[Evasion attacks]] | [[Deep Learning]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="rob-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Holdout Set'' A portion of data kept entirely separate during model development — used only once at the very end to estimate ''true generalisation performance''. ''Three-Way Split'' | Set | Purpose | Typical Size | | Training | Fit model parameters | 60–70% | | Validation | Tune hyperparameters, architecture | 15–20% | | ''Test / Holdout'' | Final unbiased evaluation | 15–20% | ''Why Holdout Matters'' If you use test data to select models or hyperparameters, the test set leaks into the model selection process — it's no longer an unbiased estimate of generalisation. ''Common Mistakes'' * Peeking at test performance before final evaluation * Re-running experiments until test performance looks good * Normalising/standardising using test set statistics (data leakage) ''When Holdout Is Not Enough'' For small datasets, even a single holdout estimate is noisy. Use [[Cross Validation]] instead — with a final holdout for the chosen configuration. ''Temporal Holdout (Time Series)'' Use the most recent data as the test set. Never shuffle time-series data before splitting. <<button "Quiz: Why must test data never be used during model selection?">> <<replace "#ho-q">> ''✓ Selection pressure causes the chosen model to be "accidentally" fitted to test data even without explicit training on it. The test error becomes an optimistic estimate — no longer unbiased.'' [[Cross Validation]] | [[Overfitting]] | [[Bias-Variance tradeoff]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="ho-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Penalty Term (Regularisation Term)'' A term added to the loss function that penalises model complexity, discouraging overfitting. ''General Form'' $$ \mathcal{L}_{\text{total}} = \underbrace{\mathcal{L}_{\text{data}}(\mathbf{w})}_{\text{fit}} + \underbrace{\lambda \Omega(\mathbf{w})}_{\text{penalty}} $$ $\lambda > 0$ controls the strength of regularisation (a hyperparameter). ''Common Choices of $\Omega$'' | Penalty | Effect | | $\|\mathbf{w}\|_2^2$ (Ridge/L2) | Shrinks weights toward zero; doesn't zero them | | $\|\mathbf{w}\|_1$ (Lasso/L1) | Zeros out small weights; sparsity | | $\|\mathbf{w}\|_1 + \|\mathbf{w}\|_2^2$ (Elastic Net) | Both effects | | KL divergence | Used in VAEs; keeps latent distribution close to prior | | Gradient penalty | Used in WGAN-GP; penalises large input gradients | ''Bayesian Interpretation'' The penalty term corresponds to a prior over weights: $\Omega(\mathbf{w}) = -\log p(\mathbf{w})$. Minimising penalised loss = MAP estimation. * L2 penalty → Gaussian prior * L1 penalty → Laplace prior (heavier tails → sparsity) ''Tuning $\lambda$'' Too small: overfitting. Too large: underfitting. Tune via cross-validation or held-out validation set. <<button "Quiz: What Bayesian prior corresponds to an L2 penalty?">> <<replace "#pt-q">> ''✓ A Gaussian (Normal) prior on weights: $p(\mathbf{w}) \propto \exp(-\lambda\|\mathbf{w}\|^2)$. Ridge regression is equivalent to MAP estimation under a Gaussian prior.'' [[Regularization]] | [[L1]] | [[L2]] | [[Lasso]] | [[Bayesian Methods]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="pt-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Objective Function'' The mathematical quantity that a learning algorithm minimises (or maximises) — the "thing we are optimising." ''Terminology'' * ''Loss function'': usually used for the per-sample error (e.g. cross-entropy on one example) * ''Cost function'': average loss over the entire training set * ''Objective function'': the full expression including regularisation terms * ''Reward'' (RL): maximised instead of minimised ''General Supervised Form'' $$ \mathcal{L}(\mathbf{w}) = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n \ell(y_i, f(\mathbf{x}_i; \mathbf{w})) + \lambda \Omega(\mathbf{w}) $$ ''Choosing the Right Objective'' * ''Regression'': MSE, MAE, Huber loss * ''Classification'': cross-entropy, hinge loss * ''Ranking'': pairwise/contrastive losses (e.g. triplet loss) * ''Generative'': ELBO (VAE), GAN minimax, diffusion score matching * ''RL'': expected cumulative reward $E[\sum_t \gamma^t r_t]$ ''Surrogate Objectives'' The true objective (e.g. 0-1 classification error) is often discontinuous and non-differentiable. We optimise a smooth surrogate (cross-entropy) that correlates with the true goal. <<button "Quiz: Why not directly minimise 0-1 classification error?">> <<replace "#obj-q">> ''✓ The 0-1 loss is piecewise constant — its gradient is zero almost everywhere, making gradient-based optimisation impossible. Cross-entropy is a smooth surrogate with informative gradients.'' [[Loss Functions]] | [[Optimization]] | [[Stocastic Gradient Descent]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="obj-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Null Hypothesis The null hypothesis ($H_0$) is the default assumption of "no effect" or "no difference" that a statistical test tries to disprove. It is the baseline against which evidence is measured. ''The logic of hypothesis testing'' We never prove $H_0$ false with certainty. Instead, we ask: "how surprising would this data be if $H_0$ were true?" If the data would be very surprising under $H_0$, we reject it. [[P-Value]] — the probability of data as extreme or more, assuming $H_0$ is true [[hypothesis test]] — the procedure for deciding whether to reject $H_0$ ''Common null hypotheses'' - "The mean of group A equals the mean of group B" — [[T-test]] - "This regression coefficient is zero" — standard output of any regression - "This feature has no predictive value" — feature selection tests - "The new model is no better than the baseline" — A/B testing ''Type I and Type II errors'' - ''Type I'' (false positive): reject $H_0$ when it is actually true. Rate = $\alpha$ (the significance level) - ''Type II'' (false negative): fail to reject $H_0$ when it is actually false. Rate = $\beta$; power = $1 - \beta$ ''Practical caution'' [[Spurious Correlations]] — with many tests, some will appear significant by chance (multiple comparisons problem). Use Bonferroni correction or FDR control. [[Regression to the Mean]] — measured effects often shrink on replication --- [[Statistics]] ← back | [[P-Value]] | [[hypothesis test]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Spurious Correlations'' A spurious correlation is a statistical association between two variables that is ''not causal'' — produced by a third variable (confounder) or by chance. ''Classic Examples'' * Nicolas Cage films per year ↔ pool drowning deaths: $r \approx 0.67$ * Margarine consumption ↔ divorce rate in Maine: $r \approx 0.99$ * Ice cream sales ↔ shark attacks (common cause: hot weather) ''Why Spurious Correlations Arise'' * ''Confounders'': a hidden variable $Z$ causes both $X$ and $Y$ * ''Multiple testing'': with enough features, some will correlate by chance ($p$-hacking) * ''Small samples'': random fluctuations look structured ''ML Consequences'' Models exploit spurious correlations in training data. A medical classifier that correlates hospital watermarks with disease severity will fail on images from different hospitals — ''shortcut learning''. ''Defences'' * Hold-out on different data distributions (OOD testing) * Causal feature selection * Invariant Risk Minimisation (IRM) * Collect data from varied environments ''Key Message'' "Correlation is not causation" is a cliché because it is always true and always violated. <<button "Quiz: What is a confounder?">> <<replace "#sc-q">> ''✓ A confounder $Z$ is a variable that causally influences both the predictor $X$ and the outcome $Y$, creating a spurious association between them even without a direct $X \to Y$ link.'' [[Causal Modeling]] | [[Regression to the Mean]] | [[null hypothesis]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="sc-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Regression to the Mean'' If you select extreme values of a noisy variable and measure it again, the second measurement will tend to be closer to the population mean. First described by Francis Galton (1886). ''Formal Statement'' If $X = \mu + \varepsilon$ with measurement noise $\varepsilon$, then $$ E[X_2 | X_1 = x] = \mu + \rho(x - \mu) $$ where $\rho$ is the correlation between measurements. Since $|\rho| \leq 1$, the conditional expectation is pulled toward $\mu$. ''Everyday Examples'' * "Sophomore slump": a player with an outstanding rookie year tends to perform less well next year — not because they got worse, but because some of year 1 was luck * Medical trials: patients recruited because of extreme symptoms improve at follow-up even without treatment * Sports Illustrated jinx: athletes on the cover tend to perform worse afterward ''ML Implications'' * Hyperparameter search: the best configuration on a validation set may look less impressive on a fresh test set * Early stopping: the stopping point is often found on a lucky batch * Evaluate models on held-out data — don't compare to the peak validation score <<button "Quiz: Why do extreme scorers tend to score closer to average on a retest?">> <<replace "#rtm-q">> ''✓ Extreme scores mix true ability and random noise. On retest, noise is independent — so the lucky noise component is gone, and the score reverts toward the true mean.'' [[Spurious Correlations]] | [[Causal Modeling]] | [[Statistics]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="rtm-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ! The Bias-Variance Tradeoff <img src="assets/bias_variance.svg" alt="Bias-variance tradeoff — error curves (left) and target diagrams (right)" style="width:100%;max-width:620px;display:block;margin:16px auto 24px auto;"> Every model you train makes errors. The bias-variance tradeoff is the framework for understanding //where those errors come from//. It tells you whether to make your model more complex or simpler, and why you can't just keep adding capacity forever. --- !! Two Sources of Error Suppose you train the same model architecture many times on different samples from the same distribution. The predictions will vary. This variation decomposes into three parts: $$\text{Expected Error} = \text{Bias}^2 + \text{Variance} + \text{Irreducible Noise}$$ ''Bias'' is the error from wrong assumptions. A model with high bias is too simple to capture the true pattern — it underfits. Its average prediction is systematically off, regardless of how much data you give it. ''Variance'' is the error from sensitivity to the particular training set. A model with high variance fits the training data very closely — including its noise — and fails to generalise. Give it a different sample and it produces a very different model. ''Irreducible noise'' is the inherent randomness in the data. No model can eliminate this. --- !! The Bullseye Picture Think of a target. Each training run produces one shot. ''Bias'' is how far the average shot is from the centre. ''Variance'' is how spread out the shots are. You want shots clustered at the bullseye — low bias and low variance. The tradeoff is that simple models cluster tightly (low variance) but aim at the wrong place (high bias), while complex models aim roughly at the right place on average (low bias) but scatter wildly (high variance). --- !! The U-Shaped Test Error Curve As model complexity increases: * ''Training error'' falls monotonically — a more complex model always fits training data better. * ''Test error'' falls at first (the model is learning real signal), then rises (the model starts fitting noise). The minimum of the test error curve is the sweet spot — just enough complexity to capture the true pattern without memorising the training set. This is why you need a [[Cross Validation|held-out validation set]]. Training error tells you nothing about where you are on this curve. --- !! Diagnosing Your Model High training error //and// high test error → high bias. The model is too simple. Fix: more capacity (deeper network, more features, lower regularisation). Low training error, high test error → high variance. The model is overfitting. Fix: more data, regularisation (L1/L2, dropout), simpler model, early stopping. Low training error, low test error → well calibrated. Don't touch it. The gap between training and test error is your variance indicator. The absolute level of training error is your bias indicator. --- !! The Modern Complication: Double Descent Classical wisdom says test error follows a U-shape. But massively overparameterised modern networks — transformers with billions of parameters — show a second descent: after a peak at interpolation threshold (where parameters = data points), test error //falls again// as you add more parameters. This ''double descent'' phenomenon challenges the classical framing but doesn't invalidate it: in the practical regime where most ML happens, the tradeoff still governs. --- !! Technical Details ''Formal decomposition:'' for squared loss, $\mathbb{E}[(y - \hat{f}(x))^2] = (\mathbb{E}[\hat{f}(x)] - f(x))^2 + \mathbb{E}[(\hat{f}(x) - \mathbb{E}[\hat{f}(x)])^2] + \sigma^2$, where $\sigma^2$ is irreducible noise. ''Regularisation reduces variance'' by penalising large weights: L2 (ridge) adds $\lambda\|w\|^2$ to the loss; L1 (lasso) adds $\lambda\|w\|_1$ and induces sparsity. ''Ensemble methods'' reduce variance by averaging many models (bagging). Boosting reduces bias by sequentially correcting errors. --- !! Quiz ''1. What is bias in the bias-variance sense?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#bv-q1">>'✓ Bias is the error from systematic wrong assumptions in the model. Formally, it is the difference between the model's average prediction (over many training sets) and the true function: $(\mathbb{E}[\hat{f}(x)] - f(x))^2$. High bias = underfitting — the model consistently predicts in the wrong direction, regardless of data size.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="bv-q1"></span> ''2. What is variance in the bias-variance sense?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#bv-q2">>'✓ Variance is the error from sensitivity to the specific training set. Formally: $\mathbb{E}[(\hat{f}(x) - \mathbb{E}[\hat{f}(x)])^2]$. A high-variance model changes dramatically when trained on different samples of the same size — it has memorised noise rather than signal. High variance = overfitting.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="bv-q2"></span> ''3. Your model has low training error but high test error. What does this tell you?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#bv-q3">>'✓ High variance — the model is overfitting. It has memorised the training set including its noise, and fails to generalise. The large train/test gap is the variance signature. Fixes: more training data, stronger regularisation (L2, dropout), simpler model architecture, or early stopping.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="bv-q3"></span> ''4. Why does training error always decrease as model complexity increases?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#bv-q4">>'✓ A more complex model has more parameters and can fit any given training set more closely — in the limit, it memorises every training point exactly (interpolation). Training error measures fit to the training data, not generalisation. This is why training error is a misleading metric: it tells you how well you memorised, not how well you learned.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="bv-q4"></span> ''5. How does L2 regularisation reduce variance?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#bv-q5">>'✓ L2 (ridge) adds $\lambda\|w\|^2$ to the loss, penalising large weights. This shrinks weights toward zero, preventing the model from fitting noise too closely. Smaller weights = smoother, less wiggly decision boundaries = lower variance. The penalty $\lambda$ controls the tradeoff: large $\lambda$ increases bias but reduces variance.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="bv-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Hypothesis Testing A hypothesis test is a formal procedure for deciding whether data provides sufficient evidence to reject a default assumption (the [[null hypothesis]]). It is the foundation of statistical inference and experimental science. ''The procedure'' 1. State $H_0$ (null) and $H_1$ (alternative) 2. Choose a test statistic and significance level $\alpha$ (typically 0.05) 3. Compute the test statistic from your data 4. Calculate the [[P-Value]]: $P(\text{statistic this extreme} \mid H_0)$ 5. If $p < \alpha$, reject $H_0$ ''Common tests'' [[T-test]] — compare means; one-sample, two-sample, or paired [[A/B Testing]] — comparing two treatments; the t-test applied to experiments Chi-squared test — test for independence between categorical variables F-test — compare variances or nested models ''Statistical power'' Power = $P(\text{reject } H_0 \mid H_1 \text{ true})$ = $1 - P(\text{Type II error})$. Low power → you might miss real effects. Power increases with sample size and effect size.
