MAML (Model-Agnostic Meta-Learning) finds initialisations that fine-tune with very few gradient steps. $$ \theta^* = \theta - \alpha \nabla_\theta \mathcal{L}_{\text{support}} $$ Then update $\theta$ to minimise $\mathcal{L}_{\text{query}}$ after these inner steps. ''3. Foundation Model + Prompting'' Large language models exhibit ''in-context few-shot learning'': prepend examples in the prompt, no weight update needed. ''Applications'' Medical imaging (rare diseases), drug discovery (few assay results), personalisation (few user examples). <<button "Quiz: How do prototypical networks classify at test time?">> <<replace "#fsl-q">> ''✓ Each class is represented by the mean embedding of its support examples (the prototype). A query is classified as the class whose prototype is nearest in embedding space.'' [[Transfer Learning]] | [[Feature Learning]] | [[Deep Learning]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="fsl-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''YOLO — You Only Look Once'' A family of real-time object detection models that frame detection as a single regression problem rather than a two-stage proposal + classification pipeline. ''Core Idea'' Divide the image into an $S \times S$ grid. Each cell predicts $B$ bounding boxes and $C$ class probabilities simultaneously in a single forward pass. Each bounding box predicts: * $(x, y)$: centre relative to cell * $(w, h)$: width/height relative to image * Confidence: $P(\text{object}) \times \text{IoU}$ ''vs Two-Stage Detectors (R-CNN family)'' | Property | YOLO | Faster R-CNN | | Speed | 30–100+ FPS | 5–15 FPS | | Accuracy | Slightly lower | Higher (historically) | | Architecture | Single-stage | Region proposal + classifier | ''Loss Function'' Multi-component loss: bounding box regression (MSE on coordinates) + confidence (MSE) + classification (cross-entropy). Different components weighted to balance learning. ''YOLO Versions'' * YOLOv1 (Redmon 2016): original single-pass detection * YOLOv3/v4: anchor boxes, multi-scale detection * YOLOv5/v8 (Ultralytics): modern re-implementations; dominant in practice ''Applications'' Autonomous vehicles, surveillance, real-time robotics, medical imaging, sports analytics. <<button "Quiz: What makes YOLO faster than two-stage detectors?">> <<replace "#yolo-q">> ''✓ YOLO processes the entire image in a single network pass, predicting all bounding boxes simultaneously. Two-stage detectors first generate region proposals, then classify each — requiring multiple forward passes and much more compute.'' [[CNN]] | [[Transfer Learning]] | [[Feature Engineering]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="yolo-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Distributions A probability distribution describes how likely each possible value of a random variable is. Before choosing a model or loss function, understanding the distribution of your data is the first and most important step. ''Continuous distributions'' [[Gaussian Distribution]] — the bell curve; defined by mean $\mu$ and variance $\sigma^2$: $$p(x) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi\sigma^2}}\exp\!\left(-\frac{(x-\mu)^2}{2\sigma^2}\right)$$ Appears everywhere by the Central Limit Theorem. [[Beta Distribtuion]] — values between 0 and 1; used to model probabilities and proportions [[Pareto Distribution]] — heavy-tailed; models wealth, city sizes, word frequencies ''Discrete distributions'' [[Bernoulli Distribution]] — single binary trial; $P(X=1) = p$ [[Poisson Distribution]] — count of rare events in a fixed interval; parameter $\lambda$ ''Why distributions matter for ML'' - Loss functions are derived from distributional assumptions (MSE ↔ Gaussian, log loss ↔ Bernoulli) - [[Maximum Likelihood Estimation]] — finding parameters that make the data most probable - [[Gaussian Mixture Models]] — modelling data as a blend of Gaussians - [[Bayesian Methods]] — distributions over parameters, not just point estimates ''Measuring and comparing'' [[KL Divergence]] — how much one distribution differs from another [[Expected Value]] — the mean under a distribution [[Independent Draws From Same Distibution]] — the i.i.d. assumption --- [[Statistics]] ← back | [[Gaussian Distribution]] | [[Maximum Likelihood Estimation]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Common Deep Learning Errors'' A taxonomy of failure modes and their fixes. | Error | Symptom | Fix | | ''Vanishing gradient'' | Loss stops decreasing; early layers don't learn | ReLU/residuals/batch-norm/careful init | | ''Exploding gradient'' | Loss goes to NaN; very large parameter updates | Gradient clipping; lower LR | | ''Dying ReLU'' | Units output 0 forever | Leaky ReLU / ELU / initialisation | | ''Overfitting'' | Train loss << test loss | Dropout, weight decay, more data | | ''Underfitting'' | Both losses high | Bigger model, longer training, better features | | ''Covariate shift'' | Activation distributions shift between batches | Batch / layer normalisation | | ''Rank collapse'' | Attention heads become identical | Spectral norm, orthogonal reg | | ''Mode collapse'' (GAN) | Generator produces few patterns | Wasserstein GAN, spectral norm | | ''Label noise'' | Model memorises wrong labels | Label smoothing, robust losses | ''Debugging Protocol'' 1. Overfit a single batch first — if it can't, the architecture is broken 2. Check gradient magnitudes per layer (use hooks) 3. Monitor training metrics (loss, gradient norm, activation statistics) 4. Ablate components systematically <<button "Quiz: What is the 'dying ReLU' problem?">> <<replace "#dle-q">> ''✓ A ReLU neuron that receives only negative inputs will always output 0 — and since its gradient is also 0, it can never recover via backpropagation. The neuron is permanently dead.'' [[vanishing gradient]] | [[activations]] | [[normalization]] | [[Deep Learning]] | [[Home]] <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="dle-q"></span> <<include "Footer">> [[Ockham's Razor]] [[Spurious Correlations]] [[Robustness]] [[Regression to the Mean]] [[Bias-Variance tradeoff]] [[generalization gap]]] [[Convex versus Concave]] [[holdout]] [[decision boundary]] [[Goodharts law]] <<include "Header">> ''HodgeRank'' A ranking method based on algebraic topology (Hodge decomposition) that decomposes pairwise comparison data into a globally consistent ranking plus an inconsistency measure. ''Problem Setup'' Given pairwise comparisons between items (ratings, preferences, tournament results), find a global ranking that best explains the data. ''Hodge Decomposition'' Pairwise comparison data lives on the edges of a graph. Hodge theory decomposes this into three orthogonal components: * ''Gradient component'': consistent with a global ranking (curl-free) * ''Curl component'': cyclic inconsistencies (A > B > C > A) * ''Harmonic component'': inconsistencies due to incomplete graph structure $$ f = f_{\text{gradient}} + f_{\text{curl}} + f_{\text{harmonic}} $$ The gradient component gives the HodgeRank ordering; the curl magnitude measures how consistent the data is with any global ranking. ''Applications'' Ranking from pairwise preferences (RLHF reward models), sports rankings, crowdsourced ratings, collaborative filtering from implicit feedback. The ratio of curl energy to total energy is a diagnostic: high curl → pairwise comparisons are fundamentally inconsistent, no ranking will fit well. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Activation Functions Activation functions introduce nonlinearity into neural networks. Without them, stacked linear layers collapse to a single linear transformation, no matter how deep the network. The choice of activation affects training stability, gradient flow, and expressive power. ''Sigmoid'' [[sigmoid function]]: $$\sigma(x) = \frac{1}{1+e^{-x}} \in (0,1)$$ Maps any input to $(0,1)$. Used in output layers for binary classification. Problem: saturates at both extremes, causing [[vanishing gradient]] in deep networks. ''Tanh'' $$\tanh(x) = \frac{e^x - e^{-x}}{e^x + e^{-x}} \in (-1, 1)$$ Zero-centred (unlike sigmoid). Still saturates; still causes vanishing gradients. ''ReLU'' [[ReLU]]: $$f(x) = \max(0, x)$$ Non-saturating for $x > 0$; gradient is always 1 or 0. Default choice for hidden layers. Problem: "dying ReLU" — units stuck at 0 if they receive consistently negative inputs. ''ELU and variants'' [[Elu]]: $f(x) = x$ if $x > 0$, else $\alpha(e^x - 1)$. Smoother than ReLU; avoids dying units. Leaky ReLU: small negative slope for $x < 0$. GELU: Gaussian error linear unit; used in BERT and GPT. ''Softmax (output layer)'' $$\text{softmax}(\mathbf{z})_k = \frac{e^{z_k}}{\sum_j e^{z_j}}$$ Converts a vector of scores to a probability distribution. Used for multi-class classification output. ''Rule of thumb'' Hidden layers: ReLU (or GELU for transformers). Output: sigmoid (binary), softmax (multiclass), linear (regression). --- [[Deep Learning]] ← back | [[ReLU]] | [[vanishing gradient]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Search / Similarity Search'' Finding the nearest neighbours of a query point efficiently — the core operation in retrieval-augmented generation, recommendation, and metric learning. ''Exact Search'' Brute-force: compute distance to every point — $O(nd)$. Correct but slow for large $n$. ''Approximate Nearest Neighbour (ANN)'' Trade a small accuracy loss for massive speed gains: * ''LSH (Locality Sensitive Hashing)'': hash similar points to same bucket with high probability; $O(1)$ expected query time * ''HNSW (Hierarchical NSW)'': navigable small-world graph; builds a multi-layer proximity graph; used in Faiss, Weaviate, Pinecone * ''IVF (Inverted File Index)'': cluster database; search only in nearest clusters; used in Faiss ''Vector Databases'' Pinecone, Weaviate, Qdrant, Chroma, Milvus — purpose-built for ANN search over embedding vectors, used in RAG pipelines. ''Evaluation Metrics'' * Recall@k: fraction of true k-NNs retrieved * Queries per second (QPS) * Index build time / memory ''Usage'': semantic search, [[Embeddings]] lookup in RAG, product recommendation, duplicate detection. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Hyperparameter Search Hyperparameters are the settings you choose before training — learning rate, batch size, network depth, regularisation strength. Unlike model parameters, they are not learned by gradient descent. Finding good hyperparameters is essential for strong performance. ''Why it's hard'' The relationship between hyperparameters and validation performance is complex, noisy, and expensive to evaluate. Each evaluation requires a full training run. ''Methods'' [[grid search]] — exhaustively evaluate every combination on a predefined grid. Simple but exponentially expensive in the number of hyperparameters. ''Random search'' — sample hyperparameter combinations randomly. Surprisingly effective: if only a few hyperparameters matter, random search is likely to find good values for them. [[automl]] — automated methods: Bayesian optimization (fit a surrogate model of performance, query it to choose next point), Hyperband (early stopping of bad runs), neural architecture search. ''What to tune'' In rough order of importance: 1. Learning rate — most impactful single hyperparameter 2. Regularisation strength ($\lambda$, dropout rate) 3. Network architecture (depth, width) 4. Batch size 5. Optimiser (usually Adam; rarely needs changing) ''Cross validation'' [[Cross Validation]] — always tune on a validation set, never the test set. Use k-fold CV for small datasets. ''Practical advice'' Start with a coarse random search over 2–3 orders of magnitude. Identify promising regions. Run a finer search in those regions. Never grid search in high dimensions. --- [[Optimization]] ← back | [[grid search]] | [[automl]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Quadratic Functions'' A quadratic function in one variable: $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$, $a \neq 0$. ''In $n$ Dimensions'' $$ f(\mathbf{x}) = \mathbf{x}^\top A \mathbf{x} + \mathbf{b}^\top \mathbf{x} + c $$ If $A \succ 0$ (positive definite): strictly convex — unique global minimum at $\nabla f = 0$: $$ 2A\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{b} = 0 \implies \mathbf{x}^* = -\frac{1}{2}A^{-1}\mathbf{b} $$ ''Quadratic Loss = MSE'' Mean squared error is a quadratic in the parameters — this is why linear regression has a closed-form solution (the normal equations). ''Quadratic Programming (QP)'' Minimise a quadratic objective subject to linear constraints. SVMs (hard and soft margin) are solved as QPs. ''Second-Order Optimisation'' Newton's method minimises a quadratic approximation at each step: $f(\mathbf{x}+\delta) \approx f(\mathbf{x}) + \nabla f^\top \delta + \frac{1}{2}\delta^\top H \delta$, solved in one step when $f$ is exactly quadratic. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Polynomial Functions and Features'' A degree-$d$ polynomial in one variable: $p(x) = \sum_{k=0}^d a_k x^k$ ''Polynomial Feature Expansion'' Extending a feature vector $\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{R}^p$ with all monomials up to degree $d$ converts a linear model into a polynomial one without changing the learning algorithm. Example (degree 2, 2 features): $[x_1, x_2] \to [1, x_1, x_2, x_1^2, x_1 x_2, x_2^2]$ Dimension grows as $\binom{p+d}{d}$ — exponential in $d$. ''Polynomial Kernel Trick'' The polynomial kernel $k(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{x}') = (\mathbf{x}^\top \mathbf{x}' + c)^d$ implicitly computes the inner product in the expanded space without materialising it. ''Universal Approximation'' By the Weierstrass approximation theorem, any continuous function on a compact domain can be approximated arbitrarily closely by a polynomial. Neural networks achieve this with far fewer parameters than a high-degree polynomial. ''Overfitting Risk'' High-degree polynomials interpolate data perfectly but oscillate wildly between points (Runge's phenomenon). Regularise or use a lower degree. <<include "Footer">> [[National AI Research Resource]] [[Flatrion]] [[Bulgarian]] [[Jackson Labs]] [[Argonne National Labs]] [[JPL]] [[Lawrence livermore]] [[NASA Ames]] [[NASA Goddard]] [[MITRE]] [[Rand]] <<include "Header">> ''Flatiron Institute'' A private, non-profit scientific research institute funded by the Simons Foundation, headquartered in New York City (est. 2016). Unusual model: gives computational scientists protected research time — no teaching, no grant-writing. Attracts top researchers with academic-style freedom and industry-level resources. ''Centers relevant to ML/data science'' * Center for Computational Biology (CCB) — single-cell genomics, protein structure, connectomics * Center for Computational Mathematics (CCM) — numerical methods, optimisation, ML theory * Center for Computational Astrophysics (CCA) — ML for telescopes and simulations * Center for Computational Neuroscience (CCN) — neural data analysis, computational models ''ML Contributions'' * Scattering transform and geometric deep learning collaborations * Efficient algorithms for large-scale matrix problems * Probabilistic methods for single-cell RNA-seq (scVI, scANVI) ''Philosophy'': fundamental research that industry won't fund and academia can't sustain — positioned between university and private lab. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Bulgarian AI Research'' Bulgaria has a notable history in mathematics, theoretical computer science, and increasingly ML research. ''Bulgarian Academy of Sciences (BAS)'' Founded 1869; Institute of Mathematics and Informatics conducts research in algorithms, computational intelligence, and statistical learning. ''Academic Institutions'' * Sofia University "St. Kliment Ohridski" — strong maths and CS departments * Technical University of Sofia * American University in Bulgaria — English-language, US-accredited ''Notable Researchers'' Several prominent ML researchers have Bulgarian roots: * Ivan Sutskever (born in Russia, raised partly in Israel; OpenAI co-founder) — co-inventor of AlexNet and a key figure in deep learning's rise * Researchers across EU and US labs with Bulgarian backgrounds ''Broader Eastern European Tradition'' Strong olympiad mathematics culture (Bulgaria consistently ranks highly in IMO) feeds into theoretical ML talent. This tradition of rigorous mathematical training has produced many contributors to foundations of learning theory and algorithms. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''The Jackson Laboratory (JAX)'' Independent non-profit biomedical research institution, founded 1929, headquartered in Bar Harbor, Maine. ''Mission'': accelerate genomic medicine for human health. ''ML/Data Science Relevance'' JAX is a major producer and distributor of standardised mouse models — generating large-scale phenotypic and genomic datasets that drive ML in biomedicine: * Mouse Phenome Database: high-dimensional phenotypic measurements * Cancer genetics: somatic mutation data, tumour microenvironment * Single-cell genomics: transcriptomics datasets used to benchmark ML methods ''Computational Science at JAX'' Growing computational biology group applying: * Graph neural networks for gene regulatory networks * Federated learning across clinical sites * ML for electronic health record phenotyping * Foundation models for genomic sequences ''Note on the name'' "JAX" as a ML framework (Google's autodiff library) is unrelated — named after its functional design philosophy, not the institution. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Argonne National Laboratory'' US Department of Energy (DOE) national laboratory operated by the University of Chicago, located near Chicago, Illinois. One of the largest US national labs (~3,400 employees). ''Relevance to AI/ML'' ''Aurora supercomputer'' (2024): Intel Xe GPU-based exascale system (>1 exaFLOP). One of the world's largest computers; used for scientific ML at scale: * Molecular dynamics simulations for drug discovery * Climate and energy systems modelling * High-energy physics data analysis (LHC data) ''AI Research Groups'' * Mathematics and Computer Science (MCS) division: scalable ML algorithms, scientific foundation models * Leadership Computing Facility: training large models for science (genomics, materials, climate) * Collaborative with DOE's AI for Science initiative ''Scientific ML Focus Areas'' * Physics-informed neural networks (PINNs) * Uncertainty quantification for simulations * Autonomous experimental design (self-driving labs) * Graph neural networks for materials discovery <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Jet Propulsion Laboratory (JPL)'' Federally funded research and development centre managed by Caltech for NASA, located in Pasadena, California. Founded 1936. ''Missions'': Mars rovers (Perseverance, Curiosity), Voyager, Cassini, James Webb Space Telescope operations, Earth observation satellites. ''ML at JPL'' * ''Autonomous navigation'': onboard ML for Mars terrain classification and hazard avoidance. Perseverance uses a DNN to autonomously drive 200m/day without Earth commands. * ''Remote sensing'': deep learning for land cover classification, wildfire detection, flood mapping from satellite imagery * ''Anomaly detection'': unsupervised methods for spacecraft telemetry monitoring * ''Image processing'': deep learning for super-resolution and artefact removal in planetary images * ''Scheduling'': constraint satisfaction and RL for deep space network scheduling ''Data Products'' JPL produces massive Earth observation datasets (SMAP soil moisture, GRACE groundwater, OCO-2 carbon) used in climate ML research. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory (LLNL)'' DOE national security laboratory in Livermore, California, managed by Lawrence Livermore National Security LLC. Founded 1952. ''Primary Mission'': nuclear weapons stewardship, national security science. ''ML/AI Relevance'' ''El Capitan'' (2024): AMD Instinct MI300A-based exascale system (~2 exaFLOPs), one of the world's most powerful computers. ''ML Research at LLNL'' * Physics-informed ML for inertial confinement fusion (NIF — National Ignition Facility) * Neural network potentials for molecular dynamics (massively accelerating atomistic simulation) * Uncertainty quantification for high-consequence predictions * Graph neural networks for materials science * Anomaly detection in national security contexts ''Open-Source Contributions'' * LBANN: Livermore Big Artificial Neural Network toolkit — optimised for HPC clusters * Maestro and MERLIN: ML workflow orchestration tools * Collaboration with MLCommons on HPC ML benchmarks <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''NASA Ames Research Center'' Located in Silicon Valley (Mountain View, CA), Ames is NASA's primary center for information technology research, astrobiology, and aeronautics. ''ML/AI Activities'' * ''Supercomputing'': Pleiades supercomputer — used for climate simulation, CFD, and ML training * ''Earth science'': deep learning for wildfire spread prediction, cloud classification, sea ice extent * ''Exoplanet detection'': ML classification of Kepler/TESS light curves (Google collaboration used neural networks to find exoplanets) * ''Air traffic management'': ML for NextGen aviation congestion prediction * ''Autonomous systems'': ML for UAV conflict detection and resolution ''Quantum Computing'' Hosts the NASA Quantum Artificial Intelligence Laboratory (QuAIL) — research into quantum ML, quantum annealing (D-Wave), and quantum optimisation for NASA scheduling problems. ''Notable Collaborations'' Historically strong ties with Stanford, UCSB, and Silicon Valley industry. The adjacent NASA Research Park hosts Google, Carnegie Mellon, and other technology organisations. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''NASA Goddard Space Flight Center'' Located in Greenbelt, Maryland (near Washington DC). NASA's largest research centre (~10,000 employees); primary centre for Earth science and space science. ''ML/AI Activities'' * ''Earth observation ML'': processing data from MODIS, Landsat, Sentinel — land cover, vegetation index, ocean colour * ''Climate emulators'': fast neural network surrogates for full climate models; enable massive ensemble runs * ''Heliophysics'': ML for solar flare prediction, geomagnetic storm forecasting * ''Atmospheric science'': deep learning for precipitation estimation (IMERG), cloud typing * ''JWST data'': ML pipelines for spectroscopic analysis, exoplanet atmosphere characterisation ''Key Data Products'' MERRA-2 reanalysis, GEOS atmospheric model outputs, GRACE/GRACE-FO gravity data — widely used as inputs and validation targets for ML models in climate and hydrology. ''Scientific Computing'' Discover supercomputer: used for large-scale climate and data processing workloads, increasingly including DL inference pipelines. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''MITRE Corporation'' Federally funded research and development centre (FFRDC) operating multiple US government research centres. Non-profit; not-for-profit; headquartered in McLean, VA and Bedford, MA. Founded 1958. ''Operates FFRDCs for'' * DoD (DoD FFRDC — C2E) * FAA, IRS, CMS, DHS, and others ''ML/AI Work at MITRE'' * ''ATLAS'' (Adversarial Threat Landscape for AI Systems): knowledge base of adversarial ML attack tactics and techniques — the AI equivalent of MITRE ATT&CK for cybersecurity * Trustworthy AI: robustness, fairness, explainability frameworks for government systems * NLP for intelligence analysis * Computer vision for surveillance and reconnaissance * Automated vulnerability discovery in software ''MITRE ATT&CK'' The original ATT&CK framework (Adversarial Tactics, Techniques and Common Knowledge) is a globally used knowledge base of cyber adversary behaviour — widely used in threat intelligence and security operations. ''Policy Relevance'' MITRE advises Congress and federal agencies; produces influential reports on AI adoption in government and national security applications. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''RAND Corporation'' Non-profit global policy think tank founded in 1948, headquartered in Santa Monica, CA. Originally spun out of Douglas Aircraft Company to advise the US Air Force. ''AI Policy Research'' RAND is one of the most influential organisations producing independent AI policy analysis: * AI governance frameworks and international standards * AI risks to national security and critical infrastructure * AI in warfare (autonomous weapons, LAWS) * Societal impacts: job displacement, inequality, misinformation ''Technical AI Work'' RAND's research incorporates quantitative and computational methods: * ML-based text analysis for policy document review * Simulation of AI-enabled military scenarios * Forecasting AI capabilities and timelines ''Notable Reports'' * "Findings and Implications for DoD" series on AI adoption * "The Malicious Use of Artificial Intelligence" (with Oxford, Cambridge, OpenAI, 2018) * Analyses of AI arms race dynamics and risk of miscalculation ''Role in the AI Policy Ecosystem'' Alongside CSET (Georgetown), FLI, ARC, and CAIS, RAND occupies the "credible non-partisan analyst" niche — producing research read by both sides of the aisle. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Major ML/AI Conferences'' Academic conferences are how ML research is primarily disseminated — unlike many fields, papers are often posted to arXiv before or alongside conference submission. ''Top Venues by Tier'' ''Tier 1 (most competitive, broadest impact)'' [[NeurIPS]] | [[ICML]] | [[ICLR]] ''Computer Vision'' [[CVPR]] | [[ECCV]] | ICCV ''AI / Reasoning'' [[AAAI]] | IJCAI ''Data Mining / Applied ML'' [[KDD]] | WWW | SIGIR ''Statistics / Probabilistic ML'' [[AI Stats]] | UAI ''Journals (peer-reviewed)'' [[JLMR]] | TPAMI | JAIR ''Publication Norms'' * Papers are ~8–10 pages + appendix * Double-blind review (author identity hidden from reviewers) * Acceptance rates: NeurIPS ~25%, ICML ~25%, ICLR ~30%, CVPR ~25% * Camera-ready often on arXiv simultaneously ''Workshops and Tutorials'' All major venues run co-located workshops on emerging topics — often where ideas appear before they reach the main track. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''KDD — Knowledge Discovery and Data Mining'' The premier data mining and applied ML conference; strong industry presence alongside academia. ''Founded'': 1989 (workshops); conference 1995 | ''Organiser'': ACM SIGKDD | ''Timing'': August ''Character'': bridges academic ML research and industrial data science practice. Strong emphasis on scalability, real-world systems, and applied impact. ''Tracks'' * Research track: algorithmic contributions * Applied Data Science track: deployed systems and real-world results * ADS papers must demonstrate deployment and business impact ''Notable contributions'' * Gradient boosting and boosted trees papers * Graph mining and social network analysis * Recommender systems (Netflix-style) * Anomaly detection in streams * XGBoost was presented here alongside ICML ''Industry Presence'' KDD draws practitioners from industry heavily — talks from Amazon, Google, Microsoft, Airbnb on deployed ML systems. The Applied Data Science track papers often have more citations than research track papers. ''KDD Cup'': annual data science competition run alongside the conference; has launched careers and benchmarks. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''ICML — International Conference on Machine Learning'' One of the top three ML conferences; focus on algorithmic and theoretical machine learning. ''Founded'': 1980 | ''Location'': varies | ''Timing'': July ''Scale'': ~6,000–10,000 attendees; ~1,800 accepted papers from ~9,000 submissions (2023). ''Character'': more theory-focused than NeurIPS; strong optimisation, learning theory, probabilistic methods, and statistical ML papers. Also covers applications and systems. ''Notable contributions historically published at ICML'' * Random Forests — Breiman 2001 * XGBoost — Chen & Guestrin 2016 * Many foundational reinforcement learning papers * Deep learning theory (double descent, NTK, etc.) ''Co-located events'': AutoML workshop, Workshop on Distribution Shift, Responsible Decision Making. ''Relationship to NeurIPS/ICLR'' The "big three" together cover essentially all significant ML research. Many groups submit the same work to multiple venues sequentially; arXiv preprints make this transparent. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''NeurIPS — Neural Information Processing Systems'' One of the top three ML conferences. Originally focused on computational neuroscience; now covers all of ML and AI. ''Founded'': 1987 | ''Location'': varies (Vancouver, New Orleans, etc.) | ''Timing'': December ''Scale'': ~15,000–16,000 attendees (2023); ~3,500 accepted papers from ~12,000 submissions. ''Notable first appearances at NeurIPS'' * AlexNet (technically NIPS 2012) — kicked off deep learning era * Generative Adversarial Networks (GAN) — Goodfellow 2014 * Attention is All You Need (Transformer) — 2017 (though also NIPS) * Dropout — 2012 * Word2Vec was ICLR but heavily NeurIPS follow-ups ''Format'': posters dominate; oral presentations are highly competitive (~100/year). Workshops on the last two days cover cutting-edge topics. ''Competition tracks'': ML competitions, datasets/benchmarks track, demonstrations. ''arXiv culture'': most NeurIPS papers are on arXiv before the conference — "NeurIPS paper" = stamp of quality, not primary dissemination mechanism. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''ICLR — International Conference on Learning Representations'' Youngest of the top three ML conferences, founded by Yoshua Bengio and Yann LeCun to focus specifically on representation learning. ''Founded'': 2013 | ''Location'': varies | ''Timing'': April/May ''Distinctive features'' * ''OpenReview'': all submissions and reviewer comments are public — unprecedented transparency in academic publishing * Strong community of discussion: authors can respond to reviews; community comments allowed * Focus: deep learning, unsupervised learning, self-supervised learning, representation quality ''Notable firsts at ICLR'' * Generative Adversarial Networks follow-ups * VAEs (Kingma & Welling) * Word2Vec was primarily ICLR 2013 * Neural Architecture Search * Many foundational LLM papers ''Scale'': ~5,000–8,000 attendees; ~1,500 accepted from ~7,000 submissions. ''Prestige trajectory'': from small niche workshop in 2013 to co-equal with NeurIPS/ICML by 2017–2018, tracking the rise of deep learning. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''CVPR — Computer Vision and Pattern Recognition'' The premier computer vision conference; often has the highest total paper count of any ML venue. ''Founded'': 1983 | ''Organiser'': IEEE | ''Timing'': June ''Scale'': ~9,000–10,000 accepted papers (2023); ~30,000 attendees. Largest ML venue by volume. ''Topics'': image classification, detection, segmentation, 3D reconstruction, video understanding, image generation, medical imaging, autonomous driving perception, multimodal models. ''Notable firsts'' * AlexNet won the ImageNet challenge (ILSVRC) whose results were presented at CVPR/NIPS 2012 * ResNet (He et al.) — CVPR 2016 * YOLO family — CVPR 2016 onward * Segment Anything (SAM) — CVPR 2023 ''Relationship to other venues'' CVPR, ICCV (odd years), and ECCV (even years) form the "big three" of computer vision. CVPR is the largest and most prestigious; ICCV is slightly more selective. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''ECCV — European Conference on Computer Vision'' Major computer vision conference held every two years (even years), alternating with ICCV (odd years). ''Founded'': 1990 | ''Location'': European cities | ''Timing'': September/October (even years) ''Scale'': ~2,500–3,000 accepted papers; acceptance rate ~25–30%. ''Character'': European flavour — strong geometric computer vision, 3D reconstruction, robotics perception, and mathematical foundations alongside deep learning. ''Notable contributions'' * Many seminal 3D reconstruction and SLAM papers * Deformable Convolutional Networks — ECCV 2016 (honorable mention) * Strong generative model papers, vision transformers (ViT variants) ''ECCV vs CVPR vs ICCV'' All three are top venues; researchers often target based on submission deadlines. ECCV's European hosting often draws strong geometric/classical CV alongside DL. ''Workshops and tutorials'': strong tradition of specialised workshops on autonomous driving, medical imaging, and video understanding. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''AAAI — Association for the Advancement of Artificial Intelligence'' One of the oldest AI conferences; broader scope than ML-focused venues, covering symbolic AI, reasoning, planning, and knowledge representation alongside ML. ''Founded'': 1980 | ''Timing'': February ''Scale'': ~3,000 accepted papers; ~8,000 attendees. ''Scope distinction'' While NeurIPS/ICML/ICLR focus on statistical/neural ML, AAAI covers: * Knowledge representation and reasoning (KR) * Automated planning and scheduling * Multi-agent systems * Constraint satisfaction * AI ethics and fairness (strong track) * Robotics * NLP and vision (overlapping with other venues) ''Notable tradition'' AAAI has run the AAAI Classic Paper Award — recognising papers from 25 years ago that had lasting impact. Good source for foundational AI reading. ''AAAI Press'' Publishes AI Magazine, AI Ethics journal, and several AI books — important popular science and policy-facing publications. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''AISTATS — Artificial Intelligence and Statistics'' Conference at the intersection of ML and statistics; more theory-oriented and smaller than NeurIPS/ICML. ''Founded'': 1985 | ''Timing'': April/May | ''Format'': ~500 papers; ~800 attendees ''Character'': rigorous probabilistic and statistical ML. Strong Bayesian, causal inference, and optimisation papers. Values mathematical rigour and statistical foundations. ''Distinguishing features'' * Smaller and more intimate than the "big three" * Higher bar for theoretical novelty * Strong probabilistic ML: Gaussian processes, variational inference, MCMC, causal ML * Proceedings published in PMLR (Journal of Machine Learning Research open-access series) ''PMLR (Proceedings of Machine Learning Research)'' The open-access publication series hosting proceedings from AISTATS, ICML, COLT, UAI, and many workshops. Free to read; CC BY licence. ''Typical AISTATS papers'' Convergence guarantees for optimisation algorithms, Bayesian nonparametric methods, finite-sample statistical bounds, causal discovery algorithms, scalable approximate inference. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''JMLR — Journal of Machine Learning Research'' The leading open-access journal in machine learning. Founded 2000 by researchers dissatisfied with expensive closed-access alternatives. ''Key facts'' * Completely free to read (CC BY) and free to publish (no author fees) * Peer-reviewed; rigorous but author-friendly process * Unlimited page length — detailed proofs and experiments expected * Impact factor equivalent to top conference proceedings ''JMLR Papers Track at ICML'' Authors can present their JMLR papers at ICML — bridging journal and conference cultures. Journal versions typically contain more theory and complete experiments than conference papers. ''PMLR (Proceedings of Machine Learning Research)'' JMLR also runs PMLR, publishing conference proceedings for ICML, AISTATS, COLT, UAI, and many workshops — free and open access. ''Notable JMLR papers'' * Scikit-learn paper (Pedregosa et al. 2011) — most cited ML paper ever * Random Forests (Breiman 2001 — technically Machine Learning journal but same spirit) * LIBLINEAR, LibSVM implementations * Many foundational theory papers ''Philosophy'': high-quality, freely accessible science is a public good. JMLR's success influenced open access norms throughout ML. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ## Diffusion Models Diffusion models are a class of generative models that learn to reverse a gradual noising process. They have become the dominant approach for high-quality image, audio, and video generation, surpassing [[GAN]]s in quality and stability. ''The forward process'' Gradually add Gaussian noise to a data sample over $T$ steps until it becomes pure noise: $$q(\mathbf{x}_t \mid \mathbf{x}_{t-1}) = \mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}_t;\, \sqrt{1-\beta_t}\mathbf{x}_{t-1},\, \beta_t \mathbf{I})$$ After $T$ steps ($T \approx 1000$), $\mathbf{x}_T \approx \mathcal{N}(\mathbf{0}, \mathbf{I})$. ''The reverse process'' Train a neural network $\epsilon_\theta(\mathbf{x}_t, t)$ to predict the noise added at each step. To generate: start from pure noise, repeatedly apply the denoising network. ''Training objective'' Minimise: $$\mathcal{L} = \mathbb{E}_{t, \mathbf{x}_0, \epsilon}\!\left[\|\epsilon - \epsilon_\theta(\mathbf{x}_t, t)\|^2\right]$$ Simply predict the noise — remarkably, this trains the model to learn the score function of the data distribution. ''Why diffusion beat GANs'' [[GAN]]s suffer from mode collapse and training instability. Diffusion models: - Train stably with a simple regression loss - Cover all modes of the data distribution - Achieve better sample quality and diversity ''Applications'' DALL-E 2, Stable Diffusion, Midjourney — text-to-image Sora — video generation Protein structure prediction (via diffusion over 3D coordinates) --- [[Deep Learning]] ← back | [[GAN]] | [[Gaussian Distribution]] <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Scaled Dot-Product Attention'' The core operation inside every Transformer: $$ \text{Attention}(Q,K,V) = \text{softmax}\!\left(\frac{QK^\top}{\sqrt{d_k}}\right)V $$ * $Q$ — Query matrix ($n \times d_k$): what am I looking for? * $K$ — Key matrix ($m \times d_k$): what do I contain? * $V$ — Value matrix ($m \times d_v$): what do I return? * $\sqrt{d_k}$ — scaling factor to prevent dot products from growing large and saturating softmax ''Intuition'' Each query token computes a compatibility score with every key. Softmax converts scores to weights. The output is a weighted sum of values — a ''soft lookup'' over the sequence. ''Complexity'' Time: $O(n^2 d)$ — quadratic in sequence length $n$. This is the bottleneck for long documents. ''Multi-Head Attention'' Run $h$ parallel attention heads with different projections, concatenate: $$ \text{MultiHead}(Q,K,V) = \text{Concat}(\text{head}_1,\ldots,\text{head}_h)W^O $$ Each head can specialise (syntax, co-reference, semantics). <<button "Quiz: Why divide by $\sqrt{d_k}$?">> <<replace "#sdpa-q">> ''✓ Large $d_k$ causes dot products to grow in magnitude, pushing softmax into saturation regions where gradients vanish. Dividing by $\sqrt{d_k}$ keeps variance constant.'' [^1] [^1]: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cory_Doctorow <<include "Header">> ''FTC and AI Regulation'' The US Federal Trade Commission (FTC) has authority over unfair or deceptive practices — increasingly applied to AI systems. ''FTC Actions on AI'' * Issued guidance that AI-generated testimonials and endorsements must be disclosed * Investigating AI companies for deceptive claims about capabilities * Examining whether AI enables price-fixing or collusion at scale * "Commercial surveillance" rulemaking — behavioural data used to train models ''Key Issues'' * ''Dark patterns'': AI-powered nudging and manipulation of consumer choice * ''Bias and discrimination'': disparate impact in lending, hiring, housing * ''Deceptive claims'': companies overstating accuracy or safety of AI products * ''Data privacy'': using consumer data to train models without consent ''Lina Khan (FTC Chair 2021–2025)'' Focused FTC attention on AI and tech concentration; warned about surveillance business model enabling AI. Co-authored academic work on algorithmic harms. ''Section 5 Authority'' The FTC's broad Section 5 authority over "unfair or deceptive acts" is flexible enough to address novel AI harms without new legislation — the agency's primary tool in the absence of comprehensive federal AI law. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Jonathan Frankle'' ML researcher; PhD from MIT (2023); now Chief Scientist at Databricks Mosaic AI. Known for the ''Lottery Ticket Hypothesis''. ''Lottery Ticket Hypothesis (2019)'' A dense, randomly-initialised neural network contains small subnetworks (''winning tickets'') that — when trained in isolation from the same initialisation — match the full network's accuracy. Formally: $f(x; \mathbf{m} \odot \theta_0)$ trained from $\theta_0$ achieves accuracy comparable to the full network $f(x; \theta)$, where $\mathbf{m}$ is a sparse mask. ''Implications'' * Suggests neural networks are massively over-parameterised at initialisation * The ''initialisation'' matters — the ticket must be trained from its original weights, not re-randomised * Motivated research into sparse training, pruning at initialisation, and neural network structure ''Scaling and Caveats'' At very large scale, the original strong lottery ticket hypothesis weakens — matching tickets are harder to find and may require iterative magnitude pruning with rewinding. Frankle refined the hypothesis with ''late resetting'' (reset to weights after a few steps, not step 0). ''At Databricks'' Leading LLM training infrastructure, mixture-of-experts research, and open-source foundation models (DBRX). <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Probing / Probe Identification'' A technique to understand what information is encoded in a neural network's internal representations by training a simple classifier (the ''probe'') on top of frozen activations. ''Method'' 1. Take a pre-trained model; freeze all weights 2. Extract activations $\mathbf{h}_\ell$ from layer $\ell$ for each input 3. Train a linear (or shallow) classifier on $\{(\mathbf{h}_\ell^{(i)}, y^{(i)})\}$ 4. High probe accuracy → layer $\ell$ encodes concept $y$ ''What Probes Reveal'' * Does BERT layer 6 encode syntactic structure (POS tags)? * Do early CNN layers encode edges? Do later layers encode semantics? * Does a language model represent numeric quantities linearly? ''Caution: Correlation vs Causation'' High probing accuracy shows the information is ''present'', not that the model ''uses'' it for its predictions. Causal interventions (activation patching) are needed to test usage. ''Linear Probing in Transfer Learning'' Freeze a pretrained backbone; train only a linear head. Probing accuracy benchmarks representation quality — commonly reported for vision models (CLIP, DINOv2, ResNet). <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''Target Identification in Drug Discovery'' The first stage of the drug discovery pipeline: identifying which biological molecule (usually a protein) to target with a drug. ''Process'' 1. Understand the disease mechanism — which proteins are involved? 2. Identify proteins that, when inhibited or activated, have therapeutic effect 3. Validate the target: genetic evidence (GWAS, CRISPR knockouts), expression data 4. Assess druggability: can a small molecule or antibody bind the target? ''ML in Target Identification'' * Protein–protein interaction networks: graph ML to prioritise targets * Causal gene expression models: predict effect of perturbing each gene * AlphaFold: predict protein 3D structure for previously unknown targets * CRISPR screen analysis: identify essential genes in disease cell lines ''From Target to Lead Compound'' Once a target is identified: virtual screening or de novo generation (ML: graph neural nets, diffusion models) to find molecules that bind, followed by ''lead optimisation'' and clinical trials. ''Challenges'' Many validated targets fail in clinical trials due to toxicity or lack of efficacy in humans — the "valley of death" between basic research and approved drugs. ML cannot yet reliably predict this. <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ''[ Staging / Delete ]'' This passage is a staging area. We train by minimising the ''Mean Squared Error'': $$\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{w}, b) = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^{n} \bigl(y_i - \hat{y}_i\bigr)^2$$ --- ''Question:'' MSE squares each residual. What effect does that have on outliers? <<button "They are penalised the same as small errors.">> <<replace "#lr-feedback">> <div class="feedback-wrong"> ✗ That would be ''Mean Absolute Error (MAE)''. Squaring makes large residuals hurt far more. </div> <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <<button "They are penalised disproportionately — a residual of 10 costs 100× more than one of 1.">> <<replace "#lr-feedback">> <div class="feedback-correct"> ✓ Exactly. Squaring amplifies large errors, making MSE sensitive to outliers — a feature or a bug depending on your problem. </div> <div class="next-steps"> ''Next:'' [[Gradient Descent Quiz]] — how we minimise this loss &nbsp;|&nbsp; [[Linear Regression]] — theory &nbsp;|&nbsp; [[Home]] ← map </div> <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <<button "Only errors above a threshold are counted.">> <<replace "#lr-feedback">> <div class="feedback-wrong"> ✗ That describes a Huber loss. Standard MSE squares every residual with no threshold. </div> <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="lr-feedback"></span> --- [[Linear Regression]] | [[Home]] ← back <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Gradient Descent We update every parameter by stepping in the direction of ''steepest descent'': $$\theta_{t+1} = \theta_t - \alpha \,\nabla_{\!\theta}\,\mathcal{L}(\theta_t)$$ - $\alpha$ — the ''learning rate'' (step size) - $\nabla_{\!\theta}\,\mathcal{L}$ — gradient: direction of steepest ascent in loss ''Intuition:'' You are on a foggy hillside. You feel the slope under your feet and take one step downhill. Repeat until you reach the valley. --- ''Question:'' If the learning rate $\alpha$ is set too large, what typically happens? <<button "Training converges faster and more reliably.">> <<replace "#gd-feedback">> <div class="feedback-wrong"> ✗ A very large $\alpha$ often causes the loss to ''increase'' — each step overshoots the minimum. </div> <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <<button "The optimizer overshoots the minimum and the loss oscillates or diverges.">> <<replace "#gd-feedback">> <div class="feedback-correct"> ✓ Correct. With $\alpha$ too large, the update $-\alpha\nabla\mathcal{L}$ jumps past the valley. The loss bounces or explodes. </div> <div class="next-steps"> ''Next:'' [[Neural Networks Quiz]] — gradient descent at scale &nbsp;|&nbsp; [[Gradient Descent]] — theory &nbsp;|&nbsp; [[Home]] ← map </div> <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <<button "Parameters stop updating because the gradient becomes zero.">> <<replace "#gd-feedback">> <div class="feedback-wrong"> ✗ Weights still update — the problem is that each update is far too large, not that updates stop. </div> <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="gd-feedback"></span> --- [[Gradient Descent]] | [[Home]] ← back <<include "Footer">><<include "Header">> ## Neural Networks A network stacks layers of parameterised transformations: $$\mathbf{h}^{(l)} = \sigma\!\Bigl(\mathbf{W}^{(l)}\,\mathbf{h}^{(l-1)} + \mathbf{b}^{(l)}\Bigr)$$ Each layer applies a ''weight matrix'' $\mathbf{W}^{(l)}$, adds a ''bias'' $\mathbf{b}^{(l)}$, then passes through a ''nonlinear activation'' $\sigma$. $$\text{ReLU:}\; f(x) = \max(0,x) \qquad \text{Sigmoid:}\; f(x) = \frac{1}{1+e^{-x}}$$ --- ''Question:'' Why are nonlinear activations necessary? <<button "They make each forward pass faster to compute.">> <<replace "#nn-feedback">> <div class="feedback-wrong"> ✗ Activations add computation. Their purpose is about what the network can ''represent'', not speed. </div> <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <<button "Without them, stacked layers collapse to a single linear transformation.">> <<replace "#nn-feedback">> <div class="feedback-correct"> ✓ Exactly. $\mathbf{W}_2(\mathbf{W}_1\mathbf{x}+\mathbf{b}_1)+\mathbf{b}_2$ is still one affine map. Nonlinearity lets deep networks learn complex functions. </div> <div class="next-steps"> ''Next:'' [[Gradient Descent Quiz]] — how networks are trained &nbsp;|&nbsp; [[Deep Learning]] — theory &nbsp;|&nbsp; [[Home]] ← map </div> <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <<button "They normalise outputs to the range [0, 1] to keep values stable.">> <<replace "#nn-feedback">> <div class="feedback-wrong"> ✗ Some activations bound outputs, but that is a side effect — the core reason is representational power. </div> <</replace>> <<script>>typesetMath();<</script>> <</button>> <span id="nn-feedback"></span> --- [[Deep Learning]] | [[Home]] ← back <<include "Footer">> <<include "Header">> ! Neural Networks <img src="assets/neural_network.svg" alt="Neural network — feedforward architecture (left) and single neuron detail (right)" style="width:100%;max-width:620px;display:block;margin:16px auto 24px auto;"> A neural network is a function built by composing many simple operations. Each operation — a neuron — takes a weighted sum of its inputs, adds a bias, and passes the result through a nonlinear function. Stack enough of these in layers and you get a machine capable of approximating virtually any function. --- !! One Neuron A single neuron computes: $$\text{output} = \sigma(\mathbf{w} \cdot \mathbf{x} + b)$$ $\mathbf{w}$ is a weight vector, $\mathbf{x}$ is the input, $b$ is a bias, and $\sigma$ is an ''activation function''. The weights and bias are learned; the activation function is chosen by the designer. Without $\sigma$, a neuron is just linear regression. The nonlinearity is what gives neural networks their expressive power — stacking linear functions gives you another linear function, no matter how deep you go. --- !! Layers Neurons are organised into layers. In a ''feedforward'' network: * ''Input layer'' — the raw features. No computation, just the data. * ''Hidden layers'' — each neuron takes all outputs from the previous layer. These learn internal representations. * ''Output layer'' — produces the prediction. The activation here depends on the task: softmax for classification, linear for regression. Information flows in one direction: input → hidden layers → output. Each layer transforms the representation, progressively extracting more abstract features. --- !! Activation Functions The choice of $\sigma$ has large practical consequences. ''ReLU'' ($\max(0, x)$) is the standard for hidden layers. It's fast to compute, doesn't saturate for positive inputs, and its derivative is simply 0 or 1 — which keeps gradients alive during backprop. ''Sigmoid'' ($1/(1+e^{-x})$) squashes output to $(0,1)$, useful for binary output layers. Saturates at extremes — derivative near zero — so it causes [[vanishing gradient]] problems in deep hidden layers. ''Softmax'' normalises a vector to a probability distribution over classes. Standard for multiclass output layers. ''GELU'' and ''SiLU'' are smooth approximations to ReLU, commonly used in transformers and modern architectures. --- !! Universal Approximation A feedforward network with a single hidden layer of sufficient width can approximate any continuous function on a compact domain to arbitrary accuracy — this is the universal approximation theorem. But "sufficient width" can be exponentially large. Depth is far more practical: deep networks can represent complex functions with exponentially fewer parameters than shallow ones. This is the practical case for depth: not that it's necessary, but that it's efficient. --- !! What the Network Learns The hidden layers learn a hierarchy of representations. In a vision network: * Early layers detect edges and textures * Middle layers detect parts (eyes, wheels) * Late layers detect objects and scenes This hierarchy emerges from training — it's not explicitly programmed. The network discovers whatever intermediate representations make the task easiest to solve. --- !! Technical Details ''Parameter count:'' a fully connected layer from $n$ to $m$ neurons has $nm + m$ parameters (weights + biases). A network with layers $[784, 256, 128, 10]$ has $784\times256 + 256\times128 + 128\times10 = 234{,}256$ parameters. ''Initialisation:'' weights must be initialised carefully — all zeros gives symmetric gradients that never break; too large causes exploding activations. He initialisation ($\mathcal{N}(0, 2/n)$) is standard for ReLU networks; Glorot/Xavier ($\mathcal{N}(0, 2/(n_{in}+n_{out}))$) for tanh/sigmoid. ''Forward pass:'' $\mathbf{h}^{(l)} = \sigma(W^{(l)} \mathbf{h}^{(l-1)} + \mathbf{b}^{(l)})$ for each layer $l$. ''Training:'' weights are updated by [[Stocastic Gradient Descent|gradient descent]] using gradients computed by [[Backpropogation and Chain Rule|backpropagation]]. --- !! Quiz ''1. Why does a neural network need nonlinear activation functions?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#nn-q1">>'✓ Without nonlinearity, any stack of linear layers collapses to a single linear transformation: $W_2(W_1 x) = (W_2 W_1)x$. No matter the depth, the network can only represent linear functions. Nonlinear activations break this — each layer can now learn nonlinear mappings, and the composition of nonlinear functions is expressive enough to approximate any continuous function.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="nn-q1"></span> ''2. What does a hidden layer actually learn?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#nn-q2">>'✓ Hidden layers learn intermediate representations — transformations of the input that make the output easier to predict. In practice these are not hand-designed: the network discovers whatever internal features minimise the loss. In vision networks, early layers learn edges, middle layers learn parts, late layers learn objects. The hierarchy of abstraction emerges from training.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="nn-q2"></span> ''3. Why is ReLU preferred over sigmoid for hidden layers?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#nn-q3">>'✓ Sigmoid saturates at both extremes — its derivative approaches 0 for large positive or negative inputs. In deep networks this causes vanishing gradients: the gradient signal multiplied across many sigmoid derivatives shrinks exponentially. ReLU has derivative exactly 1 for positive inputs, so gradients flow unchanged through those neurons. It is also cheaper to compute. The main downside is "dead neurons" — ReLU outputs permanently 0 for neurons with large negative weights.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="nn-q3"></span> ''4. What does the universal approximation theorem say, and what does it not say?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#nn-q4">>'✓ It says a single hidden layer with enough neurons can approximate any continuous function arbitrarily well. It does NOT say how many neurons are needed (can be exponentially many), how to find the right weights (training is not guaranteed to converge), or that shallow networks are practical. In practice, depth is far more parameter-efficient than width — deep networks express complex functions with far fewer total parameters.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="nn-q4"></span> ''5. How do you choose the right weight initialisation?'' <<button "Show Answer">><<replace "#nn-q5">>'✓ All-zero initialisation fails — every neuron computes the same gradient and they never differentiate. Too-large initialisation causes exploding activations. He initialisation ($\mathcal{N}(0, 2/n_{in})$) is designed for ReLU: it keeps activation variance stable across layers. Glorot/Xavier ($\mathcal{N}(0, 2/(n_{in}+n_{out}))$) is designed for symmetric activations (tanh, sigmoid). In practice, frameworks handle this automatically.'<</replace>><<script>>typesetMath();<</script>><</button>> <span id="nn-q5"></span> <<include "Footer">>
